Liability of Father, Mother, and Husband for Hitting for Discipline
Rulings on liability for hitting children and wives for discipline

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the liability rules for father, mother, and husband when hitting for discipline, and apply the principle that hitting for one's own benefit incurs liability.

Core Idea
A father hitting for education is not liable; a husband hitting for discipline is liable by consensus; a mother is liable in Abu Hanifa's view.

This lesson explores the nuanced rulings on liability when family members discipline children or wives. The Hanafi school differentiates based on the relationship and the purpose of the hitting. The key factor is whether the hitting is for the benefit of the person being hit or for the benefit of the one hitting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the hitter
Father, mother, or husband.
Each has different authority.
2
Identify the purpose
Education vs discipline, or nushuz.
Purpose determines liability.
3
Father's ruling
If for education, like teacher: no liability. For discipline, two narrations from Muhammad.
Father has inherent authority for education.
4
Mother's ruling
Abu Hanifa: liable because no authority. Sahibayn: two views.
Lack of authority is key.
5
Husband's ruling
Liable by consensus for discipline hitting, even for nushuz or prayer.
Consensus indicates strong ruling.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخلاف الوصي إذا ضرب الصغير تأديباً. وفي الكبرى: وإن كان ضربه المعلم في الموضع المعتاد فمات لا يضمن هو ولا الأب ولا الوصي في قولهم جميعاً، وكذا المؤدب الذي يعلمه الكتابة إذا ضربه بإذن والده لا ضمان عليه وعليه الكفارة في قولهما. وهذا إذا كان ضربه المعلم في موضع معتاد وفي رواية مجهولة لا كفارة عليهما والفتوى على الأول. والزوج إذا ضرب زوجته حيث تضرب للتأديب مثل ما تضرب حال نشوزها يضمن بالإجماع، والأب إذا سلم الصغير إلى معلم يعلمه القرآن أو علماً آخر فضربه المعلم للتعليم فلا ضمان على المعلم ولا على الأب والوصي. وفي المنتقى عن أبي حنيفة وأبي يوسف أن عليه الكفارة وإن ضربه حيث لا يضرب أو فوق ضرب التعليم فالمعلم ضامن. قال هشام في نوادره: قلت لمحمد: إن لم يكن الأب قال له في أمر الضرب شيئاً قال: يضمن المعلم. وفي رواية في بعض النسخ أن ضرب الصغير إنما يضمن على قول أبي حنيفة إذا كان للتأديب، أما إذا ضربه لتعليم القرآن لا يضمن كالمعلم فلا فرق بين ضرب المعلم بإذن الأب وبين ضرب الأب إذا كان للتعليم. وذكر شمس الأئمة الحلواني في شرح كتاب الإجارات أن في ضرب الأب ابنه وفي ضرب الزوج زوجته روايتين عن محمد: في رواية يضمن، وفي رواية لا يضمن. أما الوالدة إذا ضربت ولدها الصغير للتأديب فلا شك أنها تضمن على قول أبي حنيفة، وقد اختلف المشايخ فيه على قولهما: قال بعضهم لا تضمن، وقال بعضهم هي ضامنة لأن الضرب تصرف في النفس وليس لها ولاية التصرف في النفس أصلاً. وفي كتاب العلل: للزوج أن يضرب امرأته على ترك الصلاة وللأب أن يضرب ابنه على ترك الصلاة. وذكر مسألة المعلم إذا ضرب الصغير بإذن الأب على الاتفاق قال نحو ما ذكرنا. قال محمد: ثمة وهذا عندنا. وفي العيون: إذا قال لرجلين اضربا مملوكي هذا مائة سوط فليس لأحدهما أن يضربه المائة كلها، فإن ضربه أحدهما تسعة وتسعين وضربه الآخر سوطاً واحداً ففي القياس يضمن ضارب الأكثر، وفي رواية لا يضمن وهو نظير ما لو قال لامرأتيه: إن أكلتما هذا الخبر فأنتما طالقتان فأكلتاه، وإن أكلت إحداهما عامته والأخرى بقيته لا تطلق استحساناً. المحترف إذا ضرب التلميذ فمات إن كان ضربه بأمر أبيه أو وصيه لا يضمن إذا كان في الموضع المعتاد، وإن ضرب امرأته على المضجع أو في أدب فماتت يضمن إجماعاً وعليه الكفارة؛ هما فرقا بينها وبين الأب فإن ضرب الأب لمنفعة الابن وضرب المرأة لمنفعة الزوج. وفي السراجية: رجل ضرب رجلاً سياطاً فجرحه فبرأ منه فعليه أرش الضرب إن بقي أثر الضرب، وإن لم يبق لا يجب عليه شيء سوى التغزير. وقال أبو يوسف: تجب حكومة عدل. وقال محمد: أجرة الطبيب وثمن الأدوية. وفي الجامع الصغير: الخامسة وهذا إذا جرح ابتداء، فأما إذا لم يجرح في الابتداء لا يجب بالاتفاق.
The dispute about the guardian if he hits the child for discipline. And in al-Kubra: If the teacher hits in the usual place and the child dies, neither he nor the father nor the guardian is liable in all their views. Similarly, the tutor who teaches writing, if he hits with the father's permission, there is no liability on him, and expiation is due in their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) view. This is if the teacher hit in a usual place, and in an unknown narration, no expiation is due on them, and the fatwa is on the first. And the husband, if he hits his wife where she is hit for discipline like when she is rebellious, he is liable by consensus. And the father, if he hands over the child to a teacher who teaches him Quran or another knowledge, and the teacher hits for education, there is no liability on the teacher nor on the father and guardian. And in al-Muntaqa from Abu Hanifa and Abu Yusuf that expiation is due on him, and if he hits where he should not or beyond the hitting for education, the teacher is liable. Hisham said in his Nawadir: I said to Muhammad: If the father did not say anything to him regarding the matter of hitting, he said: The teacher is liable. And in a narration in some copies, that hitting the child is only liable in Abu Hanifa's view if it is for discipline; as for if he hits for teaching Quran, he is not liable like the teacher, so there is no difference between the teacher hitting with the father's permission and the father hitting if for education. And Shams al-A'imma al-Halwani mentioned in Sharh Kitab al-Ijarat that regarding the father hitting his son and the husband hitting his wife, there are two narrations from Muhammad: in one he is liable, in one he is not. As for the mother, if she hits her young child for discipline, there is no doubt that she is liable in Abu Hanifa's view, and the mashayikh differed on his view: some said she is not liable, some said she is liable because hitting is an act upon the person and she has no authority over the person originally. And in Kitab al-'Ilal: The husband may hit his wife for abandoning prayer, and the father may hit his son for abandoning prayer. And the case of the teacher hitting the child with the father's permission by agreement was mentioned, similar to what we mentioned. Muhammad said: There, and this is our view. And in al-'Uyun: If he said to two men, 'Hit this slave of mine one hundred lashes,' neither may hit the hundred all by himself. If one hits ninety-nine and the other hits one lash, in qiyas the one who hit the majority is liable, and in one narration he is not liable, and this is analogous to if he said to his two wives, 'If you both eat this bread, you are divorced,' and they both ate it, and if one ate most and the other the remainder, they are not divorced by istihsan. The professional if he hits the student and he dies: if he hit by order of his father or guardian, he is not liable if it was in the usual place. If he hit his wife on the bed or in discipline and she died, he is liable by consensus and expiation is due; they differentiated between her and the father, for the father's hitting is for the benefit of the son, and the hitting of the woman is for the benefit of the husband. And in al-Sirajiyya: A man hit a man with whips and wounded him, and he recovered from it, so upon him is the penalty of the hitting if the effect of the hitting remains, and if it does not remain, nothing is obligatory upon him except tazir. Abu Yusuf said: A fair compensation is obligatory. Muhammad said: The doctor's fee and the cost of medicine. And in al-Jami' al-Saghir: The fifth, and this is if he wounded initially; as for if he did not wound initially, nothing is obligatory by agreement.
Source pages p. 106

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of liability for hitting children and wives in various contexts. The author begins with the dispute regarding the guardian (wasī) who hits a child for discipline. According to all Hanafi imams, if the teacher (or father/guardian) hits in a usual place and the child dies, there is no liability (ḍamān) for the teacher, father, or guardian. However, expiation (kaffāra) is required according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) when the hitting is with permission and in the usual place. If the hitting is beyond what is usual or for an improper purpose, the teacher is liable. The text then discusses the husband hitting his wife for discipline (in case of nushūz) — here there is consensus that the husband is liable if she dies, because unlike the father, the husband hits for his own benefit. The mother is treated differently: according to Abu Hanifa, she is liable because she has no authority over the person of the child. The text also covers the case of ordering two persons to whip a slave: one cannot whip the entire hundred alone; if one whips 99 and the other 1, there is a disagreement on liability. The professional (muḥtarif) hitting a student is treated like the husband: liable if death occurs, even if with permission. Finally, the passage gives rulings on flogging wounds: if the wound heals, the penalty is for the beating if a mark remains; otherwise only tazir is due. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad give different views on compensation.

Key Terms
الوصي (al-waṣī): The guardian of a minor appointed by the father or judge, with authority to discipline the child.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Financial liability or compensation for harm caused, including blood-money or damages.
الكفارة (al-kaffārah): An expiation required for accidental killing or certain violations, such as freeing a slave, fasting, or feeding the poor.
التأديب (al-ta'dīb): Discipline or correction, typically of a child or wife, within lawful bounds.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which hitter is liable by consensus?
A) Father hitting for education.
B) Mother hitting for discipline.
C) Husband hitting for nushuz.
D) Teacher hitting with permission.

Key Takeaway
The purpose of the hitting determines liability: hitting for the benefit of the child may protect from liability, but hitting for one's own self-interest (husband) always incurs liability.

Special Cases: Professional, Slave Whipping, and Wound Compensation
Rulings on liability for hitting children and wives for discipline

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rulings on liability for a professional hitting a student, for ordering multiple persons to whip a slave, and for compensation when wounds heal.

Core Idea
A professional hitting a student with permission is like a teacher; ordering multiple persons to whip a slave leads to istihsan rulings; wound compensation depends on whether a mark remains.

This lesson covers additional scenarios that refine the general principles of liability for hitting. The passage discusses the professional (muhtarif) who educates students, the case of directing two persons to whip a slave, and the rules for compensating wounds that heal. These illustrate the Hanafi method of qiyas and istihsan.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
Professional hitting student, ordering two persons to whip slave, or wound healing.
Each has its own rules.
2
Professional hitting student
If with permission and in usual place, no liability like teacher.
Same conditions as teacher.
3
Ordering two persons to whip slave
Neither can hit the full hundred alone. If one hits 99, other hits 1: qiyas says the one hitting most is liable; istihsan says no liability.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here.
4
Wound compensation
If wound heals with mark, penalty for beating (arsh al-darb) is due. If no mark, only tazir.
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad differ on additional compensation.
5
Apply the principle
Liability is triggered by excessive or unauthorized hitting; compensation varies by jurist.
These rulings show the nuance in Hanafi law.

Source Text

Commentary
This passage is a comprehensive treatment of liability for hitting children and wives in various contexts. The author begins with the dispute regarding the guardian (wasī) who hits a child for discipline. According to all Hanafi imams, if the teacher (or father/guardian) hits in a usual place and the child dies, there is no liability (ḍamān) for the teacher, father, or guardian. However, expiation (kaffāra) is required according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) when the hitting is with permission and in the usual place. If the hitting is beyond what is usual or for an improper purpose, the teacher is liable. The text then discusses the husband hitting his wife for discipline (in case of nushūz) — here there is consensus that the husband is liable if she dies, because unlike the father, the husband hits for his own benefit. The mother is treated differently: according to Abu Hanifa, she is liable because she has no authority over the person of the child. The text also covers the case of ordering two persons to whip a slave: one cannot whip the entire hundred alone; if one whips 99 and the other 1, there is a disagreement on liability. The professional (muḥtarif) hitting a student is treated like the husband: liable if death occurs, even if with permission. Finally, the passage gives rulings on flogging wounds: if the wound heals, the penalty is for the beating if a mark remains; otherwise only tazir is due. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad give different views on compensation.

Key Terms
المحترف (al-muḥtarif): A professional teacher or trainer who educates students, sometimes with physical correction.
القياس (al-qiyās): Analogical reasoning in Islamic jurisprudence, used to derive rulings for new cases.
الاستحسان (al-istiḥsān): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas for equity or public interest.
أرش الضرب (arsh al-ḍarb): The monetary penalty or compensation due for a beating that leaves a scar or lasting effect.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Hanifa, what compensation is due for a healed wound that leaves no mark?
A) Arsh al-darb (penalty for beating).
B) Nothing except tazir.
C) Fair compensation (hukuma).
D) Doctor fees and medicine.

Key Takeaway
Special cases demonstrate the Hanafi use of istihsan and the importance of initial conditions (like wounding) in determining liability.

Deathbed Confession of Paternity and the Right to Qisas
A deathbed (or posthumous) acknowledgment of paternity and its effect on the right to qisas.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on whether a brother's acknowledgment of paternity grants the acknowledged son the right to qisas, and identify the intra-Hanafi disagreement on this issue.

Core Idea
A brother's acknowledgment establishes a son's right to qisas, but an earlier Hanafi authority disagrees.

This passage from al-Muntaqa presents a specific case: a man is murdered, his brother acknowledges a son of the victim, and that son claims qisas as an adult. The ruling is that the son is entitled to qisas because his lineage is established by the uncle's acknowledgment. However, the commentator Abu al-Fadl notes that this answer contradicts what is recorded in al-Asl, the foundational text of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, indicating a divergence within the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario
A man is killed intentionally. He has two sons but no known brother. His brother (paternal uncle) acknowledges a son of the victim.
The victim's brother is the one making the acknowledgment.
2
Determine the legal claim
The acknowledged son, being an adult, claims qisas (retaliation) against the murderer.
Only adult heirs can claim qisas directly.
3
Extract the ruling
The text explicitly states: 'فإن للمقر به القود' — the son has the right to qisas. This is the ruling from al-Muntaqa.
The acknowledgment is treated as sufficient proof of lineage for qisas purposes.
4
Note the disagreement
Abu al-Fadl comments that this answer is contrary to what is recorded in al-Asl. This signals an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether a brother's acknowledgment alone is sufficient.
Al-Asl is one of the six authoritative books of Zahir al-Riwayah by Imam Muhammad.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى : رجل قتل عمداً وله ابني لا أخ معروف فأقر أخوه بابن المقتول وادعى ذلك الابن وهو كبير فإن للمقر به القود. وقال أبو الفضل : هذا الجواب خلاف ما في الأصل.
And in al-Muntaqa: A man was killed intentionally, and he had two sons, no known brother, so his brother acknowledged the son of the killed one, and that son claimed (qisas) while he was an adult, then the one acknowledged in his favor has the right to qisas. Abu al-Fadl said: This answer is contrary to what is in al-Asl.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This is a specific ruling from al-Muntaqa. The scenario: a man is murdered intentionally. The victim has two sons (or perhaps two children) and no known brother. The victim's brother (paternal uncle) then acknowledges a son of the victim (i.e., he affirms that the victim had a son). That son, being an adult, claims qisas (retaliation) against the murderer. The ruling: the son is entitled to qisas because his lineage is established by the uncle's acknowledgment. Abu al-Fadl (a commentator) notes that this ruling contradicts what is recorded in al-Asl (the foundational text by Muhammad al-Shaybani). This indicates an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf on whether a brother's acknowledgment is sufficient to establish paternity for purposes of qisas.

Key Terms
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Acknowledgment or confession of a fact, here establishing lineage.
القود (al-qawad): Qisas, retaliation for intentional murder.
المنتقى (al-Muntaqā): A well-known Hanafi legal work by Imam Abu al-Fadl al-Hanafi, containing selected rulings.
الأصل (al-Asl): The foundational work of Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani, one of the six books of Zahir al-Riwayah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does Abu al-Fadl say about the ruling from al-Muntaqa?
A) It is correct and should be followed.
B) It contradicts what is recorded in al-Asl.
C) It applies only if the son is wealthy.
D) It requires the uncle to be a virgin.

Key Takeaway
A brother's acknowledgment of a murdered man's son grants the son the right to qisas according to al-Muntaqa, but this ruling is contested by an earlier Hanafi source.

Liability of a False Claimant who Frees a Slave
Case of a man who claims a slave, provides evidence, frees him, but the slave is actually free.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling when a man falsely claims a slave, frees him, and the slave is killed, and distinguish between cases with and without an heir.

Core Idea
A false claimant who frees a slave is liable for the slave's value if the slave dies without an heir.

This passage from Nawadir Hisham presents a unique scenario where a man's false claim and subsequent freeing lead to financial liability. It illustrates the Hanafi approach to ensuring compensation for harm caused by false testimony and actions based on it.

Guided Path
1
Identify the facts of the case
A man claims another as his slave, produces witnesses who testify, and frees him. Later, the person is discovered to have been free all along.
The key is that the freeing was based on false evidence.
2
Determine whether the freed person has an heir
The ruling bifurcates based on whether the freed person (the victim, if killed) has an heir. If he has an heir, the heir gets qisas for intentional killing or diyah for accidental. If no heir, the claimant who freed him gets the slave's value.
3
Apply the appropriate outcome
If the victim has an heir: heir takes qisas/diyah. If no heir: the former 'master' (the claimant) receives the slave's value in both intentional and accidental cases.
Note that the claimant receives value even in accidental killing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نوادر هشام عن أبي يوسف: رجل ادعى عبده وأقام البينة وشهد الشهود أنه كان عبده فأعتقه وهو حر اليوم، فإن كان له وارث قضى لوارثه بالقصاص في العمد وبالدية في الخطأ، وإن لم يكن له وارث فلمولاه قيمته في العمد الخطأ
And in Nawadir Hisham from Abu Yusuf: A man claimed his slave and produced evidence, and the witnesses testified that he was his slave, so he freed him, and he is free today. If he has an heir, he will be judged for his heir with qisas in intentional (killing) and with diyah in accidental. If he has no heir, then his master (gets) his value in intentional and accidental.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This is a narration from Abu Yusuf. The scenario: a man claims a person as his slave and provides witnesses who testify that the person was indeed his slave. Based on this, the man frees the slave. Later it turns out that the person was actually free all along (i.e., he was not a slave). If that person is killed (by someone), the ruling depends: if the freed person has an heir, the heir is entitled to qisas (if intentional killing) or diyah (if accidental). If there is no heir, then the person who claimed and freed him (the former 'master') gets the value of the slave (since the man's claim made him liable) in both intentional and accidental cases.

Key Terms
البينة (al-bayyinah): Evidence, here specifically witness testimony.
القصاص (al-qiṣāṣ): Retaliation, the right to take life for intentional murder.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money, financial compensation paid to the victim's heirs in cases of accidental killing or when qisas is not applicable.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The value of the slave
B) Qisas (retaliation) against the killer
C) Diyah (blood money)
D) Nothing; the claimant gets everything

Key Takeaway
A false claimant who frees a slave becomes liable for the slave's value if the slave dies without an heir; otherwise, the heir gets qisas or diyah.

Ruling on Qisas for Cutting a Child's Hand in Custody
Ruling on an injury case with slave status acknowledgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on when qisas is due for intentionally cutting the hand of a child in custody, given an acknowledgment of slavery before the child's death.

Core Idea
Qisas for injury to a child in custody is established by the cutter's acknowledgment of slave status affirmed by the custodian.

This lesson examines a specific case from the Nawadir of Ibn Sama'a, where Abu Yusuf rules on qisas (retaliation) for intentionally cutting a child's hand. The ruling turns on a distinctive acknowledgment: the cutter claims the child is the custodian's slave, and the custodian confirms it. If this exchange occurs before the child dies, the cutter becomes liable for qisas. Understanding this ruling clarifies how Hanafi law handles intentional injury when the victim's status is uncertain and how acknowledgment can fix liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their roles
There are three parties: the cutter (offender), the child (victim), and the custodian (in whose hand the child is). The child is initially in the custodian's possession, apparently as a slave.
2
Note the act and its nature
The cutter intentionally (ʿamdan) cuts the child's hand. This is a deliberate injury that normally triggers qisas if the victim is a free person or a slave with a master.
Intentional cutting of a limb is a form of jināyah ʿalā mā dūn al-nafs (injury short of death).
3
Examine the acknowledgment
After the cutting, the cutter says: 'He is your slave.' The custodian responds: 'I affirm him as such.' This mutual acknowledgment establishes the child's legal status as a slave owned by the custodian at the time of the injury.
The acknowledgment (iqrār) is crucial because it clarifies ownership, which affects who holds the right to qisas.
4
Observe the timing condition
The text specifies that this acknowledgment occurs before the victim (child) dies. The condition is: 'if he said this statement before the death of the victim' (qabla mawt al-majnī ʿalayh).
Timing matters because if the child dies first, the case becomes one of homicide, not mere injury.
5
State the resulting legal consequence
Given the acknowledgment before death, qisas (retaliation) is due upon the cutter. The right to claim qisas belongs to the master (custodian) because the child is his slave.
Qisas for injury to a slave goes to the master, not to the slave's relatives.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي نوادر ابن سماعة قال: سمعت أبا يوسف يقول في رجل في يده صبي صغير فقطع الرجل يد الصبي عمداً ثم قال القاطع هو عبدك وقال الذي في يده هو أصدقه على ذلك، ولو قال هذه المقالة قبل موت المجني عليه فعلى الجاني القود.
And in Nawadir Ibn Sama'a: I heard Abu Yusuf say regarding a man who had a young child in his custody, and the man cut the child's hand intentionally, then the cutter said, 'He is your slave,' and the one in whose hand (the child was) said, 'I affirm him as such.' If he said this statement before the death of the victim, then qisas is due upon the offender.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This ruling from Abu Yusuf deals with a situation where a man has a young child in his custody (apparently as a slave). Another person cuts the child's hand intentionally. After the cutting, the cutter claims that the child is the slave of the custodian, and the custodian affirms that. If this acknowledgment occurs before the child dies from the injury, then the cutter is liable for qisas (retaliation) for the cutting of the child's hand. The rationale seems to be that the child's status as a slave of the custodian means the right to qisas belongs to the master (custodian). By acknowledging and affirming, the liability for qisas is established on the cutter.

Key Terms
المجني عليه (al-majnī 'alayh): The victim, here the child whose hand was cut.
القود (al-qawad (or al-qisās)): Retaliation or qisas, specifically for injury to a limb.
الإقرار (al-iqrār): Acknowledgment or admission, here the cutter's statement and the custodian's affirmation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Immediately after cutting the hand.
B) After the child dies.
C) Before the child dies, if the acknowledgment is made.
D) Only if the child's parents file a complaint.

Key Takeaway
Qisas for intentional cutting of a child's hand is due when the cutter acknowledges the child is the custodian's slave and the custodian affirms, provided this occurs before the child dies.

Dying Declarations and Son's Evidence in Diyat Cases
Three rulings from al-Muntaqa on evidence and diyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rulings on accepting a dying declaration and on a son's evidence against another son for accidental injury, and explain the consequences for inheritance and diyah liability.

Core Idea
A dying declaration is admissible, and a son's evidence against his brother can exclude him from inheritance and shift diyah to the 'aqila.

This passage from al-Muntaqa presents three independent rulings from early Hanafi authorities. The first deals with the admissibility of a dying declaration of murder and subsequent evidence by the heir. The second, from Abu Yusuf, addresses a unique scenario where a son gives evidence against another son for accidentally injuring their father, leading to exclusion from inheritance and diyah liability on the 'aqila. The third, from Muhammad al-Shaybani, clarifies liability when a person brings a sleeping or unconscious individual into a building that collapses. These rulings illustrate how evidence and liability interact in Hanafi diyat law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the first ruling on dying declaration
Read the first sentence: 'A man said, "So-and-so killed me," then his heir brought evidence against another man that he killed him, his evidence is accepted.' Note that the dying declaration itself is not enough; the heir must bring evidence. The ruling confirms that such evidence is accepted.
2
Analyze Abu Yusuf's ruling on son's evidence
Read the second ruling: A wounded man says 'So-and-so wounded me,' then his son brings evidence against another son of the wounded man for an accidental wound. Abu Yusuf accepts the evidence and excludes the defendant son from inheritance. Understand that despite the conflict of interest, the evidence is valid because it leads to a just outcome.
The son giving evidence is testifying against his brother; this is allowed here, unlike general rules against relatives testifying against each other, because the purpose is to establish diyah liability and adjust inheritance.
3
Determine the consequence for diyah
Since the defendant son is excluded from inheritance, the diyah (blood money) for the accidental injury is placed on the 'aqila (the male relatives on the father's side). This follows the general Hanafi rule that 'aqila pays diyah for accidental harm.
4
Examine Muhammad's ruling on bringing a sleeping person
Read the third ruling: If a man brings a sleeping or unconscious person into his house and the house collapses on that person, he is not liable unless the person is an insane person or a child. This is because such persons cannot take care of themselves, so the host assumes a duty of care.
This ruling is an exception to the general principle that one is not liable for acts of God (house collapse).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى : فقال فلان قتلني ثم أقام وارثه بينة على رجل آخر أنه قتله قبلت بينته. وذكر بعد رجل جرح ذلك هذه المسألة أبي يوسف: رجل قال فلان جرحني فأقام ابن له بينة على ابن له آخر عن خطأ فإني أقبل البينة على الابن وأحرمه عن الميراث بذلك. فلما أجزنا ذلك في الميراث جعلنا الدية على عاقلته قال هشام سمعت محمداً يقول في رجل أدخل نائماً أو مغمى عليه في بيته فسقط البيت عليه قال : لا يضمن إلا في المعتوه والصبي.
And in al-Muntaqa: A man said, 'So-and-so killed me,' then his heir brought evidence against another man that he killed him, his evidence is accepted. And after that, a man was wounded by that, this issue of Abu Yusuf: A man said, 'So-and-so wounded me,' so his son brought evidence against another son of his (the wounded man's) regarding an accidental (wound), and I (Abu Yusuf) accept the evidence against the son and deprive him of inheritance by that. Since we allowed that in inheritance, we placed the diyah upon his 'aqila. Hisham said: I heard Muhammad say regarding a man who brought a sleeping or unconscious person into his house, and the house fell on him: He said: He is not liable except in the case of an insane person or a child.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage contains three distinct rulings. First, a dying declaration ('So-and-so killed me') is admissible, and if the heir brings evidence against another man, it is accepted. Second, a ruling from Abu Yusuf: if a man says 'So-and-so wounded me' and then his (the wounded man's) son brings evidence against another son of the wounded man (i.e., the wounded man's other son) for an accidental wound, Abu Yusuf accepts the evidence against that son and excludes him from inheritance. Since the son is thus excluded from inheritance (as a penalty or due to his liability), the diyah (blood money) is placed on the 'aqila (the male relatives on the father's side who pay diyah). Third, a ruling from Muhammad: if a man brings a sleeping or unconscious person into his house and the house collapses on that person, the man is not liable for the death unless the person brought in is an insane person or a child (i.e., those who cannot take care of themselves).

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'āqilah): The group of male relatives (usually on the father's side) who are responsible for paying diyah in cases of accidental killing or injury.
الميت (al-mayyit): The deceased person, here the one who made the dying declaration.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) He is executed.
B) He is excluded from inheritance.
C) He must pay diyah out of his own pocket.
D) He is disqualified as a witness.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a son's evidence against his brother for accidental injury is accepted, resulting in the defendant's exclusion from inheritance and diyah liability on the 'āqila.

Ruling on Liability for Bringing a Sleeping Person into a Collapsing House
Case of a man who brings a sleeping person into his house and the house collapses, killing him.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a sleeping or unconscious person is brought into a collapsing house, and identify the exception for insane persons and children.

Core Idea
The host is not liable for a death caused by a collapsing house unless the victim is insane or a child.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Imam Muhammad: a man brings a sleeping or unconscious person into his house, and the house collapses, killing the person. The key legal question is whether the host is liable for the death. Imam Muhammad provides a nuanced answer: generally no liability, but liability if the victim is an insane person (ma'tuh) or a child (sabi). This case illustrates the Hanafi approach to causation and responsibility in tort law (diyat).

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
The host brings a sleeping or unconscious person into his house, and then the house collapses, causing the death of that person.
The key is the host's act of bringing the person into a dangerous environment.
2
Determine the default ruling
Imam Muhammad states that the host is not liable (la yadman) for the death. No diyah or qisas is due.
The death is attributed to the collapse, not to the host's action directly.
3
Identify the exception
Liability (daman) applies only if the victim is an insane person (ma'tuh) or a child (sabi).
The Arabic phrase: 'لا يضمن إلا في المعتوه والصبي' — 'He is not liable except in the case of an insane person or a child.'
4
Understand the rationale
A sleeping or unconscious person may wake up and save themselves, whereas an insane person or child lacks the cognitive ability to avoid danger. Therefore, bringing them into a hazardous house constitutes negligence.
This distinction is based on the victim's capacity to protect themselves.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال هشام سمعت محمداً يقول في رجل أدخل نائماً أو مغمى عليه في بيته فسقط البيت عليه قال : لا يضمن إلا في المعتوه والصبي.
Hisham said: I heard Muhammad say regarding a man who brought a sleeping or unconscious person into his house, and the house fell on him: He said: He is not liable except in the case of an insane person or a child.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This is a short ruling from Imam Muhammad. A man brings a sleeping or unconscious person into his house. The house collapses, killing that person. Muhammad rules that the man is not liable (i.e., does not have to pay diyah or face qisas) because the death is due to the collapse, not the man's action. However, if the person brought in is an insane person (ma'tuh) or a child (sabi), then the man is liable because these individuals cannot be expected to protect themselves, and bringing them into a dangerous situation constitutes negligence.

Key Terms
المعتوه (al-ma'tūh): An insane person, one lacking reason.
الصبي (al-ṣabī): A child, typically one who has not reached puberty.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

C) Only if the person is insane or a child.
D) Only if the person is awake and conscious.

Key Takeaway
The host is not liable for a death caused by a collapsing house unless the victim is an insane person or a child, due to their inability to protect themselves.

Proportional Liability for Joint Injuries to a Slave
Case of joint injuries to a slave with recovery and death.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the liability of each injurer when two persons jointly injure a slave, applying the Hanafi rule of proportional payment based on the 'arsh of each injury.

Core Idea
When two persons injure a slave, they pay his value in proportion to the injury each caused, not equally.

This passage from al-Muntaqa presents a series of rulings on joint injuries to a slave. It establishes a key principle: liability is shared proportionally to the extent of each injury, measured by the 'arsh (compensation due for that injury). We will parse the various sub-scenarios to understand how this principle is applied in practice.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base case
Read the first scenario: two persons each injure the slave in different parts (e.g., one gouges eyes, another cuts leg/hand), and the slave recovers. Note that the injuries together exhaust the slave's full value.
If injuries together reduce value to zero, the injurers become co-owners in proportion to their liability.
2
Extract the proportion rule
The key phrase is 'يغرمان قيمته على قدر أرش جراحتهما' – they pay his value according to the amount of the compensation for their injuries. This means payment is proportional to the 'arsh (fixed compensation) of each injury, not equal.
'Arsh is the standard compensation for a specific injury to a free person; here it is used as a measure of damage to the slave's value.
3
Analyze the death scenario
If the slave dies from accidental injuries, the first injurer pays the 'arsh of his injury from the slave's value at the time of the first injury; the remaining value is split equally between both. This adjusts for the fact that the second injury may have been made worse by the first.
The 'arsh is calculated from the slave's value after the first injury.
4
Parse the recovery scenario with overlapping exhaustion
If the slave recovers, and the last injury alone exhausts the value, and the first also exhausts, then the first pays 'arsh and the second pays the full value of the slave as injured by the first wound, and the slave goes to the second.
This handles cases where each injury individually would have exhausted the value.
5
Parse the opposite exhaustion scenario
If the first injury is the one that exhausts the value (alone), then the second injurer pays only his 'arsh, not a share of the full value.
This prevents double liability when the slave's value was already eliminated by the first injury.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى : رجل فقأ عيني عبد وقطع الآخر رجله أو يده فبرأ وكانت الجناية منهما معاً فعليهما قيمته أثلاثاً ويأخذان العبد فيكون بينهما على قدر ذلك، وكذلك كل جارحة من اثنين معاً جراحة عضو وجراحة الآخر في عضو تستغرق ذلك القيمة كلها فإنه يدفعه إليها ويغرمان قيمته على قدر أرش جراحتهما ويكون بينهما على ذلك، وإن مات منهما والجراحة خطأ فعلى كل واحد منهما فعلى الجارح الأول أرش جراحته من قيمته مجروحاً بالجراح الأولى وما بقي من قيمته فعليهما نصفان وإن برأ منهما والجراحة الأخيرة تستغرق القيمة والجراحة الأولى تستغرق فعلى الأول أرش جراحته وعلى الثاني قيمته مجروحاً بالجرح الأول ويدفع العبد إليه، وإن كانت الجراحة الأولى هي التي تستغرق القيمة فعلى الجارح الثاني أرش جراحته.
And in al-Muntaqa: A man gouged out the eyes of a slave, and another cut off his leg or hand, and he recovered, and the injury from both (was) together, so upon them both is his value in thirds, and they take the slave and it is between them according to that. Similarly, every (case of) two persons together, one injuring a limb and the other injuring a limb (such that) that (injury) exhausts the entire value, then he (the slave) is handed over to them and they pay his value according to the amount of the compensation for their injuries, and it is between them accordingly. If he dies from them and the injuries are accidental, then upon each one of them: upon the first injurer is the compensation for his injury from his (the slave's) value when injured by the first injuries, and what remains of his value, upon them both equally (half each). If he recovers from them, and the last injury exhausts the value and the first injury exhausts (it as well), then upon the first is the compensation for his injury, and upon the second is his value when injured by the first wound, and he hands the slave over to him. If the first injury is the one that exhausts the value, then upon the second injurer is the compensation for his injury.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This is a detailed ruling on joint injuries to a slave. Two people each injure the slave in different parts (one gouges eyes, another cuts leg/hand). The slave recovers. The rule: if the two injuries together bring the slave's value to nothing (i.e., the slave's value is diminished to zero), the two injurers must pay the full value of the slave, divided proportionally to the extent of each injury (measured by the 'arsh or compensation for that injury). They then take possession of the slave in proportion to what they paid. The passage then explains various sub-scenarios: if the slave dies from the injuries (accidental), each injurer pays a share: the first pays the 'arsh of his injury (calculated from the slave's value before any injury), and the remaining value is split equally between them. If the slave recovers but the last injury alone exhausts the value, the second pays the full value (as if he owned the slave) and the first gets the slave but must pay the second the 'arsh of his injury? The text is complex. The key principle: liability is shared proportionally to the injury, and if multiple injuries collectively destroy the value, they share the slave's value.

Key Terms
أرش الجراحة (arsh al-jirāḥah): The fixed compensation for a specific injury to a free person or the reduction in value of a slave due to injury.
القيمة (al-qīmah): The market value of the slave at the time of injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) They each pay half of the slave's value.
B) They pay the full value in proportion to the 'arsh of each injury.
C) The first to strike pays all.
D) They divide the slave between them without payment.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, joint injuries to a slave require each injurer to pay compensation in proportion to the damage they caused, measured by the 'arsh of their specific injury.

Ruling on Accessory in Murder and Theft
Case of accessory in murder and theft

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the liability of a person who holds a victim while another kills or steals, and distinguish it from the liability of the direct perpetrator.

Core Idea
The holder (mumsik) bears no liability; only the direct actor is liable.

In Hanafi law, the person who merely holds a victim while another kills or steals is not held liable for the crime. This ruling is based on the principle that liability follows direct causation. In this lesson, we examine the precise ruling from the matn and its application to both murder and theft cases.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties involved
Recognize the two roles: the holder (mumsik) and the direct perpetrator (killer or thief).
The holder merely restrains the victim; the direct perpetrator commits the act.
2
State the ruling for intentional and accidental killing
For intentional murder (ʿamd), the killer faces qiṣāṣ; for accidental (khaṭaʾ), he pays diyah. The holder has no liability.
The text explicitly says 'lā shayʾa ʿalā al-mumsik' (nothing upon the holder).
3
Apply the same principle to theft
The same rule applies: if one holds the owner while another steals, only the thief is liable for the stolen property.
The text says 'ḍamān al-dirham ʿalā al-ākhidh' (liability is on the taker).
4
Understand the Hanafi legal basis
Hanafi law holds the direct cause (mubāshir) liable, not the indirect cause (mutasabbib). The holder is not the direct cause of death or theft.
This contrasts with other schools that may impose joint liability on accessories.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن أمسك رجلاً حتى جاء آخر وقتله عمداً أو خطأ فلا شيء على الممسك عندنا وعلى القاتل القصاص في العمد والدية في الخطأ. وعلى هذا من وهي جاء آخر وأخذ دراهمه فضمان الدراهم على الآخذ عندنا لا على الممسك.
And whoever holds a man until another comes and kills him intentionally or accidentally, there is nothing upon the holder according to us, and upon the killer is qisas in intentional and diyah in accidental. And upon this (analogy) is whoever (is in a situation where) another comes and takes his dirhams, so the liability for the dirhams is upon the taker according to us, not upon the holder.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This is a clear Hanafi ruling on accessories. If someone holds a victim while another person kills him (whether intentionally or accidentally), the holder is not liable for the death. Only the actual killer faces qisas or diyah. The same principle applies to theft: if one person holds the owner while another steals the money, the thief is solely liable for the stolen property. This is based on the Hanafi principle that the direct cause (the actual killer or taker) is the one who creates liability, not the indirect cause (the holder).

Key Terms
الممسك (al-mumsik): The person who holds or restrains the victim, facilitating the crime.
الضمان (al-ḍamān): Liability for damages, here for stolen property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what is the liability of a person who holds a victim while another kills him?
A) He is jointly liable for qisas or diyah.
B) He is liable only if the killing is intentional.
C) There is no liability on him.
D) He is liable for half the blood money.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the holder (mumsik) bears no liability for murder or theft; only the direct perpetrator is liable.

Compensation for Shubha Intercourse or Hymen Removal
Ruling on compensation calculation for shubha intercourse or hymen removal.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the compensation for intercourse with a slave-girl under shubha or hymen removal by comparing mahr al-mithl and the deficiency in value.

Core Idea
Compensation is the greater of mahr al-mithl and the reduction in value due to hymen loss.

This lesson examines two methods of calculating financial compensation for harming a slave-girl: when a man mistakenly believes she is his own (shubha) or when he removes her hymen. The ruling from al-Khaniyya, as explained by Ibn Nujaym, provides a clear formula that ensures the victim is fully compensated without double-counting.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the obligation
The act is intercourse under shubha or hymen removal. The obligation is financial compensation.
Shubha negates the hadd penalty but not civil liability.
2
Determine mahr al-mithl
Calculate the standard dower (mahr al-mithl) for a woman of similar status to the slave-girl.
This is based on age, beauty, family, and other relevant factors.
3
Calculate the deficiency in value
Determine the reduction in the slave-girl's market value due to loss of virginity (removal of hymen).
This is a separate measure from mahr al-mithl.
4
Compare and take the greater
Compare mahr al-mithl and the deficiency. The larger amount is wajib; the smaller is included in it.
The text: 'يدخل الأقل في الأكثر' (the lesser is included in the greater).
5
Apply to factual scenarios
Practice with examples: if mahr al-mithl is 100 and deficiency 80, pay 100. If mahr is 60 and deficiency 90, pay 90.
The goal is full compensation without duplication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية : لو وطئ جارية إنسان بشبهة أو أزال بكارتها فعلى قول أبي يوسف ومحمد ينظر إلى مهر مثلها فيزاد إلى نقصان بكارتها إن كان أكثر يجب ذلك ويدخل الأقل في الأكثر . ولو أن صبياً زنى في صبية وأذهب عذرتها كان عليه المهر بإزالة البكارة لو كانت المرأة بالغة مستكرهة، وإن كانت مطاوعة لا يجب المهر لأنه لو وجب على الصبي كان لولي الصبي أن يرجع بذلك عليها كما لو أمر صبياً بشيء يلحقه ضمانه كان لولي الصغير أن يرجع على الأمر فلا يفيد تضمين الصغير ولو أن امرأة بالغة غصبها فزنى بها وأذهب عذرتها بأمرها كان على الصبي مهرها لأن أمر الأمة لم يصح في حق مولى الأمة. حريق وقع في محلة فهدم رجل دار
And in al-Khaniyya: If a man has intercourse with someone's slave-girl under shubha or removes her hymen, then according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, one looks at the mahr al-mithl of her like, then it is increased to the deficiency of her hymen. If it (the mahr) is more, that is obligatory, and the lesser is included in the greater. And if a boy commits zina with a girl and removes her hymen, upon him is the mahr for removal of the hymen if the woman was an adult coerced; and if she was consenting, the mahr is not obligatory because if it were obligatory on the boy, the boy's guardian would have the right to claim it back from her, just as if a boy was ordered to do something that entails liability, the guardian of the minor has the right to recourse against the one who gave the order, so making the boy liable is useless. And if a woman was an adult, he had intercourse with her and removed her hymen by her command, then upon the boy is her mahr because the command of the slave-girl is not valid regarding the right of the master of the slave-girl. A fire occurred in a neighborhood, so a man demolished a house...
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage contains two distinct rulings from al-Khaniyya. First, if a man has intercourse with another's slave-girl under a mistaken belief of ownership (shubha) or removes her hymen, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that compensation is calculated by comparing the mahr al-mithl (market value of a similar woman's dower) with the reduction in value due to loss of virginity. The greater of the two is taken; the lesser is included in the greater. Second, if a minor boy (sabiyy) commits zina with a girl and removes her hymen, the ruling differs based on consent: if the girl is an adult and was coerced, the boy must pay mahr. If she consented, no mahr is due because if the boy were liable, his guardian would have recourse against the girl (as she commanded or consented), negating the point of making the boy liable. The text adds a case where an adult woman ordered the boy to do it: here the boy must pay mahr because the woman's command is invalid regarding the rights of her master (if she is a slave-girl). Finally, the passage begins a ruling on a fire in a neighborhood and a man demolishing a house, but the text cuts off.

Key Terms
شبهة (shubhah): A mistaken belief or uncertainty that the act is lawful, here believing the woman is his own slave.
مهر المثل (mahr al-mithl): The standard dower for a woman of similar status, used as compensation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad, how is compensation calculated for intercourse with a slave-girl under shubha?
A) Only the reduction in hymen value.
B) Only the mahr al-mithl.
C) The greater of mahr al-mithl and the deficiency in value.
D) The average of the two.

Key Takeaway
Compensation for shubha intercourse or hymen removal is wajib as the greater of mahr al-mithl and the deficiency in value, with the lesser included in the greater.

Liability of a Minor for Removing Hymen via Zina
Ruling on a minor boy's liability for mahr when he commits zina and removes hymen, with conditions of coercion and consent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a minor boy is liable for mahr when he removes a girl's hymen through zina, based on the conditions of coercion, consent, and the woman's status (free or slave).

Core Idea
A minor is liable for mahr for hymen removal only if the woman was coerced adult or a slave-girl who commanded him.

This lesson addresses the liability of a minor boy (sabiyy) who commits zina with a girl and removes her hymen. The ruling distinguishes between cases where the woman is an adult and coerced versus consenting, and also considers a special case involving a slave-girl. Understanding these nuances is essential for correctly applying Hanafi law in matters of sexual offenses involving minors.

Guided Path
1
Identify the actors and act
A minor boy (sabiyy) commits zina with a girl, resulting in removal of her hymen.
The focus is on the boy's liability for mahr.
2
Check the woman's status and consent
Determine if the woman is an adult (balighah) or a slave-girl, and whether she was coerced (mustakrihah) or consenting (muti'ah).
Consent here implies the woman commanded or willingly participated.
3
Apply the ruling for adult free woman
If she is an adult free woman and coerced: the boy must pay mahr. If she consented: no mahr is due to the boy because his guardian can claim recourse against her.
The text emphasizes that making the boy liable is useless if the guardian can recover from the woman.
4
Apply the ruling for a slave-girl commanding the boy
If the woman is a slave-girl and she commanded the boy (ordered him to do it), the boy must pay mahr because her command is invalid with respect to her master's rights.
A slave's command does not bind her master.
5
Synthesize the conditions
The minor's liability hinges on whether the woman's consent or command is legally effective. Coerced adult: effective (no consent) -> mahr due. Consenting adult: her consent allows recourse -> no mahr on boy. Slave-girl commanding: her command is void -> mahr due.
The principle is that the boy's liability is avoided only if the woman's own liability can be enforced.

Source Text

صبي (sabiyy): A minor boy, below the age of puberty.
مهر (mahr): The dower or compensation for marriage; here used as compensation for hymen removal.

Joint Liability for Injuries to a Slave: Proportional Payment Based on Arsh
Calculation of liability for joint non-fatal and fatal injuries to a slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate and state the liability of two injurers when they wound different limbs of a slave, applying the rule of proportional payment based on arsh.

Core Idea
When two people injure different limbs of a slave, liability is proportionate to the arsh of each wound.

The Hanafi jurists, particularly in works like al-Muntaqa, developed detailed rules for joint torts involving slaves. When two people each wound a slave in different limbs, the compensation is not simply split equally. Instead, the value of the slave is apportioned according to the fixed compensation (arsh) for each injury. This lesson parses the text to understand the calculation in various scenarios, including recovery, death, and cumulative fatal wounds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two injuries
Note the specific organs wounded (e.g., eyes, leg, hand) and whether the injuries are separate or overlapping.
The text assumes different limbs, so no overlap.
2
Determine if the slave recovers or dies
The ruling changes depending on whether the slave survives or dies from the wounds.
If the slave recovers, both pay proportionally. If death occurs, fatal wounder pays more.
3
Calculate arsh for each injury
Arsh is a fixed proportion of the slave's value. For example, blinding both eyes takes full value (tastaghriqu al-qimah).
Refer to Hanafi diyat schedules for exact arsh proportions.
4
Apportion the slave's value according to arsh ratios
If both wounds are non-fatal and slave recovers, the injurers pay the value in proportion to their arsh, and they become co-owners of the slave in that ratio.
Example: if arsh for wound A is 1/3 and for wound B is 1/6, they pay in 2:1 ratio.
5
Handle special cases: fatal or cumulative wounds
If one wound is fatal (arsh = full value) and the other is minor, the fatal wounder pays the full value minus the arsh of the minor wound, and receives the slave. If the first wound alone equals full value, the second wounder pays only his arsh.
The text gives two sub-scenarios: last wound fatal vs. first wound fatal.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى : رجل فقاً عيني عبد وقطع الآخر رجله أو يده فبرأ وكانت الجناية منهما معاً فعليهما قيمته أثلاثاً ويأخذان العبد فيكون بينهما على قدر ذلك، وكذلك كل جارحة من اثنين معاً جراحة عضو وجراحة الآخر في عضو تستغرق ذلك القيمة كلها فإنه يدفعه إليها ويغرمان قيمته على قدر أرش جراحتهما ويكون بينهما على ذلك، وإن مات منهما والجراحة خطأ فعلى كل واحد منهما فعلى الجارح الأول أرش جراحته من قيمته مجروحاً بالجراح الأولى وما بقي من قيمته فعليهما نصفان وإن برأ منهما والجراحة الأخيرة تستغرق القيمة والجراحة الأولى تستغرق فعلى الأول أرش جراحته وعلى الثاني قيمته مجروحاً بالجرح الأول ويدفع العبد إليه، وإن كانت الجراحة الأولى هي التي تستغرق القيمة فعلى الجارح الثاني أرش جراحته.
And in al-Muntaqa: A man poked out the eyes of a slave, and another cut his leg or hand, and he recovered, and the injury was from both together; then upon both is his value in thirds, and they take the slave, so it is between them in that proportion. And likewise, every wounder of two together, one wounding an organ and the other wounding an organ, (if) that encompasses the entire value, then he gives him (the slave) to them, and they pay his value according to the amount of the arsh of their wounds, and it is between them in that proportion. And if he dies from them and the wounding was error, then upon each one... And if he recovers from them and the last wound encompasses the value and the first wound encompasses it, then upon the first is the arsh of his wound, and upon the second is his value wounded by the first wound, and he gives the slave to him. And if the first wound is the one that encompasses the value, then upon the second wounder is the arsh of his wound.
Source pages p. 107

Commentary
This passage from al-Muntaqa presents a complex case of joint liability for injuries to a slave. Two men each injure the slave in different limbs (e.g., one blinds both eyes, the other cuts a leg/hand). The slave recovers. The liability is calculated based on the arsh (fixed compensation) for each injury relative to the slave's value. The general principle: each pays a share of the slave's value proportionate to the arsh of his injury, and they become co-owners of the slave. If one injury is fatal, the liability changes: the one who caused the fatal wound may pay more. The text gives detailed scenarios: when both wounds are non-fatal and the slave recovers, they pay in thirds; when one wound is fatal and the other not, the fatal wounder pays more. This requires careful parsing of arsh values and the slave's wounded value.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Fixed compensation for specific bodily injuries to a free person or slave, not exceeding full blood-money.
تستغرق القيمة (tastaghriqu al-qimah): The injury's arsh equals or exceeds the full value of the slave.
جرح (jarh): Wound or injury

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If two people injure a slave in different limbs, both wounds non-fatal, and the slave recovers, how do they determine payment?
A) Equal halves
B) In proportion to the arsh of their wounds
C) The one who caused more pain pays more
D) The first injurer pays full value

Key Takeaway
In joint injury to a slave, each injurer pays a share of the slave's value proportionate to the arsh of his wound, and they become co-owners in that proportion.

Self-Defense Against Children and the Insane: Liability for Defensive Killing
Ruling on killing attacking children/insane in self-defense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on killing an attacking child or insane person in self-defense and identify the intra-madhhab dispute regarding liability.

Core Idea
Killing an attacker who lacks legal capacity triggers diya, not qisas, and is prohibited even in self-defense.

Self-defense is a recognized right, but what if the attacker is a child or an insane person? The Hanafi school addresses this edge case with a nuanced ruling that balances the defender's necessity against the attacker's incapacity. This lesson unpacks the initial ruling from Imam Muhammad, the subsequent dispute, and the operative fatwa on liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and initial ruling
Read the first part of the passage: a person is surrounded by children or insane individuals intending to kill or take property, and he cannot repel them except by killing. What is the initial ruling from Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir?
The ruling is that killing is not permissible, and diya is obligatory.
2
Analyze the dispute within the Hanafi school
The passage then mentions that Abu Mut'i held the defender is liable (daman). Al-Mu'alla questioned Muhammad, who seemingly agreed with Abu Mut'i. Later scholars like Nasir and the 'shaykh' gave fatwa with liability, while Faqih Abu Bakr gave fatwa of non-liability.
Note the distinction: the initial text says 'la yahill' (not permissible) yet later fatwa seems to accept liability if one kills.
3
Determine the mufta bihi (operative ruling)
According to the shaykh (likely Abu Hafs), fatwa is given with liability (daman). Also Nasir used to judge with liability. Thus the operative ruling is that if one kills an attacking child or insane person in self-defense, diya is due. The defender is not to be killed in retribution (qisas), but must pay blood money.
This is the established position for fatwa despite the initial prohibition.
4
Identify the conditions for this ruling
The passage implies conditions: the attackers must be children or insane, they must intend to kill or take property, and there must be no other way to repel them except by killing.
These conditions are implicit; they define when the ruling applies.
5
Apply the ruling to practical scenarios
Consider a modern case: a mentally ill person breaks into your home with a knife. You have no escape. Can you kill them? According to Hanafi fatwa, you may not intentionally kill, but if you do, you must pay diya. However, some later scholars (Abu Bakr) said no liability. The mufta bihi is liability.
This ruling is specific to attackers lacking legal responsibility (ahliyyah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الجامع الصغير عن محمد فيمن اجتمع عليه الصبيان أو المجانين يريدون قتله . وفي الحاوي : أو أخذ ماله ولا يقدر على دفعهم إلا بالقتل قال : ليس له أن يقتلهم ولو قتل تجب عليه الدية . قال المعلى قلت لمحمد إن صاحبنا يقول بالضمان وعنى أنه أبو مطيع قال المعلى : كنت في الطواف فإذا محمد بن الحسن فقال يا خراساني القول ما قال صاحبكم. قال الشيخ وبه يفتي وكان نصير يقضي بالضمان في الصبي والمجنون والبهيمة إذا قتله الرجل دافعاً. وكان الفقيه أبو بكر يفتي بعدم الضمان . قال الفقيه أبو الليث : هذا القول يخالف ما قيل في الروايات الظاهرة
And in al-Jami' al-Saghir from Muhammad concerning one upon whom children or insane persons gather intending to kill him. And in al-Hawi: or take his money, and he is not able to repel them except by killing, he said: It is not permissible for him to kill them, and if he kills, diya is obligatory upon him. Al-Mu'alla said: I said to Muhammad: 'Our companion says with liability,' meaning Abu Mut'i. Al-Mu'alla said: I was in tawaf and there was Muhammad ibn al-Hasan; he said: 'O Khurasani, the statement is what your companion said.' The shaykh said: And by it fatwa is given. Nasir used to judge with liability in (the case of) the child, the insane, and the animal if the man kills them while defending. And the faqih Abu Bakr used to give fatwa of non-liability. Al-faqih Abu al-Layth said: This statement contradicts what is said in the apparent narrations.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents a dispute within the Hanafi school regarding self-defense against attackers who lack legal responsibility: children (sibyan) and the insane (majanin). According to Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir and al-Hawi, it is not permissible to kill such attackers, even if they are trying to kill the victim or take his property and cannot be repelled otherwise. If one kills them, diya (blood money) is obligatory. However, there is a difference: Abu Mut'i (a Hanafi scholar) held that the defender is liable (daman). Al-Mu'alla relates that Imam Muhammad actually agreed with Abu Mut'i's position (i.e., liability). The text then notes that the later shaykh (possibly Abu Hafs or others) gave fatwa according to Muhammad's final position (i.e., liability). Nasir used to judge with liability for child, insane, and also animals. On the other hand, Faqih Abu Bakr gave fatwa of non-liability. Faqih Abu al-Layth critiques the non-liability view as conflicting with the apparent narrations (al-riwayat al-zahirah). The operative ruling in later fatwa (mufta bihi) seems to be liability (daman) when killing in self-defense against these categories, but there is ikhtilaf.

Key Terms
صبيان (sibyan): Children who have not reached puberty; legally not fully responsible.
مجانين (majanin): Insane persons; also lack full legal responsibility.
ضمان (daman): Liability, here meaning the obligation to pay diya.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the initial ruling from Muhammad, what is the consequence of killing an attacking child in self-defense?
A) Qisas
B) No liability
C) Diya is obligatory
D) Tazir

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, killing a child or insane attacker in self-defense is prohibited and incurs diya; later fatwa settled on liability (daman) as the operative ruling.

Rulings on Mutual Killing with Unknown Aggressor
Three scenarios of mutual killing with unknown aggressor, rulings on diyat.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the diyat (blood money) rulings for three scenarios of mutual killing between a free man and a slave when the initial aggressor is unknown.

Core Idea
The weapon used dictates liability: the better-armed fighter's side pays compensation when aggressor unknown.

When two people fight and both die, and it is unknown who struck first, Islamic law faces a dilemma in assigning liability. The Hanafi school, through the principle of istihsan (juristic preference), infers the likely aggressor from the type of weapon used. This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents three key scenarios involving a free man and a slave, each leading to different rulings on diyat.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and weapons
For each case, note the status of each combatant (free man vs. slave) and the weapon each carries (sword vs. staff).
Weapons indicate lethality: sword > staff.
2
Determine the outcome
Both die, and the initial aggressor is unknown.
This fact triggers the need for istihsan.
3
Apply the Hanafi ruling
State the diyat (or lack thereof) based on the weapon distribution. Compare the three cases given.
Case 1: free man sword + slave staff → nothing due. Case 2: slave sword + free man staff → free man's 'aqlah pays half slave's value. Case 3: both staffs → free man's 'aqlah pays full slave's value, master compensates wound.
4
Identify the underlying principle
The ruling is based on istihsan, which presumes the more lethal weapon signals aggression.
Qiyas would lead to no liability because causation is uncertain.
5
Locate the additional report
Note the transmission from Bishr ibn al-Walid from Abu Yusuf, which confirms the same logic for a similar scenario.
This reinforces the school's position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابن سماعة. محمد: حر معه سيف وعبد معه عصا فالتقيا وضرب كل واحد منهما صاحبه حتى قتله وماتا ولا يدري أيهما بدأ بالضرب فليس على ورثة الحر ولا على مولى العبد شيء، وإن كان السيف بيد العبد والعصا بيد الحر فعلى عاقلة الحر نصف قيمة العبد ولا شيء لورثة الحر على مولى العبد، وإن كان بيد كل واحد منهما عصار وضرب كل واحد منهما الآخر وشجه موضحة ثم ماتا ولا يدري من الذي بدأ بالضرب فعلى عاقلة الحر قيمة العبد صحيحاً لمولاه ثم يقال لمولاه ادفع من ذلك قيمة الشجة إلى ولي الحر وهذا استحسان والقياس أن لا يكون له شيء منه بشر بن الوليد عن أبي يوسف في رجل ضرب كل واحد منهما صاحبه هذا بالسيف وهذا معه عصا فماتا ولا يدري أيهما بدأ قال: على صاحب العصا نصف دية صاحب السيف على عاقلته وليس لصاحب العصا شيء.
Ibn Sama'ah: Muhammad (al-Shaybani) said: A free man with a sword and a slave with a staff met, and each struck his companion until he killed him, and both died, and it is not known which one started the striking. So nothing is due from the heirs of the free man nor from the master of the slave. If the sword was in the slave's hand and the staff in the free man's hand, then upon the 'aqlah (tribal payers) of the free man is half the value of the slave, and nothing is due for the heirs of the free man from the slave's master. If each of them had a staff and each struck the other and inflicted a head wound (mudihah), then both died, and it is not known who started the striking, then upon the 'aqlah of the free man is the full value of the slave (as if sound) to his master, then it is said to the master: Pay from that the value of the head wound to the guardian of the free man. This is istihsan. By qiyas, he would have nothing from it. (Also reported) from Bishr ibn al-Walid from Abu Yusuf concerning a man where each struck his companion, one with a sword and one with a staff, and both died, and it is not known who started. He said: Upon the companion of the staff is half the blood-money of the companion of the sword on his 'aqlah, and the companion of the sword has nothing.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage presents multiple scenarios of mutual killing between a free man and a slave where the initial aggressor is unknown. The rulings vary based on the weapons used. The key point: if both have equal means (both staffs), and both die, the free man's 'aqlah pays the slave's value to the master, but the master must compensate the free man's family for the wound; if the weapons differ, the free man's 'aqlah pays only half the slave's value when the slave had the sword. The author notes that this is istihsan (juristic preference), as qiyas would leave nothing for the free man's family. The additional report from Abu Yusuf confirms the principle that liability is on the less powerful weapon (staff) holder.

Key Terms
عاقلة ('aqlah): The tribal group or community responsible for paying blood money on behalf of a member who kills accidentally or quasi-intentionally.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from qiyas for a stronger reason, here based on customary probability.
دية (diyah): Blood money, the financial compensation for homicide or injury, replacing qisas in certain cases.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where a free man with a sword and a slave with a staff fight and both die, and it is unknown who struck first, what is the ruling according to the Hanafi school?
A) Nothing is due from either side.
B) The free man's 'aqlah pays half the value of the slave.
C) The slave's master pays full diyah for the free man.
D) The free man's 'aqlah pays full diyah for the free man.

Key Takeaway
In mutual killing with unknown aggressor, the Hanafi school uses istihsan to assign diyat based on the lethality of weapons, presuming the better-armed party is the aggressor.

The Methodological Shift: From Qiyas to Istihsan
Methodological analysis of qiyas vs istihsan in the mutual killing scenarios.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi reasoning for preferring istihsan over qiyas in the mutual killing scenarios, and identify the illa (effective cause) that justifies the departure.

Core Idea
Istihsan overrides qiyas when customary probability indicates aggression, ensuring compensation flows to the weaker party.

The previous lesson presented the rulings on mutual killing with unknown aggressor. This lesson dives deeper into the legal methodology behind those rulings. Why does the Hanafi school set aside the straightforward qiyas (analogy) that would yield no liability? The answer lies in istihsan (juristic preference), a source of law unique to the Hanafi methodology. Here, we unpack the logical structure of the qiyas argument, the istihsan counter-argument, and the extracted illa (effective cause) of weapon disparity.

Guided Path
1
Construct the qiyas (analogy) argument
Start with the principle that liability requires proof of causation. Since the aggressor is unknown, qiyas says no one is liable. Identify the asl (original case) and the hukm (no liability).
Asl: any case where causation is uncertain. Hukm: no liability.
2
Identify the far' (new case)
The far' is the mutual killing with different weapons. The apparent similarity to the asl is the unknown aggressor.
The far' is not automatically the same because of the weapon factor.
3
Extract the illa (effective cause) for istihsan
The Hanafi jurists identify that the difference in weaponry (lethality) is a strong indicator of who likely started the aggression. This illa is khafiyyah (hidden) and extracted via tanqih al-manat (refining the rationale).
The illa is the 'customary probability' that the more lethal weapon signals aggression.
4
See how istihsan applies
Because the weapon difference is a reliable indicator, the school prefers to assign liability based on that indicator, overriding the qiyas. This is istihsan.
Istihsan is not arbitrary; it is a stronger evidence in this context.
5
Contrast with the direction of the illa in other scenarios
When both have staffs (equal weapons), the illa of weapon difference disappears. Then istihsan leads to a different allocation: full value of slave to master, offset by wound compensation. This shows the scope of the illa.
The illa only applies when weapons differ in lethality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ابن سماعة. محمد: حر معه سيف وعبد معه عصا فالتقيا وضرب كل واحد منهما صاحبه حتى قتله وماتا ولا يدري أيهما بدأ بالضرب فليس على ورثة الحر ولا على مولى العبد شيء، وإن كان السيف بيد العبد والعصا بيد الحر فعلى عاقلة الحر نصف قيمة العبد ولا شيء لورثة الحر على مولى العبد، وإن كان بيد كل واحد منهما عصار وضرب كل واحد منهما الآخر وشجه موضحة ثم ماتا ولا يدري من الذي بدأ بالضرب فعلى عاقلة الحر قيمة العبد صحيحاً لمولاه ثم يقال لمولاه ادفع من ذلك قيمة الشجة إلى ولي الحر وهذا استحسان والقياس أن لا يكون له شيء منه بشر بن الوليد عن أبي يوسف في رجل ضرب كل واحد منهما صاحبه هذا بالسيف وهذا معه عصا فماتا ولا يدري أيهما بدأ قال: على صاحب العصا نصف دية صاحب السيف على عاقلته وليس لصاحب العصا شيء.
Ibn Sama'ah: Muhammad (al-Shaybani) said: A free man with a sword and a slave with a staff met, and each struck his companion until he killed him, and both died, and it is not known which one started the striking. So nothing is due from the heirs of the free man nor from the master of the slave. If the sword was in the slave's hand and the staff in the free man's hand, then upon the 'aqlah (tribal payers) of the free man is half the value of the slave, and nothing is due for the heirs of the free man from the slave's master. This is istihsan. By qiyas, he would have nothing from it. He said: Upon the companion of the staff is half the blood-money of the companion of the sword on his 'aqlah, and the companion of the sword has nothing.

استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from qiyas for a stronger reason, here based on customary probability.
العلة (al-'illah): The effective cause or rationale behind a ruling, especially in analogy (qiyas).
تنقيح المناط (tanqih al-manat): Refining the underlying rationale by testing it across different cases.
