Validity of a Dying Declaration in Diyat: The Condition of Public Knowledge
Ruling on validity of victim's testimony disowning a known wound

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the condition of 'public knowledge' (ma'rifah) to determine the legal effect of a wounded person's testimony (ishhad) in diyat cases.

Core Idea
A dying declaration about the cause of a wound is binding unless the wound is publicly known.

In Hanafi fiqh, the testimony of a wounded person regarding the cause of his wound is treated with nuance. This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents a key condition: whether the wound was known to the judge and the public. If unknown, the victim's declaration that so-and-so did not wound him is valid and conclusive, barring later evidence from heirs. If known, the declaration is disregarded as false. Understanding this distinction is crucial for adjudicating diyat claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal scenario
A man intentionally wounds another with a sword. The wounded man, before dying, formally declares (ashhada) that the perpetrator did not wound him. He then dies from the wound. What is the effect of this declaration?
The declaration is akin to a dying declaration (ishhad).
2
Distinguish the two conditions
The text states two possibilities: either the wound of the accused is known ('arifa) before the judge and the people, or it is not known. Parse each condition separately.
The term 'ma'ruf' (known) is key.
3
Apply the ruling for an unknown wound
If the wound is not known, the testimony (ishhad) is valid (sahih). The text later adds from al-Dhakhirah that even if the heirs bring evidence after his death, that evidence is not accepted. So the declaration bars any subsequent claim.
The declaration is conclusive because there is no public knowledge to contradict it.
4
Infer the ruling for a known wound
Although not explicitly stated, it follows that if the wound is known, the declaration is invalid. The victim's denial contradicts established fact, so it is disregarded. The heirs may then pursue qisas or diyah.
This is the logical corollary: knowledge defeats the declaration.
5
Consider the authority and cross-madhhab view
This ruling is from al-Dhakhirah, a later Hanafi work. Other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow heirs to bring evidence even after such a declaration, but the Hanafi position is that once valid, it is conclusive.
Know the mufta bihi: the position stated here is operative.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا جرح الرجل عمداً بالسيف فأشهد المجروح بالسيف على نفسه أن فلاناً لم يجرحه ثم مات المجروح من هذا الإشهاد؟ قالوا: هذا على وجهين إما أن تكون جراحة فلان معروفة عند القاضي وعند الناس أو غير معروفة، فإن لم تكن معروفة كان الإشهاد صحيحاً وفي الذخيرة وإن أقام الورثة بينة بعد ذلك على أن فلاناً جرحه لم تقبل هذه البينة.
And if a man wounds (another) intentionally with a sword, then the wounded man testifies (ashhada) against himself that so-and-so did not wound him, then the wounded man dies from that wound? They said: This is of two aspects: either the wound of so-and-so is known before the judge and the people, or it is not known. If it is not known, the testimony is valid. And in al-Dhakhirah: Even if the heirs later bring evidence that so-and-so wounded him, that evidence is not accepted.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage discusses a case where a wounded person, before dying, gives a sworn testimony (ishhad) declaring that a certain person did not wound him. The ruling depends on whether the wound was publicly known. If the wound was not widely known, the testimony of the victim is considered valid and conclusive, and any later claim or evidence from the heirs is rejected. This is based on the principle that a dying person's declaration about his own injury is binding, similar to a bequest, as long as it is not contradicted by known facts. The 'al-Dhakhirah' reference adds that even post-mortem evidence from heirs is inadmissible.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): A formal testimony or declaration made by a wounded person about the cause of his wound, akin to a dying declaration.
جرح معروف (jarh ma'ruf): A wound that is publicly known and acknowledged by people or the judge.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) The wound must not be known to the public.
C) The testimony must be made in writing.
D) The heirs must agree.

Key Takeaway
The validity of a wounded person's testimony that someone did not wound him depends on whether the wound was publicly known; if unknown, it is binding and bars later evidence.

Collective Striking of Slave by Multiple Persons
Rulings on liability when multiple persons strike a slave and one hits without permission

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the liability of an unauthorized striker when multiple strikes cause a slave's death, applying the Hanafi fractional method.

Core Idea
An unauthorized striker pays both the arsh of his strike and a fraction of the slave's total value.

This passage from al-Tajrid presents a detailed fiqh problem: when ten authorized men and one unauthorized man strike a slave, and the slave dies, how is liability apportioned? Understanding the calculation is crucial for Islamic tort law (diyat). The Hanafi approach achieves precision by considering both the incremental harm (arsh) and the proportional share of the total value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage and note the two separate cases: (1) ten authorized lashes plus one unauthorized, and (2) master's ten lashes plus one unauthorized. Recognize that the calculation differs for each.
The first scenario involves only hired strikers; the second involves the master himself.
2
Distinguish authorized vs unauthorized strikes
In the first scenario, ten men are ordered to strike once each – these are authorized. The eleventh striker is unauthorized. Liability only attaches to the unauthorized person.
Authorized strikers (including the master) are not liable for death unless they exceeded limits.
3
Compute the arsh (deficiency) from the unauthorized strike
Determine the slave's market value after the ten authorized lashes (i.e., without the unauthorized strike). The unauthorized striker pays the difference between that value and the value after his lash. This is the 'arsh' of his strike.
The passage says 'arsh of the deficiency caused by his strike, as if struck ten lashes' – meaning the value after ten is the baseline.
4
Compute the fractional share of the slave's value
Add the unauthorized strike to the total (11 lashes). The unauthorized striker pays 1/11 of the slave's value after all eleven lashes. This fraction represents his proportional causal contribution to the death.
The total liability is the sum of the arsh and the fraction.
5
Analyze the second scenario
When the master strikes ten times first, then an unauthorized man strikes once and the slave dies, the unauthorized man pays the arsh of his lash plus half (1/2) of the value after eleven lashes. The fraction is larger because the master's strikes already created a baseline, and the unauthorized strike is more significant.
The half (1/2) is derived from the fact that the unauthorized strike caused the remaining damage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي التجريد ولو أمر رجل عشرة رجال أن يضرب كل واحد منهم عبده سوطاً ففعلوا ثم إن آخر ضرب سوطاً ولم يأمره فمات العبد من ذلك كله فعلى الذي لم يؤمر أرش ما نقص بضربه مضروباً عشرة أسواط وعليه أيضاً جزء من أحد عشر جزءاً من قيمته مضروباً أحد عشر سوطاً. ولو أن المولى ضربه بيده عشرة أسواط ثم ضربه هذا الرجل سوطاً ومات فعليه نقصان سوطه ونصف قيمته مضروباً أحد عشر سوطاً.
And in al-Tajrid: If a man ordered ten men to each strike his slave one lash, and they did so, then another (person) struck a lash without being ordered, and the slave died from all of that, then upon the one who was not ordered is the compensation (arsh) of the deficiency caused by his strike, calculated as if struck ten lashes, and upon him also a portion of one eleventh of his value as if struck eleven lashes. And if the master himself struck him ten lashes, then this man struck him one lash and he died, then upon him is the deficiency of his lash and half of his value as if struck eleven lashes.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage from al-Tajrid provides detailed calculations for when multiple people strike a slave and one unauthorized strike contributes to death. The key is apportioning liability. In the first scenario, ten authorized lashes and one unauthorized: the unauthorized striker pays (1) the arsh (diminution in value) that his strike caused, based on the value if only ten lashes had been given (i.e., the incremental harm), and (2) a share of the full value (1/11) because his strike was part of the total eleven. In the second scenario, the master himself gave ten lashes, then the unauthorized man gave one: here the outsider pays not only the deficiency from his single lash, but also half the value (since his lash, being after the master's, caused a greater proportionate harm? The text says 'half' but careful examination suggests it's a mathematical fraction from the total eleven lashes: when the master gave ten, the outsider's one lash is considered to have caused half the death? Actually the math: the outsider is liable for 'deficiency of his lash' (i.e., how much the value dropped due to that lash) plus half the value of the slave after eleven lashes? This is a complex calculation indicating that the unauthorized striker's contribution is weighted more heavily when the master had already disciplined.

Key Terms
أرش (arsh): Compensation for decrease in value of a slave due to injury, not full value.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where ten authorized men strike a slave once each, then an unauthorized man strikes once and the slave dies, what does the unauthorized man pay?
A) Full value of the slave.
B) Arsh of deficiency from ten lashes plus 1/11 of value.
C) Only the arsh of his own lash.
D) Nothing because he was not ordered.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi method apportions liability based on the fraction of total strikes and the arsh (diminution in value) from the unauthorized strike.

Killing Non-Accountable Attackers: Diyah or No Liability?
Ruling on killing children or insane persons in self-defense when not able to repel otherwise

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi positions regarding killing children or insane persons in self-defense and identify the conflicting opinions within the school.

Core Idea
Killing a legally non-accountable attacker incurs diyah, yet some Hanafi authorities exempt liability.

This passage presents a challenging scenario: you are attacked by children or insane persons who intend to kill you or take your property, and you cannot repel them except by killing. The Hanafi school has a clear ruling from Zahir al-Riwayah that killing is impermissible and diyah is wajib if done. However, there is a notable intra-Hanafi disagreement, with some later scholars favoring no liability. We will dissect the arguments and the final tarjih.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Scenario and Default Ruling
Recognize the situation: children or insane persons attack intending to kill or take property, and the defender cannot repel without killing. The default ruling from Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir: it is not permissible to kill them; if done, diyah is wajib.
This is the position of Zahir al-Riwayah.
2
Locate the Opposing View
Abu Mut' al-Balkhi held that there is no daman (liability) if the killing is in self-defense. Imam Muhammad ibn al-Hasan reportedly agreed in a private conversation, saying 'the correct opinion is what your companion said.'
This creates an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
3
Trace Later Fatwa Practice
Nasir used to rule with liability (daman). Al-Faqih Abu Bakr gave fatwa of no liability. The shaykh (likely the author of al-Bahr al-Ra'iq) says the fatwa is on no liability, but Abu al-Layth criticizes this as contradicting the apparent narrations.
The later Hanafi tradition is divided.
4
Analyze the Tarjih
The shaykh prefers no liability for practical fatwa, but acknowledges the weight of Zahir al-Riwayah. Thus, the mufta bihi is no daman, but the matter is not settled definitively.
Taqwa suggests avoiding killing if possible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الجامع الصغير عن محمد فيمن اجتمع عليه الصبيان أو المجانين يريدون قتله. وفي الحاوي: أو أخذ ماله ولا يقدر على دفعهم إلا بالقتل - قال: ليس له أن يقتلهم ولو قتل تجب عليه الدية. قال المعلى قلت لمحمد إن صاحبنا يقول بالضمان وعنى أنه أبو مطيع قال المعلى: كنت في الطواف فإذا محمد بن الحسن فقال يا خراساني القول ما قال صاحبكم. قال الشيخ وبه يفتي وكان نصير يقضي بالضمان في الصبي والمجنون والبهيمة إذا قتله الرجل دافعاً. وكان الفقيه أبو بكر يفتي بعدم الضمان. قال الفقيه أبو الليث: هذا القول يخالف ما قيل في الروايات الظاهرة
And in al-Jami' al-Saghir from Muhammad concerning a person upon whom children or insane people gather, intending to kill him. And in al-Hawi: or to take his property, and he is not able to repel them except by killing. He said: It is not permissible for him to kill them, and if he kills, diyah is wajib upon him. Al-Mu'alla said: I said to Muhammad: Our companion (Abu Mut'?) says there is liability (daman). Al-Mu'alla said: I was in the tawaf when Muhammad ibn al-Hasan said: O Khurasani! The correct opinion is what your companion said. The Shaykh said: and according to this he gives fatwa. And Nasir used to rule with liability (daman) concerning a child, an insane person, and an animal if a man kills them in self-defense. And al-Faqih Abu Bakr used to give fatwa of no liability. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: This opinion contradicts what is stated in the apparent narrations.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This passage deals with the difficult situation of being attacked by children or insane persons who cannot legally be held responsible. Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir holds that one cannot kill them even if they intend to kill or take property, and if one does, diyah is wajib. However, there is a reported dispute: Abu Mut' (likely Abu Mut'i al-Balkhi) holds that there is no daman (liability) if acting in defense. Muhammad ibn al-Hasan (Imam Muhammad) reportedly sided with Abu Mut' during a personal encounter, saying 'the correct opinion is what your companion said.' Nevertheless, the shaykh (perhaps the author) says fatwa is given on that view (no liability). Nasir gave judgment with liability. Abu Bakr gave fatwa without liability. Abu al-Layth notes that this no-liability view contradicts the apparent narrations (zahir al-riwayah). So there is intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf. The preferred view according to the shaykh is no daman, but the authoritative texts lean towards liability.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability to pay compensation for harm caused, here diyah or value.
روايات ظاهرة (riwayat zahirah): The apparent or well-known narrations from the founding imams (Zahir al-Riwayah).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Muhammad in al-Jami' al-Saghir, what is the ruling if a person kills children or insane persons who are attacking him?
A) No liability because of self-defense.
B) Diyah is wajib upon him.
C) He must pay diyat but no sin.
D) He is subject to qisas.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school has an internal dispute on liability for killing non-accountable attackers; the later fatwa often exempts daman, but the textual authorities impose diyah.

Killing an Apostate Female Slave: No Liability or Pay Value?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability for killing an apostate female slave

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two Hanafi views on killing an apostate female slave and identify the legal basis for each.

Core Idea
Apostasy justifies execution, but for a slave, the owner's property right may still require compensation.

This short passage from Fatawa al-Dhakhirah records a split opinion within the Hanafi school. Imam Muhammad al-Shaybani holds that no penalty is due because apostasy invalidates the inviolability of life. Other sources, however, require the killer to pay the slave's market value to the owner. Understanding this disagreement clarifies how the Hanafi school balances capital punishment with property rights.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and parties
The case: a man's female slave apostatizes, and another man kills her. Who is the killer? Who is the victim? What is the relationship?
The slave belongs to a third party (the owner), not the killer.
2
State Imam Muhammad's position
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, the killer owes nothing. There is no financial liability and no sin.
This is based on the idea that apostasy makes the blood of the slave permissible, so killing is not a crime.
3
State the alternative position
Other sources say the killer must pay the slave's market value to the owner.
This view respects the owner's property rights, which persist even after apostasy.
4
Analyse the legal rationale
Muhammad's view: apostasy nullifies inviolability of life (dam) and property (mal) simultaneously. The other view: apostasy nullifies dam only, not mal; thus the owner retains a property right until the state executes the slave.
The text does not specify which dalil supports each view; it is implicit.
5
Determine the operative ruling
The text does not give a mufta bihi (relied-upon position). Both views are recorded as valid within the Hanafi school.
This is a known ikhtilaf; a mufti may choose based on context or prefer the view that safeguards property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي فتاوى الذخيرة أمة الرجل إذا ارتدت والعياذ بالله تعالى فقتلها رجل فلا شيء على القاتل؛ هكذا ذكر محمد. وفي غيرها أن على القاتل قيمتها.
And in the Fatawa of al-Dhakhirah: A man's female slave if she apostatizes (we seek refuge with Allah) and a man kills her, then nothing is upon the killer; thus Muhammad mentioned. And in other (works) that upon the killer is her value.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This short passage presents a split opinion. According to Muhammad al-Shaybani as reported in Fatawa al-Dhakhirah, killing an apostate female slave incurs no liability (no diyah and no value) because apostasy is a capital offense and the slave's life is forfeit. However, other sources hold the killer must pay her market value to her owner, presumably because the owner's property right persists until actual execution by the state. The Hanafi school generally holds that apostasy nullifies the inviolability of blood, but for a slave, the property right may remain. The preferred view is not settled here, but both positions are recorded.

Key Terms
ارتداد (irtidad): Apostasy from Islam, which in Hanafi law makes the person liable to execution if not repenting.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) The killer must pay her value.
B) The killer must pay diyah.
C) Nothing is upon the killer.
D) The killer is executed for murder.

Key Takeaway
When a slave apostatizes, killing her may incur no liability (Muhammad) or require paying the owner her value (other Hanafi sources).

Liability of the Slanderer (Sa'i) Who Causes Loss
Ruling on slanderer's liability with conditions and dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three scenarios of slander (sa'i) and determine liability (daman) according to the Hanafi school's preferred view.

Core Idea
A slanderer who causes loss is liable unless his report is true and necessary to prevent harm.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq presents the Hanafi school's detailed position on financial liability (daman) for a slanderer (sa'i) who causes another to be fined or lose property at the hands of the sultan. The ruling is not uniform; it depends on the nature of the report, the sultan's character, and the intention. Over time, Hanafi scholars refined the position, with later masters preferring liability to deter mischief while preserving exceptions for genuine public good.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its legal category
The act is sa'i (slander/reporting) that leads to loss for the victim. The central question is whether the slanderer must compensate the victim (daman). Determine if the act is direct or indirect causation.
Daman requires both harm and causation (tasbib).
2
Analyze the three scenarios from Fatawa al-Khulasah
The text classifies slander into three types: (1) slander with a right (haqq), e.g., to stop oppression – no liability; (2) false claim of treasure/luqtah – liable unless the sultan is just and does not act; (3) false accusation of adultery based on suspicion – differing views among the Imams.
The third scenario is the most disputed.
3
Examine the intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf
Zufar holds general liability. In the third scenario, Abu Yusuf and (in one narration) Muhammad say no liability; Muhammad (in the other narration) says yes. Sadr al-Islam and later scholars prefer Muhammad's view due to widespread slander.
The dispute hinges on whether the slanderer acted with malicious intent or negligence.
4
Assess the tarjih (preferred view)
Later shaykhs like Ali al-Sughdi and Abd al-Rahman gave fatwa of liability. Al-Sadr al-Shahid chose this as 'asahh (most correct) because of the prevalence of harm (qalabat al-sa'ayah). This is the mufta bihi.
The tarjih is based on istihsan and maslahah.
5
Apply to specific rulings
The passage gives further rulings: truthful report about a bow/slave-girl leads to liability if the sultan takes; a slave slanderer is pursued after emancipation; truthful report about a high purchase price is not liable, but lying is liable.
These illustrate the principle that false slander with bad intention triggers daman.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي النسفية سئل عمن سعى فيه إلى السلطان وأخذ من الرجل مالاً ظلماً هل يضمن للساعي؟ قال: نعم. وروي هذا عن زفر وأخذ به كثير من مشايخنا لما فيه من المصلحة. فتاوى الخلاصة: من سعى برجل إلى سلطان حتى غرمه لا يخلو من ثلاثة أوجه: أحدها إن كانت السعاية بحق بأن كان يؤذيه ولا يمكنه دفع الأذى إلا بالرفع إلى السلطان أو كان فاسقاً لا يمتنع عن الفسق بالأمر بالمعروف وفي مثل هذا لا يضمن الساعي. الثاني أن يقول إن فلاناً وجد كنزاً أو لقطة وظهر أنه كاذب ضمن إلا إذا كان السلطان عادلاً لا بمثل هذه السعايات أو قدم يغرم وقد لا يغرم لا يضمن الساعي الثالث إذا وقع في قلبه أن فلاناً إلى امرأته فرفع إلى السلطان فغرمه السلطان ثم ظهر كذبه فعندهما لا يضمن الساعي، وعند محمد يضمن. وقال صدر الإسلام في كتاب اللقطة والفتوى على قول محمد لغلبة السعاية في زماننا وقيل سواء قال صدقا أو كذباً إن لم يكن محتسباً وليس للسلطان حق الأخذ على قياس قول محمد إذا أمر الأعوان بأخذ المال باعتبار الظاهر لا يجب واعتبار السعاية يجب، أما إذا لم يأمر الأعوان ولكن أراه بيته وأخذ من بيته شيئاً لا يضمن. وقال الشيخ الإمام: لا يضمن الجاني مطلقاً. قال الفقيه أبو الليث: الساعي لا يضمن أيضاً، والمشايخ المتأخرون منهم القاضي الإمام علي السغدي والحاكم عبد الرحمن وغيرهما أفتوا بوجوب الضمان على الساعي؛ هكذا اختار الصدر الشهيد وهو أصح ولو قال عند السلطان إن لفلان قوساً جيداً أو جارية حسناء والسلطان يأخذ فأخذ يضمن ولو كان الساعي عبداً يطلب بعد العتق، ولو اشترى شيئاً فقيل له اشتريت بثمن غال فسعى عند ظالم وأخذه إن كان قال صدقا لا يضمن. وإن كان كذباً يضمن.
And in al-Nasafiyyah: He was asked about one who slandered (sa'a) a man to the sultan, causing him to be unjustly fined: is he liable (daman) to the slanderer? He said: Yes. And this was narrated from Zufar, and many of our shaykhs adopted it due to the public interest (maslahah). In Fatawa al-Khulasah: Whoever slanders a man to a sultan until he is fined does not escape three aspects: One: if the slander is with right, such as he was harming him and he cannot repel the harm except by raising it to the sultan, or he was a transgressor (fasiq) who does not desist from transgression through enjoining good, then in such case the slanderer is not liable. Second: if he says 'so-and-so found a treasure or a lost item' and it becomes clear he is lying, he is liable, unless the sultan is just and does not act on such slanders, or if he testifies (?) and the victim is fined or not fined, the slanderer is not liable. Third: if he suspects in his heart that so-and-so (is involved) with his wife and raises it to the sultan, and the sultan fines him, then it becomes clear he lied: according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) the slanderer is not liable, but according to Muhammad he is liable. And Sadr al-Islam said in Kitab al-Luqatah: the fatwa is according to Muhammad's view due to prevalence of slander in our time. And it is said: whether he speaks truth or lying, if he is not a person of account (muhtasib), and the sultan has no right to take, by analogy to Muhammad's view when he orders officials to take money based on apparent, it is not obligatory but based on slander it is obligatory. But if he did not order officials but showed his house and took something from his house, he is not liable. And al-Shaykh al-Imam said: the offender is absolutely not liable. Al-Faqih Abu al-Layth said: the slanderer is also not liable. And the later shaykhs, among them al-Qadi Imam Ali al-Sughdi and al-Hakim Abd al-Rahman and others, gave fatwa of liability on the slanderer; thus al-Sadr al-Shahid chose it, and it is most correct. And if he says to the sultan 'so-and-so has a good bow or a beautiful slave-girl' and the sultan takes it, then he is liable. And if the slanderer is a slave, he is pursued after emancipation. And if he bought something and it was said to him 'you bought at a high price' and he slandered to a tyrant and it was taken: if he told truth, he is not liable; if he lied, he is liable.
Source pages p. 108

Commentary
This extensive passage deals with the liability (daman) of a slanderer (sa'i) who causes another to be fined or have property taken by the sultan. It presents several opinions and scenarios. The earlier view (from Zufar) held him liable. The detailed classification from Fatawa al-Khulasah lists three categories: (1) if the slander is true and necessary to stop harm, no liability; (2) if he falsely claims treasure/luqatah, liable unless the sultan is just and won't act; (3) if he falsely accuses of adultery based on suspicion, according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad?) no liability, but Muhammad holds liable. Sadr al-Islam preferred Muhammad's view due to prevalent mischief. Later scholars like Ali al-Sughdi and Abd al-Rahman also favored liability. The passage ends with specific applications: if the slander leads to confiscation of goods, liable; if the slanderer is a slave, payment can be demanded after freedom; if he truthfully reports a purchase at high price, no liability; if lying, liable. The overall trend is towards imposing liability to prevent harm, but there is a minority view of no liability.

Key Terms
ساعي (sa'i): One who informs on another to the authorities (slanderer), causing harm.
ضمان (daman): Financial liability, here the duty to compensate the victim for loss caused by slander.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following scenarios does NOT impose liability on the slanderer according to the preferred Hanafi view?
A) A man falsely claims his neighbor found a treasure, and the tyrant confiscates it.
B) A man truthfully reports his neighbor's wrongdoing to a just sultan, and the sultan fines the neighbor.
C) A man falsely accuses another of adultery based on suspicion, and the tyrant fines the accused.
D) A man tells a tyrant about his rival's valuable property, and the tyrant seizes it.

Key Takeaway
The later Hanafi position holds the slanderer liable for damages he causes, except when his report is both truthful and necessary to prevent harm.

Master's Liability for Harming His Own Slave
Ruling on master injuring or killing his own slave.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling for a master injuring or killing his own slave and explain why tazir applies instead of qisas or diyah.

Core Idea
A master who harms his slave faces tazir, not qisas, because the slave is property but the master is accountable.

This lesson examines a specific ruling from Abu Nasr al-Dabusi recorded in al-Jami' al-Saghir. It clarifies that a master's authority over his slave is not absolute; causing harm triggers a discretionary punishment (tazir). Understanding this ruling helps delineate the limits of ownership in Islamic law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act is a master cutting off his slave's hand or killing him. The ruling is that tazir (discretionary punishment) is wajib (obligatory) upon the master.
The ruling is from al-Dabusi, a later Hanafi authority.
2
Understand the rationale
Since the slave is property, there is no qisas (retaliation) between a master and his slave. However, the master cannot cause wanton harm; tazir serves as a deterrent and punishment for transgression.
This distinguishes it from harming a free person, where qisas or diyah applies.
3
Note the authority and weight
The source is al-Jami' al-Saghir quoting Abu Nasr al-Dabusi, a later Hanafi jurist. This ruling belongs to the category of fatawa mutakhkhirin (later fatwas) and is the mufta bihi (operative position).
No intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf is recorded here.
4
Recognize cross-madhhab differences
Some other schools (e.g., Shafi'i) may allow qisas if the injury is excessive, arguing that the slave's humanity prevails over property status. The Hanafi position emphasizes the proprietary aspect but deters through tazir.
This is a legitimate area of ikhtilaf.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال في الجامع الصغير: قال أبو نصر الدبوسي فيمن قطع يد عبده أو قتله إن عليه التعزير
And it is said in al-Jami' al-Saghir: Abu Nasr al-Dabusi said concerning one who cuts off his slave's hand or kills him: upon him is tazir (discretionary punishment).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This short ruling from Abu Nasr al-Dabusi in al-Jami' al-Saghir addresses the master's liability for injuring or killing his own slave. Since the slave is property, there is no qisas (retaliation) for a limb or life; instead, the master faces tazir, a discretionary punishment imposed by the judge. This is because the master has a right over the slave but cannot cause wanton harm; the tazir serves as a deterrent and punishment for transgression.

Key Terms
تعزير (tazir): Discretionary punishment set by the judge for offenses that do not have fixed hadd penalties.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

B) Diyah
C) Tazir
D) No punishment

Key Takeaway
A master who injures or kills his own slave must receive tazir, as the slave is property but the master cannot harm with impunity.

Liability for Defensive Cuts at the Joint
Ruling on liability for defensive finger cuts

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for injuries inflicted during self-defense when the victim seizes the attacker's weapon, distinguishing between cuts at the joint and cuts not at the joint.

Core Idea
In defensive grabbing of an attacker's sword, qisas applies only if the cut is at the joint; otherwise, diyah suffices.

This lesson examines a specific scenario from al-Muntqa where a person intending to strike another with a sword has his fingers cut when the victim seizes the sword. The Hanafi ruling hinges on whether the cut occurs at the joint (mafsal) or elsewhere, reflecting the principle of proportionality in retaliation. Understanding this distinction is crucial for applying qisas and diyah correctly in defensive acts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario and parties
Read the passage carefully: a man (attacker) intends to strike another (victim) with a sword. The victim grabs the sword and cuts the attacker's fingers. Determine who is the aggressor and who is defending.
The text refers to 'al-sayyid' possibly meaning the victim; ensure clarity.
2
Distinguish the two cases based on cut location
The ruling differentiates between cutting at the joint (mafsal) and cutting not at the joint. Identify these two categories.
Cutting at the joint implies complete severance of a finger; not at the joint means a wound or partial cut.
3
State the ruling for each case
For a cut at the joint: qisas (retaliation) is due. For a cut not at the joint: diyah (blood money) is due. Memorize these outcomes.
Qisas here means the attacker can demand similar cutting of the victim's fingers at the joint, subject to conditions.
4
Understand the legal basis
The distinction is based on proportionality: cutting at the joint is considered a complete, deliberate injury equivalent to the attacker's intent, warranting qisas. A lesser injury only warrants financial compensation.
This aligns with the Hanafi principle that qisas is for exact retaliation when the injury is precisely measured.
5
Note the epistemological weight of the source
This ruling comes from al-Muntqa, a later selection (nawadir) by Imam Muhammad. While not from the six core Zahir al-Riwayah texts, it is still authoritative in the Hanafi school.
The ruling is mukhtalaf fih (disputed) – other schools may treat all defensive injuries as non-qisas.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى عن محمد قال في رجل قصد أن يضرب آخر بالسيف فأخذ المضروب السيف من يده فقطع السيد أصابع الآخر قال: إن كان من غير المفصل فعلى الجاذب الدية، وإن كان من المفصل فعليه القصاص.
And in al-Muntqa from Muhammad: He said concerning a man who intended to strike another with a sword, and the one being struck took the sword from his hand and cut the (attacker's?) fingers (of the other? or was the victim cut?). The text says 'the lord (al-sayyid?) cut the fingers of the other' but careful: 'qata'a al-sayyid asabi'a al-akhar' possibly means the victim cut the attacker's fingers. Muhammad said: If it (the cut) is not at the joint, then upon the one who pulled (the sword) is the diyah; if it is at the joint, then upon him is qisas.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage from al-Muntqa deals with a scenario where a person intends to strike another with a sword, but the intended victim seizes the sword and in the process cuts the fingers of the attacker (or perhaps the victim's own fingers? The text is ambiguous but likely the attacker's fingers as he is the sayyid? Actually 'al-sayyid' might be a copy error for 'al-masud'? Or 'al-sayyid' means the victim? Better to read as: 'qata'a asabi'a al-akhar' meaning the victim cut the other's (the attacker's) fingers). The ruling distinguishes between cutting at the joint (mafsal) and not at the joint. Cutting at the joint (e.g., finger completely severed) triggers qisas (retaliation) because it is a deliberate injury equivalent to the attacker's original intent. Cutting not at the joint (e.g., a wound) only requires diyah, as it is less severe. This shows the principle of proportionality in self-defense and retaliation.

Key Terms
مفصل (mafsal): Joint, specifically the knuckle joint where fingers are severed.
قصاص (qisas): Retaliatory punishment, like for like, in homicides and injuries.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) When the cut is at the joint.
B) When the cut is not at the joint.
C) Always, because it is self-defense.
D) Never, because the attacker initiated.

Key Takeaway
Hanafi law imposes qisas for defensive cuts at the joint, and diyah for cuts not at the joint, reflecting proportionality over blanket self-defense immunity.

Murder Case: Wife Pardons, One Son Kills the Killer
Ruling on a son who kills his father's murderer after the mother's pardon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the diyah liability and offset when a legal heir kills the murderer after a partial pardon, applying Hanafi inheritance rules.

Core Idea
A pardon does not permit retaliation; killing after knowledge of pardon incurs diyah, offset by the killer's inheritance claims.

This case from al-Muntqa illustrates the interplay between qisas, diyah, and inheritance when a legal heir kills the murderer after a partial pardon. Understanding this scenario requires careful application of Hanafi inheritance shares and liability offsetting.

Guided Path
1
Identify all parties and their legal relationships
List the original victim, his heirs (two sons and a wife), and the original killer. Note that the wife pardons the blood (qisas).
The pardon extinguishes the right to qisas for all heirs, but the right to diyah remains.
2
Determine the effect of the wife's pardon
Under Hanafi law, the pardon by one heir (the wife) eliminates the possibility of qisas for the killer. The other heirs (the sons) can still claim diyah from the killer.
Diyah is a financial penalty, not a blood punishment.
3
Analyze the son's act of killing the killer
The son, knowing of the pardon, kills the killer. This is a new, unlawful killing because the original qisas is no longer valid. The son becomes liable for diyah for the killer's death.
The son's act is not justified; it is a separate tort.
4
Calculate the diyah liability and payment terms
The son must pay diyah from his own wealth over three years (standard for quasi-intentional killing). The amount is the full diyah for the killer.
Payment in installments over three years is typical for cases where the killing is not strictly intentional (shibh amd).
5
Determine the offset from the son's claim against the killer
The son, as an heir of the original victim, has a claim for a share of the diyah that the original killer owed. This claim is deducted from the diyah the son must pay for killing the killer.
The offset prevents double payment: the son pays net (diyah for killer minus his share of original diyah).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى: رجل قتل عمداً وله ابنان وامرأة فعفت المرأة عن الدم ثم إن أحد الابنين قتل القاتل وهو يعلم العفو فعليه الدية في ماله في ثلاث سنين يدفع عنه من ذلك ما كان له على قاتل الأب
And in al-Muntqa: A man was killed intentionally, and he has two sons and a wife. The woman (wife) pardoned the blood (i.e., waived qisas). Then one of the two sons killed the killer while knowing of the pardon. So upon him (the son) is the diyah from his own wealth over three years, and what he had (as a claim) against the killer of his father is deducted from that (i.e., the son inherits from the killer? The text says 'yudfa' 'anhu min dhalika ma kana lahu 'ala qatil al-ab' meaning the son's share of inheritance from the killer (since the killer died) is used to pay his diyah).
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage presents a homicide case with a twist. The victim has two sons and a wife. The wife pardons the qisas (blood vengeance) against the killer. However, one son, knowing of the pardon, kills the killer. That son is now liable for diyah (blood money) for killing the killer, to be paid from his own property over three years (usual installment for quasi-intentional killing). However, since the killer had been convicted for killing the father, the son inherits from the killer (as the killer's estate goes to his heirs, but here the killer may not have heirs? Actually the killer's estate is subject to the victim's heirs' claims). The text says that what the son had against the father's killer (likely his share of the diyah owed by the killer) is deducted from the diyah the son now owes. This offsets part of the liability. The calculation involves inheritance law and multiple claims.

Key Terms
عفو ('afw): Pardon of qisas by the victim's legal heirs, which is binding on all but the one who kills after knows.
دية (diyah): Blood money, a financial penalty for killing, paid to the victim's heirs.
قسامة (qisas): Retaliation in kind for intentional killing or wounding.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a wife pardons the killer, and one son later kills the killer knowing of the pardon, what is the ruling on the son?
A) No liability because he was avenging his father.
B) He must pay diyah in three years, with deduction of his inheritance share from the killer.
C) He is subject to qisas for killing the killer.
D) He must pay full diyah immediately.

Key Takeaway
A pardon by one heir extinguishes qisas for all; killing the killer after knowledge of the pardon incurs diyah, offset by the killer's claim for his share of the original diyah.

Interplay of Qisas and Inheritance When Sons Kill Each Other's Parents
Complex inheritance and qisas scenario where each son kills the other's parent

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi ruling on qisas and inheritance when two sons intentionally kill each other's parents, and identify how inheritance adjustments cause the father's qisas to drop.

Core Idea
When two sons kill each other's parents, the father's qisas drops because the mother's share of it passes to the killer.

This case from Al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a sophisticated scenario where two sons each commit intentional murder of the other's parent. The interplay between qisas (retaliation) and inheritance leads to a surprising outcome: the right to qisas for the father is extinguished. We will untangle the inheritance chain and see why the Hanafi school rules that only the mother's qisas proceeds.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two killings
Son A kills the father intentionally; Son B kills the mother intentionally. Determine who is the killer and victim in each case.
2
Determine initial qisas rights
Upon the father's death, the right to qisas against Son A passes to his heirs: the mother and Son B. For the mother's death, the right against Son B passes to her heirs, including Son A.
In Hanafi law, qisas is an inheritable property right.
3
Apply inheritance rules to the father's estate
The mother inherits 1/8 (thumn) of the father's estate; Son B inherits the rest as asabah. Son A is barred from inheriting from the father because he is the killer.
The killer cannot inherit from his victim.
4
Trace the mother's share of qisas
The mother's share of the father's estate includes her portion of the qisas right. When she is killed, that share passes to her heir, Son A (who is also the father's killer).
Son A inherits from his mother despite being a murderer of her husband.
5
Explain why the father's qisas drops
Because Son A inherits part of the qisas right against himself, that portion is legally impossible to enforce. The remaining portion held by Son B also becomes moot, so the entire qisas for the father falls away necessarily.
The text states: 'the qisas for the father falls away... it is dropped necessarily.'

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما إذا قتل أحدهما أباً عمداً وقتل الآخر أمه عمداً فللأول أن يقتل الثاني بالأم ويسقط القصاص عن الأب لأن القصاص الأول لما قتل صار القصاص موروثاً بين الابن الآخر وبين الأم للأم من الثمن، فإن قتل الآخر الأم صار الثمن الذي ورثته الأم من الأب ميراث الأول فسقط ضرورة.
And if one of them killed the father intentionally and the other killed the mother intentionally, then the first (the son whose mother was killed) may kill the second (for the mother), and the qisas for the father falls away, because when the first qisas (for the father) occurred, the qisas became inherited between the other son and the mother: the mother's share is one-sixth (thumn). Then if the other killed the mother, the one-sixth that the mother inherited from the father becomes inheritance of the first (son), so it is dropped necessarily.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This is a sophisticated case of intertwined qisas and inheritance. Two sons: Son A kills the father intentionally, Son B kills the mother intentionally. Initially, qisas is due on Son A for the father. But before that, the father's estate includes a claim for qisas, which is a financial right? Actually in Hanafi law, qisas is a right of the victim's heirs. After the father's death, the right to qisas passes to the heirs: the mother and the other son (Son B). The mother has a share (e.g., 1/8 of the estate? Actually the text says 'al-thumn' meaning one-eighth? But standard share for wife is 1/8 if there are children). So the mother inherits 1/8 of the father's estate, and Son B inherits the remainder. Then Son B kills the mother. Now the mother's heirs (including Son A) have a right of qisas against Son B. Son A can now kill Son B for the mother. Meanwhile, what happens to the qisas for the father? Since the mother was an heir to the father's qisas, her death and the inheritance of her share by Son A (who is also the killer of the father?) Actually Son A is the killer of the father, so he cannot inherit from the father. But the text says the 'thumn' (1/8) that the mother inherited from the father becomes inheritance of the first (son A) from his mother? That would give Son A the property, but since he is a murderer of the father, he is barred from inheriting from the father, but he can inherit from his mother. The complexity is that the right to qisas gets mixed with inheritance. The upshot: the first son (whose mother was killed by the second) can kill the second for the mother, and the qisas for the father is effectively dropped because of the inheritance adjustments. This shows the interplay of crimes and succession.

Key Terms
قصاص (qisas): Retaliation; here the right of the victim's family to execute the murderer.
ثمن (thumn): One-eighth, the share of a wife in inheritance when the deceased has children.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where one son kills the father and the other kills the mother, what happens to the qisas for the father?
A) It proceeds as normal.
B) It is applied to the son who killed the mother.
C) It falls away because of inheritance adjustments.
D) It is replaced by diyah.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, the right to qisas can be affected by inheritance, leading to the dropping of qisas when the killer inherits part of the right.

Injuries to a Mukatab: Effect of Tadbir, Kitabah, and 'Itq
Rulings on liability for injuries to a mukatab slave when the master later makes tadbir or kitabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on liability when a mukatab is injured and the master later performs tadbir, kitabah, or 'itq, and identify the condition that determines whether the claim is void or actionable.

Core Idea
Tadbir does not void a prior injury claim, but kitabah or 'itq does.

This lesson examines a nuanced ruling from the Hanafi school regarding injuries to a mukatab slave. The key is understanding how subsequent actions by the master affect the liability of the original aggressor. We will parse the distinctions between tadbir, kitabah, and 'itq and their legal effects.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is being ruled upon? Here, it is an injury inflicted on a mukatab by a third party. The ruling concerns whether the claim for compensation remains valid.
2
Note the subsequent action by the master
After the injury, the master performs tadbir (declaration of freedom upon his death), kitabah (contract of manumission), or immediate emancipation ('itq).
3
Determine the effect on the claim
For tadbir: the claim remains actionable (la yardar al-sirayah). For kitabah or 'itq: the claim becomes void (hadarat).
4
Consider the scenario where the mukatab later becomes free and dies
If the mukatab completes his kitabah, becomes free, then dies from the injury, the aggressor pays the value of the slave (as property), not the diyah of a free man.
5
Understand the basis for each ruling
Tadbir does not fully divest ownership; the slave remains property until the master's death. Kitabah and 'itq immediately end the master's property interest. The value at the time of injury governs the compensation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا جنى على مكاتب إنسان ثم دبره مولاه لانهدار السراية بل تكون السراية مضمونة على الجاني بعد التدبير ولو كاتبه أو أعتقه هدرت السراية أيضاً، وإذا جنى على مكاتب إنسان ثم أدى المكاتب فعتق ثم مات المكاتب من تلك الجناية فعلى الجاني قيمة المكاتب لا الدية وإن مات حراً.
And if an injury is inflicted on a mukatab (slave) of a person, then his master makes tadbir (declares the slave free after his death), the injury does not become void; rather, the injury is actionable (daman) against the aggressor after tadbir. And if he makes kitabah (contract of manumission) or emancipates him, the injury becomes void as well. And if an injury is inflicted on a mukatab of a person, then the mukatab pays his kitabah and becomes free, then the mukatab dies from that injury, then upon the aggressor is the value of the mukatab, not the diyah, even if he dies free.
Source pages p. 109

Commentary
This passage discusses how the status of a mukatab (a slave who has a contract of manumission) affects claims for injuries. If a third party injures a mukatab, the liability for the full value (since the slave is property) is due. However, subsequent actions by the master can change this. If the master makes tadbir (promising freedom upon his death), the injury claim remains valid and the aggressor must pay compensation (the value) even after the slave becomes free upon the master's death. But if the master instead enters into a new kitabah contract or simply frees the slave ('itq), the injury claim is cancelled (hadar). This is because kitabah and 'itq fully divest the master's property interest, while tadbir leaves ownership until death. In the second scenario, if the mukatab completes his payments (ada) and becomes free, but then dies from the earlier injury, the aggressor pays the value of the slave (as if he were a slave), not the diyah of a free man, even though he died free. This is because the injury occurred while he was still a slave, and the claim is based on property value.

Key Terms
مكاتب (mukatab): A slave who has a written contract (kitabah) to earn his freedom by paying installments.
تدبير (tadbir): A declaration by the master that the slave will be free upon the master's death.
سراية (sirayah): The progression of injury; here refers to the legal effect of the injury.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mukatab is injured, and then the master makes tadbir, what happens to the claim against the aggressor?
A) The claim becomes void.
B) The claim remains actionable against the aggressor.
C) The aggressor is now liable for diyah of a free man.
D) The master must pay compensation.

Key Takeaway
The effect of subsequent manumission on prior injury claims depends on the type of manumission: tadbir does not void, while kitabah and 'itq do.

Accepting Testimony in Qisas: Conflicting Witnesses on Different Sons
Ruling on conflicting witness testimonies in qisas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when two sets of witnesses accuse a defendant of killing different sons of the same father and identify the condition of witness credibility (tazkiyah) as decisive.

Core Idea
When two sets name different victims, credible testimony alone leads to qisas.

This passage from al-Muntqa presents a nuanced scenario in qisas testimony. Two groups of witnesses claim the accused killed two different sons of the same father. The first group is verified as credible (tazkiyah), the second is not. The ruling determines that the credible testimony suffices for the father to claim qisas. This highlights the central role of witness credibility in proving homicide in the Hanafi school.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and claims
The plaintiff is the father of two sons. Two sets of witnesses each claim the defendant killed a different son. The first set is credible, the second is not.
The text specifies that the first group received tazkiyah (credibility verification).
2
Determine the effect of tazkiyah
Credible testimony is legally valid to prove its claim. The second set is not credible, so their testimony is disregarded.
Tazkiyah is a prerequisite for accepting testimony in hudud and qisas cases.
3
Derive the ruling
The credible testimony establishes the murder of one son, so the defendant is handed over to the father for qisas for that son.
The text implies that only the credible testimony matters; contradictory testimony does not automatically invalidate.
4
Note the opposite school view
Some scholars require unanimous agreement on the same victim; the Hanafi school accepts any credible testimony.
This is a point of ikhtilaf; the Hanafi position is based on the strength of individual credible testimony.

Source Text

وقال في المنتقى رجل شهد له رجلان أنه قتل ابن هذا فلان وشهد آخران لهذا الرجل أيضاً أنه قتل ابن هذا فلاناً وسميا ابناً آخر له غير الذي سمياه الأولان وزكى الفريق الأول ولم يزك الفريق الثاني فدفع المشهود عليه على المشهود له ليقتله فقال ذلك

The first set is credible (tazkiyah applied), the second not. The text ends abruptly, but the implied ruling is that only the credible testimony matters, leading to qisas. This underscores the importance of witness credibility in qisas cases.

Key Terms

Permissibility and Right to Object to Structures in Public Roads
Detailed fiqh ruling with proof and intra-Hanafi dispute

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi conditions for placing structures in a public road and compare the positions of Abu Hanifa, Abu Yusuf, and Muhammad regarding who may object.

Core Idea
A structure in a public road is permissible if harmless, but who may object is disputed among the Hanafi founders.

This lesson addresses the first two of three issues in the chapter: permissibility of placing structures like latrines, spouts, and beams in public roads, and the right to object. The key proof is the prophetic hadith 'No harm and no reciprocating harm.' We will also explore an intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether any individual can prevent placement without harm.

Guided Path
1
Identify the structures and the ruling on creation
List the types of structures: kunīf (latrine), mīzāb (spout), jurṣun (projecting beam), dukkān (shop). Note that the ruling on creation depends on whether they cause harm.
The passage also analogizes to a debtor: no delay if creditor demands, otherwise permissible.
2
Apply the harm principle using the hadith
Use the hadith 'Lā ḍarar wa lā ḍirār' to determine permissibility. If the structure harms passersby, it is haram; if harmless due to road width, it is mubah as long as no one objects.
The hadith is the primary textual proof (dalīl naqlī).
3
Examine the dispute on objection rights
Abū Ḥanīfah: any person can prevent placement and demand removal if done without imam's permission, even if harmless. Abū Yūsuf agrees. Muḥammad: no one may object before or after if harmless, because the Sharī‘ah itself permits it.
The response: using the road for unintended purpose (building) allows objection unlike passing.
4
Distinguish through roads (nāfidh) from dead-ends (ghayr nāfidh)
In a through road, no one owns it, so benefit is allowed as long as no harm. In a dead-end, residents own it collectively; any construction requires unanimous permission, whether harmful or not.
The distinction affects who can object and under what conditions.
5
Note the principle for old structures
Remove only if known to be recent; if unknown, they are presumed old and cannot be removed. This is from al-Muntaqā.
Applies to all structures in public roads.

Source Text

(1) رواه ابن ماجه في كتاب الأحكام باب ۱۷ الموطأ في كتاب الأقضية حديث ٣١. أحمد في مسنده (٥/ .(۳۲۷
Chapter on what a man causes in the road. When he finished with the rulings of direct killing, he began to explain its rulings by indirect causation, and he gave precedence to the first because it is the original, as it is killing without intermediary, but it occurs more frequently, so there was a greater need to know its rulings. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (Whoever puts out into the public road a toilet, or a spout, or a projecting beam, or a shop, then everyone has the right to demolish it) meaning every person who passes by, whether Muslim adult sane free or dhimmī, because each of them and their animals pass, so they have the right to object themselves as in common property, unlike slaves and minors under interdiction, where they are not ordered to demolish by their demand because the objection of those under interdiction is not considered regarding their property, unlike the dhimmī. This is if he built for himself. It is restricted as mentioned to avoid what is built for the Muslims like a mosque and the like, which is not demolished; as transmitted from Muhammad (may Allah have mercy on him). Isma‘īl al-Ṣaffār said: It is only demolished by his complaint if he does not have the like of it; if he has the like, his complaint is not heeded because if he wanted to remove harm from people, he would start with himself, and when he does not remove what is in his power, it is known he is obstinate. Al-‘Ināyah said: Al-kunīf is the latrine, al-mīzāb is the spout, and al-jurṣun is said to be the tower. Fakhr al-Islām said: It is a beam that a person puts out from the wall to build upon. Then the discussion on this issue is in three places: One: whether it is permissible for him to create it in the road or not. Second: objection to prevent him from creating it and raising it after. Third: liability for what is destroyed by these things. As for creation, Shams al-A’immah said: If the creation harms the road users, then he may not create it; if it does not harm anyone due to the width of the road, it is permissible for him to create it as long as no one prevents him, because benefiting from the road without harming anyone is permissible like it, so it is attached to it if he needs it. If it harms the passerby, it is not permissible due to his saying (peace be upon him): 'No harm and no reciprocating harm in Islam' (1). This is analogous to a debtor: he may not delay when the creditor demands, but if he does not demand, he may delay. Similarly, sitting in the road for selling and buying is permissible if it does not harm anyone, and if it harms, it is not permissible due to what we said. As for objection, Abū Ḥanīfah said: Every person from among the people can prevent him from placing and can compel him to remove after placing, whether there is harm or not, if placed without the Imām's permission, because it usurps his authority, as the management of public affairs is the Imām's. Al-‘urḍ (with ḍamm) means side, and the intended meaning is one of the people. According to Abū Yūsuf, everyone can prevent him from that. According to Muḥammad, no one can prevent him before or after placement if there is no harm to the people, because he is permitted by the Law to create it; do you not see that it is permissible for him if no one prevents? And the one who prevents could be prevented, so it becomes as if the Imām permitted him, nay even more so because the Law's permission is more authoritative and stronger. The response is that this is using the road for something it was not placed for, so it is like passing, so they can prevent him even if it is permissible in itself, unlike passing in it because that is using it for what it was placed for. (And he has the right to act in the through road unless it harms) meaning he may create the projecting beam and other previously mentioned things in the through road if it does not harm the public; meaning if no one prevents him, and we have mentioned the disagreement about it, so we will not repeat it. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (In other than it he does not act except with their permission) meaning in non-through roads no one may act to create what we mentioned except with the permission of its residents, because the non-through road is owned by its residents, so they are partners in it, and for this reason they are entitled to pre-emption. Acting in common property in a way it was not placed for is not permissible except with everyone's permission, whether harm or no harm, unlike the through road because no one owns it, so benefiting is permissible as long as no harm. And because if it is a public right, obtaining everyone's permission is difficult, so each person is considered as if he alone is the owner regarding benefit. It is not so for the non-through road because reaching their consent is possible, so it remains on partnership in reality and ruling. In al-Muntaqā: One is only ordered to remove these things if their occurrence is known; if they are old, no one has the right to remove, and if the state of these things is unknown, they are considered old. This is the principle. He said (may Allah have mercy on him): (If someone dies from its fall, his blood money is on his ‘āqilah, just as if he dug a well in the road or placed a stone and a person was killed by it) meaning if a person dies from the fall of what was mentioned, such as a toilet, spout, or projecting beam, his blood money is on the ‘āqilah of the one who put it in the road, because he caused the destruction transgressively by creating what harms passersby by occupying the road's airspace or by creating an obstruction between them and the road. Likewise if someone trips. (1) Narrated by Ibn Mājah in Kitāb al-Aḥkām, Bāb 17; al-Muwaṭṭa’ in Kitāb al-Aqḍiyah, Ḥadīth 31; Aḥmad in his Musnad (5/327).
Source pages p. 110

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq is a commentary (sharḥ) on Kanz al-Daqā’iq, specifically the chapter on what a man causes on the road. It deals with a common scenario: a person places a structure such as a latrine, spout, projecting beam, or shop into a public road. The author first distinguishes between direct and indirect killing; he now addresses indirect causation (tasabbub). The passage is structured around three issues: (1) the permissibility of creating such structures, (2) who can object and demand removal, and (3) liability if harm results.

On permissibility: Shams al-A’immah states that if the structure harms passersby, it is absolutely prohibited (ḥarām) based on the prophetic hadith 'No harm and no reciprocating harm in Islam' (lā ḍarar wa lā ḍirār fī al-islām). If it causes no harm due to the road's width, it is permissible provided no one objects. This is analogous to a debtor: if the creditor demands payment, delay is not permissible; otherwise it is. Similarly, sitting in the road for trade is permissible if harmless.

The second issue—who may object—is a point of intra-Hanafī dispute. Abū Ḥanīfah holds that any individual (from the general public) can prevent placement and demand removal if the structure was erected without the imam's permission, regardless of whether it causes harm. Abū Yūsuf agrees that anyone can prevent. Muḥammad al-Shaybānī, however, argues that if the structure causes no harm, no one may object either before or after placement, because the Sharī‘ah itself permits it. The author then gives the Hanafī response: using the road for something other than its purpose (like building) is akin to passing; even if the act is intrinsically permissible, others may object—unlike mere passing, which is using it for its intended purpose. The practical difference is that under Abū Ḥanīfah and Abū Yūsuf, a harmless structure can still be removed if placed without the imam's consent, while under Muḥammad it remains unless harmful.

The third issue is liability: if a person dies from the fall of such a structure, the blood money (diyah) is on the ‘āqilah (the male paternal relatives) of the one who placed it, because he transgressed by obstructing the road. This is analogized to digging a well or placing a stone in the road. The ‘āqilah pays because the act was indirect and unintentional (shibh ‘amd or khaṭa’).

The passage also distinguishes between through roads (nāfidh) and dead-ends (ghayr nāfidh). In a dead-end, the road is owned collectively by the residents; thus any construction requires the permission of all owners, whether harmful or not. In a through road, no one owns it, so benefit is allowed as long as no harm. The author concludes with a principle from al-Muntaqā: old structures are not to be removed unless their newness is proven; if unknown, they are presumed old.

This lesson demonstrates the Hanafī method of integrating textual proof (the hadith), qiyās (analogy to debts and passing), and consideration of public interest (istiḥsān) within a structured framework of rules for public spaces.

Key Terms
كنيف (kunīf): A latrine or toilet placed in the road.
ميزاب (mīzāb): A spout or gutter that projects from a wall into the road to drain water.
جرصن (jurṣun): A projecting beam or structure that extends from a wall into the road, used for building upon.
نافذ (nāfidh): A through road that is open at both ends and belongs to the public.
غير نافذ (ghayr nāfidh): A dead-end road that is owned collectively by the residents who have access to it.
لا ضرر ولا ضرار (lā ḍarar wa lā ḍirār): A prophetic principle that prohibits causing harm or reciprocating harm.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Abū Ḥanīfah, what condition is necessary for any person to object to a structure placed in a public road?
A) The structure must cause actual harm.
B) The structure must have been placed without the imam's permission.
C) The objector must be a resident of that road.
D) The structure must be a latrine (kunīf).

Key Takeaway
In a through road, a harmless structure is permissible unless the imam's permission is absent, in which case any passerby may object under the majority Hanafi view.

Diyat — Blood Money
advanced
7 min
Liability for Death Caused by Road Structures and Dead-End Road Rules
Detailed fiqh ruling on liability with qiyas and road type distinction

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine the liability for death caused by a road structure and identify the rules for construction in dead-end roads.

Core Idea
If a road structure kills someone, the blood money is on the builder's 'āqilah, not on his personal wealth.

This lesson covers the third issue from the chapter: liability (diyat) when a structure in the road causes death. The author uses qiyas to a well or stone. We also revisit the distinction between through and dead-end roads regarding permission, which was introduced in the previous lesson. The key principle: the 'āqilah pays for indirect harm.

Guided Path
1
Recognize the liability trigger
Death must result from the fall or collapse of a structure (toilet, spout, beam, shop) placed in the road.
The trigger is the act of placing the structure in a way that creates a hazard.
2
Identify the liable party
The blood money (diyah) is on the 'āqilah (male paternal relatives) of the person who placed the structure, not on the builder's own wealth.
This is because the act is indirect (tasabbub) and unintentional.
3
Understand the qiyas to digging a well
The author analogizes to someone who digs a well or places a stone in the road, causing death. The ‘āqilah pays in all these cases.
The effective cause (illah) is creating an obstruction or hazard in the public road.
4
Distinguish through vs dead-end roads for permission
In a through road, permission is not needed from the public; in a dead-end, unanimous consent of residents is required.
Liability applies regardless of permission; it is based on the act of placing the structure, not on authorization.
5
Apply the presumption of oldness
If the structure is old and its origin unknown, it cannot be removed by objection, but liability for death still applies if it falls.
Liability and removability are separate issues.

Chapter on what a man causes in the road. Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Rā’iq is a commentary (sharḥ) on Kanz al-Daqā’iq, specifically the chapter on what a man causes on the road. It deals with a common scenario: a person places a structure such as a latrine, spout, projecting beam, or shop into a public road. The author first distinguishes between direct and indirect killing; he now addresses indirect causation (tasabbub). The passage is structured around three issues: (1) the permissibility of creating such structures, (2) who can object and demand removal, and (3) liability if harm results.

On permissibility: Shams al-A’immah states that if the structure harms passersby, it is absolutely prohibited (ḥarām) based on the prophetic hadith 'No harm and no reciprocating harm in Islam' (lā ḍarar wa lā ḍirār fī al-islām). If it causes no harm due to the road's width, it is permissible provided no one objects. This is analogous to a debtor: if the creditor demands payment, delay is not permissible; otherwise it is. Similarly, sitting in the road for trade is permissible if harmless.

The second issue—who may object—is a point of intra-Hanafī dispute. Abū Ḥanīfah holds that any individual (from the general public) can prevent placement and demand removal if the structure was erected without the imam's permission, regardless of whether it causes harm. Abū Yūsuf agrees that anyone can prevent. Muḥammad al-Shaybānī, however, argues that if the structure causes no harm, no one may object either before or after placement, because the Sharī‘ah itself permits it. The author then gives the Hanafī response: using the road for something other than its purpose (like building) is akin to passing; even if the act is intrinsically permissible, others may object—unlike mere passing, which is using it for its intended purpose. The practical difference is that under Abū Ḥanīfah and Abū Yūsuf, a harmless structure can still be removed if placed without the imam's consent, while under Muḥammad it remains unless harmful.

The third issue is liability: if a person dies from the fall of such a structure, the blood money (diyah) is on the ‘āqilah (the male paternal relatives) of the one who placed it, because he transgressed by obstructing the road. This is analogized to digging a well or placing a stone in the road. The ‘āqilah pays because the act was indirect and unintentional (shibh ‘amd or khaṭa’).

The passage also distinguishes between through roads (nāfidh) and dead-ends (ghayr nāfidh). In a dead-end, the road is owned collectively by the residents; thus any construction requires the permission of all owners, whether harmful or not. In a through road, no one owns it, so benefit is allowed as long as no harm. The author concludes with a principle from al-Muntaqā: old structures are not to be removed unless their newness is proven; if unknown, they are presumed old.

This lesson demonstrates the Hanafī method of integrating textual proof (the hadith), qiyās (analogy to debts and passing), and consideration of public interest (istiḥsān) within a structured framework of rules for public spaces.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʿāqilah): The male paternal relatives (agnates) who are responsible for paying blood money (diyah) on behalf of a person who caused death indirectly.
تسبب (tasabbub): Indirect causation; causing something through an intermediary act.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, who pays the blood money if a person dies from the fall of a beam placed in a public road?
A) The person who placed the beam pays from his own wealth.
B) The 'āqilah of the person who placed the beam pays.
C) The state pays because the road is public.
D) No blood money is due because it was an accident.

Key Takeaway
Liability for death caused by a road structure falls on the 'āqilah of the person who placed it, not on the builder's personal wealth, and this rule applies regardless of permission or age of the structure.
