Liability for Falling Structures in the Road
Ruling on blood-money liability when a placed structure falls and kills someone

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability for a falling structure in the road, identify the conditions for liability on the 'aqila, and apply the istihsan rule when the victim's location is uncertain.

Core Idea
Blood-money for a falling structure falls on the 'aqila if the placement was transgressive; doubt leads to half liability via istihsan.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq builds on the matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, expanding the ruling on liability for structures placed in public roads that subsequently fall and cause death. The author uses analogical reasoning (qiyas) to establish the standard rule, then introduces an important istihsan exception when the victim's location is uncertain. Understanding this ruling sharpens your ability to distinguish between direct and indirect causation, and to see how Hanafi jurists balance strict analogy with equitable outcomes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the liability rule
The act is placing a structure (like a waterspout, privy, or projection) in the road that falls and kills someone. The ruling is that the blood-money (diyah) is on the 'aqila (paternal relatives) of the person who placed it.
The 'aqila pays over three years.
2
Understand the condition for liability
The placement must be transgressive (without right). If the structure was on private property, there is no liability because no transgression occurred.
Transgression (ta'addi) is the key element.
3
Examine the analogical proof
The ruling is analogized to digging a well or placing a stone in the road. The common cause (illa) is creating a public hazard without right, leading to death.
Qiyas is used to extend the rule to any similar hazard.
4
Encounter the doubt scenario
If it is unknown whether the victim was inside (private property) or outside (public road) when the structure fell, qiyas would give no liability (certainty of innocence prevails), but istihsan imposes half diyah.
Istihsan overrides qiyas here for equity.
5
Understand the justification for half diyah
Since full liability applies if outside and no liability if inside, the doubt results in half as a compromise. The author rejects a suggestion of three-quarters by explaining that the two states cannot both occur—it is a single incident.
This differs from cases with multiple wounds, where cumulative liability may apply.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ( فإن مات أحد بسقوطها فديته على عاقلته كما لو حفر بئراً في طريق أو وضع حجراً فتلف به إنسان أي إذا مات إنسان بسقوط ما ذكره من كنيف أو ميزاب أو جرصن فديته على عاقلة أخرجه إلى الطريق لأنه تسبب للهلاك متعدياً في إحداث ما من تضرر به المارة بإشغال هواء الطريق به أو بإحداث ما يحول بينهم وبين الطريق، وكذا إذا عثر بنقضه إنسان. ولو عثر بما أحدث به هو رجل فوقع على آخر فماتا فديتهما على عاقلة إحدثه لأن الواقع كالمدفوع على الآخر، ولو سقط الميزاب فأصاب ما كان في الداخل رجلاً فقتله فلا ضمان على أحد لأنه وضع ذلك في ملكه فلا يكون متعدياً فيه. وإن أصابه ما كان خارجاً فيه يضمن وإن لم يعلم أخرجا أم دخلا لأنه إن كان خارجاً ضمن، وإن كان داخلاً لا يضمن، ففي القياس لا يضمن بالشك لأن فراغ ذمته ثابت بيقين وفي الشغل شك، وفي الاستحسان يضمن النصف لأنه في حال يضمن الكل، وفي حال لا يضمن شيئاً فيضمن النصف، ولا يقال ينبغي أن يضمن ثلاثة أرباع الدية لأنه يضمن في حالة النصف وهو ما إذا أصابه الطرفان فيتنصف فيكون مع النصف الأول ثلاثة أرباع لأن أحوال الإصابة حالة واحدة فلا تتعدد الاستحالة اجتماعهما بخلاف حالة الجرحين
He (may Allah have mercy on him) said: (If someone dies from its fall, his blood-money is on his 'aqila) just as if he dug a well in the road or placed a stone and a person perished from it. That is, if a person dies from the fall of what he mentioned, like a privy, a waterspout, or a projection, then his blood-money is on the 'aqila of the one who brought it out to the road, because he caused destruction by transgressing in creating something that harms passersby by occupying the air of the road with it, or by creating something that obstructs between them and the road. Likewise, if a person trips over his debris. And if a person trips over what he (someone else) created, and falls on another, and both die, then their blood-money is on the 'aqila of the one who created it, because the fallen one is like a pushed one upon the other. And if the waterspout falls and strikes what was inside (a person indoors) and kills him, there is no liability on anyone, because he placed that in his own property, so he is not transgressing in it. And if it strikes what was outside, he is liable. And if it is not known whether it was outside or inside, then in analogy (qiyas) he is not liable due to doubt, because the release of his liability is certain and the liability is doubtful; in istihsan (juristic preference) he is liable for half, because in one state he would be liable for the whole, and in another state he would not be liable for anything, so he is liable for half. And it is not said that he should be liable for three-quarters of the blood-money, because he would be liable in the state of half (if it strikes both sides?), so it halves, making three-quarters with the first half, because the states of injury are one state and cannot be multiple; impossibility of their conjunction, unlike the case of two wounds.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage continues the expansion of the matn from Kanz al-Daqa'iq. The author explains that if someone dies from the fall of a structure (like a privy, waterspout, or projection) that was placed in the road, the blood-money (diyah) is on the 'aqila (the paternal relatives liable for compensation) of the person who placed it, because he caused the death by transgression: he occupied the airspace or obstructed the road, analogous to digging a well or placing a stone. Similarly, if someone trips over the debris and falls on another, killing both, the liability is on the 'aqila of the one who created the obstruction, as the falling person is treated like one pushed. However, if the structure falls on a person who is inside (i.e., within private property), there is no liability because the structure was on the owner's property, so no transgression. If it strikes someone outside, liability applies. A key fiqh point: if it is uncertain whether the victim was inside or outside at the time of injury, qiyas (analogy) dictates no liability (since certainty of innocence is presumed), but istihsan (juristic preference) dictates half liability: because if it were outside, full liability; if inside, no liability; the doubt leads to half. The author rejects the idea of three-quarters by explaining that the two possible states (inside or outside) cannot both occur; it is a single incident, unlike two separate wounds that could each independently cause some liability.

Key Terms
العاقلة (al-'aqila): The paternal relatives (asabah) of the killer who are responsible for paying blood-money (diyah) in cases of unintentional killing or certain torts.
تسبب (tasabbub): Causing harm indirectly, not by direct action (mubasharah); here, placing a structure leads to death when it falls, so it is tasabbub.
الاستحسان (al-istihsan): Juristic preference that deviates from strict qiyas to achieve a more equitable result; here, imposing half diyah in case of doubt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In case of doubt whether a person killed by a falling waterspout was inside or outside, what is the Hanafi ruling according to istihsan?
A) No liability because of certainty of innocence.
B) Full diyah because the default is liability.
C) Half diyah because it is a compromise between full liability and no liability.
D) Three-quarters diyah because of combined probabilities.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that blood-money for death caused by a fallen public structure is on the transgressor's 'aqila, with half-diyah in case of doubt by istihsan.

Liability for Workers: Direct vs Indirect Causation
Ruling on liability for direct killing by workers before handover

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mubasharah and tasabbub in Hanafi liability law and apply the rule to determine diyah, kaffarah, and inheritance rights in construction accidents.

Core Idea
Before work is delivered, workers' acts are directly theirs—full liability, kaffarah, and inheritance bar.

When hired workers cause harm, the key is whether the harm was directly caused by their actions (mubasharah) or indirectly (tasabbub). This passage examines a scenario where workers building a projection cause a death before completion. The ruling hinges on the transfer of responsibility: until the work is delivered, the workers' acts are their own, making them directly liable. This contrasts with cases where a structure placed earlier later falls, which is indirect causation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the factual scenario
Workers are hired to build a wing or awning; it falls before completion and kills a person.
Note that the work has not been handed over to the owner.
2
Determine the nature of causation
Since the work is not delivered, the act is directly attributed to the workers (mubasharah).
Mubasharah means the actor's own physical action caused death.
3
Apply the Hanafi rule for mubasharah
Direct causation imposes diyah (on 'aqila), kaffarah (expiation), and bar from inheriting from the victim.
Kaffarah is wajib; inheritance is prohibited.
4
Distinguish from previous cases (tasabbub)
Earlier cases (projecting a wing, waterspout, privy) where the structure falls later are indirect causation; no kaffarah, no inheritance bar.
Tasabbub only triggers diyah, not kaffarah or inheritance bar.
5
Analyze the qiyas to the hired slaughterer
If the employer misled the workers (falsely claimed a right), workers are liable but can recover from employer by analogy to the slaughterer case.
The common illa: the doer acts under the employer's order but is directly liable; he can seek recourse.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استأجر رب الدار الفعلة لإخراج الجناح أو الظلة فوقع قبل أن يفرغوا من العمل فقتل إنساناً فالضمان عليهم لأن التلف بفعلهم لأن العمل لا يكون مسلماً إلى رب الدار قبل فراغهم منه فانقلب فعلهم قتلا حتى وجبت عليهم الكفارة ويحرمون من الإرث بخلاف ما تقدم من المسائل من إخراج الجناح أو الميزاب أو الكنيف إلى الطريق فقتل إنساناً بسقوطه حيث لا تجب فيه الكفارة ولا يحرم الإرث لأنه تسبب وهنا مباشرة والقتل غير داخل في عقده فلم يستند فعلهم إليه فاقتصر عليهم. قال شيخ الإسلام رحمه الله تعالى : هذا على وجوه : إما إن قال لهم ابنوا لي جناحاً على فناء داري فإنه ملكي ولي منه حق إشراع الجناح إليه من القديم ولم تعلم الفعلة ثم ظهر بخلاف ما قال ثم سقط فأصاب شيئاً فالضمان على الآجر ويرجعون بالضمان على الأمر قياساً واستحساناً. سواء سقط قبل الفراغ من العمل أو بعده، لأن الضمان وجب على الفاعل بأمر الأمر فكان له أن يرجع به عليه كما لو استأجر شخصاً ليذبح له شاة ثم استحقت الشاة بعد الذبح كان للمستحق أن يضمن الذابح ويرجع الذابح به على الأمر فكذا هذا. وأما إذا قال لهم اشرعوا لي جناحاً على فناء داري وأخبرهم أنه ليس له حق الشرع في القديم أو لم يخبرهم حتى بنوا ثم سقط فأتلف شيئاً ؛ إن سقط قبل الفراغ من العمل فالضمان عليهم ولم
And if the owner of the house hires workers to construct a wing or an awning, and it falls before they finish the work and kills a person, then liability is on them because the destruction is by their act, since the work is not delivered to the owner before they finish it, so their act becomes killing, such that the expiation (kaffarah) becomes obligatory on them and they are deprived of inheritance. Unlike the previous cases of projecting a wing, waterspout, or privy into the road and a person dies by its fall, where kaffarah is not obligatory nor is inheritance prevented, because it was tasabbub (indirect causation), while here it is mubasharah (direct causation), and the killing is not part of their contract, so their act is not attributed to him, so liability is confined to them. Shaykh al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This has aspects: if he said to them 'Build for me a wing on the courtyard of my house, for it is my property and I have the right to project the wing from ancient times,' and the workers did not know, then it appears contrary to what he said, and it falls and hits something, then liability is on the workers, and they may claim compensation from the employer by qiyas and istihsan, whether it fell before or after finishing the work, because liability fell on the doer by the order of the commander, so he may claim it back from him, just as if a person hires someone to slaughter a sheep for him, then the sheep is claimed after slaughter, the claimant may hold the slaughterer liable, and the slaughterer may claim from the employer. As for if he said to them 'Project for me a wing on the courtyard of my house' and informed them that he has no right to project in ancient times, or he did not inform them, and they built, and it fell and destroyed something: if it fell before finishing the work, liability is on them, and...
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage distinguishes between tasabbub (indirect causation) and mubasharah (direct causation) in liability. In the prior cases (placing a structure that later falls), the harm is caused indirectly (tasabbub), so only diyah applies but no kaffarah, and the killer can inherit. Here, workers hired to build a projection cause the death by their own physical action (e.g., the structure falls while they are working). Since the work has not been delivered to the owner, the act is directly theirs (mubasharah). Therefore, they must pay kaffarah (expiation) and are barred from inheriting from the victim (if applicable). Shaykh al-Islam then details scenarios: if the employer falsely claims a right to project (but actually has none), the workers are liable but can seek recourse from the employer by qiyas to the slaughter example. If the employer tells them he has no right or does not inform, and the structure falls before completion, the workers bear liability alone (no recourse to employer). The passage ends mid-sentence.

Key Terms
مباشرة (mubasharah): Direct physical causation of harm; the actor's own body or immediate instrument causes death.
تسبب (tasabbub): Indirect causation, e.g., placing an object that later causes harm; here, the original placement leads to death later.
الكفارة (al-kaffarah): Expiation for accidental killing: freeing a slave or fasting two months; required only in mubasharah cases.
ظلة (zullah): Awning or projecting structure; relevant to liability when it falls.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why is kaffarah obligatory on the workers but not on the employer when a projection falls and kills someone before completion?
A) Because the employer is not the direct cause.
B) Because the work is not yet delivered, so the act is mubasharah of the workers.
C) Because the workers are contractors, not employees.
D) Because the employer did not intend the harm.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, direct causation (mubasharah) before work is delivered imposes full liability (diyah, kaffarah, inheritance bar) on the worker, while indirect causation (tasabbub) only triggers diyah.

Liability for Waterspout and Half Diya by Istihsan
Rulings on waterspout falling on person, inside vs outside, and istihsan for half diya

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi istihsan ruling for waterspout liability and distinguish it from the qiyas outcome.

Core Idea
When liability is certain in some states and certain in none, istihsan splits the difference.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq deals with a specific liability case when a waterspout falls from a property. It illustrates how the Hanafi school uses istihsan (juristic preference) to override a strict qiyas that would result in no compensation, opting for half diya as an equitable middle ground. We will walk through the two clear scenarios, the ambiguous case, and the reasoning behind the istihsan ruling.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two clear cases
The passage first gives two rulings: if the victim was inside the property, no liability because the owner placed the waterspout lawfully; if outside, full liability because it endangers the public.
Inside = no liability; outside = full diya.
2
Understand the ambiguous case
When it is unknown whether the victim was inside or outside, we do not know which ruling applies. The passage asks: should the owner pay or not?
3
Apply qiyas
By strict analogy (qiyas), the owner pays nothing because the default is immunity from liability (bara'at al-dhimmah), and obligation is doubtful. Certainty is not overridden by doubt.
Qiyas relies on the principle 'al-yaqin la yuzalu bi al-shakk'.
4
Apply istihsan
By istihsan, the owner pays half the full diya. This is because in one possible state (outside) he would owe full diya, in the other (inside) he owes nothing. The average (half) is most just.
Istihsan here is based on equity and practicality.
5
Refute the objection for three-quarters
One might argue that there is a third possibility: the victim being hit at the threshold, which would be half-case. But the author explains that the states of injury are one state (inside or outside) and cannot coexist, unlike two separate wounds.
Thus, only two states exist, so half is correct, not three-quarters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو سقط الميزاب فأصاب ما كان في الداخل رجلاً فقتله فلا ضمان على أحد لأنه وضع ذلك في ملكه فلا يكون متعدياً فيه. وإن أصابه ما كان خارجاً فيه يضمن وإن لم يعلم أخرجا أم دخلا لأنه إن كان خارجاً ضمن، وإن كان داخلاً لا يضمن، ففي القياس لا يضمن بالشك لأن فراغ ذمته ثابت بيقين وفي الشغل شك، وفي الاستحسان يضمن النصف لأنه في حال يضمن الكل، وفي حال لا يضمن شيئاً فيضمن النصف، ولا يقال ينبغي أن يضمن ثلاثة أرباع الدية لأنه يضمن في حالة النصف وهو ما إذا أصابه الطرفان فيتنصف فيكون مع النصف الأول ثلاثة أرباع لأن أحوال الإصابة حالة واحدة فلا تتعدد الاستحالة اجتماعهما بخلاف حالة الجرحين
And if a waterspout falls and hits a person inside [the property], killing him, there is no liability on anyone because he placed it in his property, so he is not transgressing. But if it hits him while outside, he is liable. If it is unknown whether he was outside or inside, because if he was outside he is liable, and if inside he is not liable, then by qiyas he is not liable due to doubt because his immunity is established by certainty and obligation is doubtful. By istihsan, he pays half the diya because in one case he pays fully, and in one case he pays nothing, so he pays half. And it cannot be said that he should pay three-quarters of the diya, because he is liable in the half-case—that is, when it hits both sides—then it would be divided, so with the first half it becomes three-quarters; but the states of injury are one state, not multiple, because their combination is impossible, unlike the case of two wounds.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling from Kitab al-Diyat on liability when a waterspout (mizab) falls from a property. The author distinguishes two clear cases: if the victim was inside the property, no liability because the owner placed it on his own property lawfully; if the victim was outside, liability applies because it is a public thoroughfare. The key point is the third scenario: when it is unknown whether the victim was inside or outside at the time. By strict qiyas (analogy), the owner is not liable because the default is freedom from liability (bara'at al-dhimmah) and obligation is doubtful. However, by istihsan (juristic preference), the owner pays half the full diya. This is because the situation is such that in one possible state he owes full diya, in another state he owes nothing; taking the average is equitable. The author then refutes a potential objection: one might argue that there are three states (outside, inside, and the half-state of being at the threshold?), but he clarifies that the half-state is already included, and the two states (inside/outside) cannot coexist, unlike two separate wounds where each can independently cause death. Thus, the istihsan ruling is half, not three-quarters.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas for a more equitable ruling
ميزاب (mizab): Waterspout or drainpipe protruding from a building
قياس (qiyas): Analogical deduction; in this passage, the strict analogy that leads to no liability
دية (diya): Blood money paid for wrongful death

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a waterspout falling on a person whose location (inside/outside) is unknown, what is the Hanafi ruling by istihsan?
A) No liability
B) Full diya
C) Half diya
D) Three-quarters diya

Key Takeaway
Where liability is certain in one state and certain in none, istihsan dictates half diya as the just middle ground.

Liability After Selling an Obstruction: Seller Remains Responsible
Ruling that seller remains liable for obstruction placed in road even after sale

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the Hanafi ruling on liability for road obstruction after sale from the case of a leaning wall and apply the principle that liability follows the act of obstruction, not ownership.

Core Idea
Selling an object placed in the road does not transfer liability — the original actor remains liable for his act of obstruction.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Ra'iq clarifies a key distinction in Hanafi tort law. The rule is that if you place an object in a public road and then sell it, you remain liable for any harm it causes. The reason is that liability arises from the act of obstructing the road, not from ownership. This is contrasted with a leaning wall, where liability depends on official notice (ishhad) tied to ownership. Understanding this distinction is essential for correctly assigning liability in property-related accidents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage carefully and note the two cases: (1) placing a beam or wing in the road and then selling it, and (2) a leaning wall after sale with notice.
Focus on the key difference: obstruction vs. leaning wall.
2
State the Hanafi ruling for each
For obstruction: the seller is liable. For the leaning wall: neither seller nor buyer is liable. Write down these two positions.
3
Locate the illa (legal cause)
Identify why the seller is liable for the obstruction: because his act of placing the object continues after sale. The illa is 'ishghal al-tariq' (obstructing the road), which does not depend on ownership.
For the wall, the illa is the notice (ishhad), which requires ownership.
4
Apply the reasoning to the distinction
Explain why the sale does not nullify the seller's liability for obstruction: the act of obstruction is a continuous tort, and ownership transfer does not undo the original wrong. In contrast, the notice for the wall is void because ownership changed.
This illustrates the principle that liability can follow the actor, not the property.
5
Note the practical implication
The ruling means that even after selling the property, the original wrongdoer cannot escape liability by transferring ownership. The buyer is not liable unless he actively contributes to the harm.
This encourages careful behavior when encroaching on public spaces.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو أشرع جناحاً إلى الطريق ثم باع الكل فأصاب الجناح رجلاً فقتله أو وضع خشبة في الطريق ثم باع الخشبة وتركها المشتري حتى عطب بها إنسان فالضمان على البائع لأن فعله لم ينفسخ بزوال ملكه وهو الموجب بخلاف الحائط المائل إذا باعه بعد الإشهاد عليه ثم سقط في ملك المشتري على إنسان حيث لا يضمن البائع ولا المشتري لأن المشتري لم يشهد عليه وهو شرط الحائط المائل، وفي حق البائع قد بطل الإشهادة الأول لأن الملك شرط لصحة الإشهاد فيبطل بخروجه عن ملكه لأنه لا يتمكن من نقض ملك الغير، وفيما نحن فيه إنما يضمن بإشغال الطريق لا باعتبار الملك والإشغال باق بعد البيع ألا ترى أن ذلك الإشغال لو حصل من غير مالك كالمستأجر أو المعير أو الغاصب يضمن وفي الحائط لا يضمن غير المالك
And if one extends a wing into the road, then sells the whole property, and the wing hits a person and kills him; or if one places a beam in the road, then sells the beam and the buyer leaves it until a person is harmed by it, the liability is on the seller, because his action is not nullified by the transfer of ownership—that action is the cause. This is unlike a leaning wall: if one sells it after giving notice (ishhad) of its danger, then it falls in the buyer's property onto a person, neither the seller nor the buyer is liable, because the buyer did not give notice—which is a condition for a leaning wall—and regarding the seller, the first notice becomes invalid because ownership is a condition for the validity of notice, and it is nullified when the property leaves his ownership, as he cannot demolish another's property. In our case, liability is due to obstructing the road, not due to ownership, and the obstruction remains after the sale. Do you not see that if the obstruction was done by a non-owner—such as a lessee, borrower, or usurper—they are liable; while in the case of a wall, a non-owner is not liable.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage distinguishes two scenarios of liability after transfer of ownership. In the first scenario, a person extends a wing (a protruding structure) into the public road, then sells the building. If the wing later falls and kills someone, the seller remains liable. Similarly, if someone places a beam in the road, sells it, and the buyer leaves it there causing harm, the seller is liable. The reason (illa) is that liability is based on the act of obstructing the public road, not on ownership of the object. That act continues after sale. In contrast, a leaning wall (ha'it ma'il) is different: if the owner gives official notice (ishhad) of its danger and then sells, the notice becomes void because ownership is required for the validity of the notice; after sale, only the new owner could give notice. If the wall falls in the buyer's property, neither is liable: the seller because his notice is invalid, the buyer because he did not give notice. The underlying principle is that obstruction of the road is actionable regardless of ownership, while the leaning wall case is tied to the specific legal procedure of ishhad.

Key Terms
إشهاد (ishhad): Official notification or warning about a dangerous property, typically in the presence of witnesses
إشغال الطريق (ishghal al-tariq): Obstructing the public road

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If someone places a beam in the road and then sells it, and the buyer leaves it causing injury, who is liable according to the Hanafi school?
A) The buyer only
B) The seller only
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
Liability for road obstruction attaches to the actor who created the obstruction, not the owner, and cannot be shed by selling the property.

Liability of Hired Workers: Direct Act vs. Causation
Ruling distinguishing direct and indirect liability of hired workers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between direct perpetration (mubasharah) and causation (tasabbub) in accidental killing and apply the Hanafi ruling on liability of hired workers before delivery of work.

Core Idea
Before work is delivered, hired workers are direct agents; their act is killing, not causation.

In Hanafi fiqh, liability for accidental death depends on whether the act is direct (mubasharah) or indirect (tasabbub). This lesson examines the ruling when hired workers are constructing a protruding structure and it falls before completion. The key is that until the work is delivered, the workers' control makes them direct perpetrators, incurring full liability including kaffarah and inheritance preclusion.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
The passage contrasts two cases: (1) owner hires workers to build a projection that falls before completion, (2) owner himself places a projection on the road.
Both involve a structure killing a person.
2
Determine the legal distinction
In scenario 1, workers are direct (mubasharah) because work not delivered and under their control; in scenario 2, owner is causer (tasabbub) because work was completed and placed.
Delivery transfers control to owner.
3
Apply the consequences
Direct act: workers must pay diya, observe kaffarah, and are precluded from inheritance. Causation: owner pays diya via 'aqilah, no kaffarah, no inheritance preclusion.
Kaffarah is obligatory only for direct killing.
4
Note the contractual element
The killing is not part of the contract, so the act is not attributed to the owner; liability stays with the workers.
Contract scope matters for attribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استأجر رب الدار الفعلة لإخراج الجناح أو الظلة فوقع قبل أن يفرغوا من العلم فقتل إنساناً فالضمان عليهم لأن التلف بفعلهم لأن العمل لا يكون مسلماً إلى رب الدار قبل فراغهم منه فانقلب فعلهم قتلا حتى وجبت عليهم الكفارة ويحرمون من الإرث بخلاف ما تقدم من المسائل من إخراج الجناح أو الميزاب أو الكنيف إلى الطريق فقتل إنساناً بسقوطه حيث لا تجب فيه الكفارة ولا يحرم الإرث لأنه تسبب وهنا مباشرة والقتل غير داخل في عقده فلم يستند فعلهم إليه فاقتصر عليهم
And if the owner of the house hires workers to extend a wing or awning, and it falls before they finish the work, killing a person, the liability is on them, because the destruction is by their act, since the work is not delivered to the owner before its completion. Thus their act becomes killing, so that kaffarah is obligatory on them and they are precluded from inheritance. This is unlike the previous cases of extending a wing, waterspout, or latrine onto the road, which kill a person by falling, where no kaffarah is obligatory and they are not precluded from inheritance, because there it was causation (tasabbub) while here it is direct perpetration (mubasharah), and the killing is not part of their contract, so the act is not attributed to the owner—thus liability is limited to them.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This passage distinguishes the liability of hired workers from that of the property owner in construction accidents. When the owner hires workers to build a protruding structure (wing, awning), and the structure falls and kills someone before the work is finished, the workers are directly liable. The reason is that until completion, the work is under their control—they have not yet delivered it to the owner. Therefore, their action is considered direct killing (mubasharah), requiring kaffarah (expiation) and preclusion from inheritance (if the victim is their heir). This contrasts with the earlier cases where the owner himself placed the structure (wing, waterspout, latrine) onto the road; there, the owner is liable civilly (diya on his 'aqilah) but not criminally (no kaffarah, no inheritance preclusion) because it was causation (tasabbub) not direct act. The workers' liability is independent of the contract; the owner is not liable because the killing was not part of the contract.

Key Terms
مباشرة (mubasharah): Direct perpetration of an act, as opposed to causation (tasabbub)
تسبب (tasabbub): Causation, setting in motion a chain of events indirectly
كفارة (kaffarah): Expiation required for accidental killing (freeing a slave, fasting, etc.)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why do workers pay kaffarah when a structure they are building falls and kills someone before completion?
A) Because the owner ordered them
B) Because they are direct agents (mubasharah)
C) Because the structure was defective
D) Because they are responsible for the material

Key Takeaway
Hired workers are liable for deaths caused by structures before delivery because their act is direct (mubasharah), not causation.

Liability of Workers and Commissioner in Construction Contracts: Three Scenarios
Three scenarios of hiring workers with liability analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the liability rules in three hiring scenarios and apply the Hanafi principle of shifting liability via istihsan after completion.

Core Idea
After completion, the commissioner’s benefit shifts liability to him via istihsan, even if the order was invalid.

This passage from Shaykh al-Islam analyses nuanced liability cases when hired workers build a structure on property where the commissioner may or may not have rights. The rulings depend on knowledge, timing, and the principle of semblance of validity (shubhat al-sihhah). You will learn the qiyas and istihsan reasoning for each scenario.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios
List the three fact patterns: (1) commissioner falsely claims an ancient right, workers unaware; (2) commissioner admits or workers know he has no right; (3) hiring to build in the middle of a road.
Pay attention to the workers’ knowledge and the nature of the property.
2
Determine the qiyas ruling for each
For each scenario, state the liability under strict analogy (qiyas) before and after completion. Scenario 1: workers liable but recover from commissioner. Scenario 2: workers liable, no recovery. Scenario 3: workers liable, no recovery.
Qiyas is the default rule before considering istihsan.
3
Identify the istihsan exception for scenario 3
For scenario 3 (building in middle of road), istihsan overrides qiyas after completion: the commissioner becomes liable. Before completion, workers remain liable.
The rationale is the semblance of validity after the commissioner benefits.
4
Analyse the rationale: why istihsan overrides qiyas after completion?
The commissioner’s order is valid in that he can benefit after construction. Manifesting the semblance of validity after completion is more appropriate than before, because the invalidity was only due to his initial lack of right, which is remedied by completion.
This is a key Hanafi principle: shubhat al-sihhah can affect liability after benefit.
5
Apply the analogy to the slaughter case
The passage uses the analogy of hiring someone to slaughter a sheep that turns out to be owned by another. In that case, the slaughterer pays the owner but recovers from the hirer. This supports the workers’ right to recover when they acted in good faith on the commissioner’s order.
Understand how the illa (acting under authority) transfers.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال شيخ الإسلام رحمه الله تعالى : هذا على وجوه : إما إن قال لهم ابنوا لي جناحاً على فناء داري فإنه ملكي ولي منه حق إشراع الجناح إليه من القديم ولم تعلم الفعلة ثم ظهر بخلاف ما قال ثم سقط فأصاب شيئاً فالضمان على الآجر ويرجعون بالضمان على الأمر قياساً واستحساناً. سواء سقط قبل الفراغ من العمل أو بعده، لأن الضمان وجب على الفاعل بأمر الأمر فكان له أن يرجع به عليه كما لو استأجر شخصاً ليذبح له شاة ثم استحقت الشاة بعد الذبح كان للمستحق أن يضمن الذابح ويرجع الذابح به على الأمر فكذا هذا. وأما إذا قال لهم اشرعوا لي جناحاً على فناء داري وأخبرهم أنه ليس له حق الشرع في القديم أو لم يخبرهم حتى بنوا ثم سقط فأتلف شيئاً ؛ إن سقط قبل الفراغ من العمل فالضمان عليهم ولم يرجعوا به على الآمر قياساً، وإن سقط بعد الفراغ من العمل فكذلك في جواب القياس لأن المستأجر أمرهم بما لا يملك مباشرته بنفسه وقد علموا فساد أمره فلم يحكم بالضمان على المستأجر كما لو استأجر رجلاً ليذبح شاة جاز له وأعلمه فذبح ثم ضمن الذابح للجار لم يرجع به على الآمر ، وكذا لو استأجرهم ليبنوا له بيتاً في وسط الطريق ثم سقط وأتلف شيئاً يرجعوا به على الآمر وفي الاستحسان يكون الضمان على الأمر لأن هذا الأمر صحيح من حيث إنه لا يجوز بيعه فمن حيث إن الأمر صحيح يكون إقرار الضمان على الأمر بعد الفراغ من العمل، ومن حيث إنه فاسد يكون الضمان على العامل قبل الفراغ من العمل عملاً بها، وإظهار شبهة الصحة بعد الفراغ من العلم أولى من إظهاره قبل الفراغ لأن أمر الأمر إنما لا يصلح من حيث إنه لا يملك الانتفاع بفناء داره وإنما حصل له ذلك بعد الفراغ من العمل
Shaykh al-Islam (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This has aspects: First, if he says to them, 'Build for me a wing upon the courtyard of my house, for it is my property and I have the right to extend it from ancient times,' and the workers do not know [otherwise], then it turns out to be contrary to what he said, and it falls and hits something—the liability is on the hirers (workers), and they can recover from the commissioner by qiyas and istihsan, whether it falls before or after completion, because the liability is established on the agent by the order of the commissioner, so he can recover from him, just as if one hires a person to slaughter a sheep which then turns out to belong to another, the owner can claim from the slaughterer and the slaughterer recovers from the commissioner. Second, if he says to them, 'Extend a wing upon my courtyard,' and informs them that he has no ancient right, or does not inform them, and they build it, then it falls and damages something: if it falls before completion, liability is on them and they do not recover from the commissioner by qiyas. If it falls after completion, similarly in the answer of qiyas, because the hirer ordered them to do what he cannot do himself, and they knew of the invalidity of his order, so the commissioner is not liable, just as if one hires a man to slaughter a sheep which is owned by another and informs him, and the slaughterer is then liable to the owner, he does not recover from the commissioner. Similarly, if he hires them to build a house in the middle of the road, and it falls and damages something, they can recover from the commissioner. By istihsan, liability is on the commissioner because this order is valid in one respect (since it is not permissible to sell it), so from the perspective of validity, liability is fixed on the commissioner after completion; from the perspective of invalidity, liability is on the worker before completion. Manifesting the semblance of validity after completion is more appropriate than before completion, because the commissioner's order is only invalid due to his lack of right to use the courtyard, and that right accrues to him after completion.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
Shaykh al-Islam (likely Khwajah Parhan or similar) analyzes three scenarios for hired workers constructing a wing. Scenario 1: The owner claims he has an ancient right to extend his wing over the courtyard and the workers believe him, but it turns out false. Whether the accident occurs before or after completion, the workers are liable but can recover from the owner (because they acted on his order). The analogy is hiring someone to slaughter a sheep that later turns out to be owned by another—the slaughterer pays the true owner but recovers from the hirer. Scenario 2: The owner admits or the workers know he has no right. In qiyas, the workers are liable and cannot recover, because they knowingly performed an unlawful act. This applies regardless of timing; after completion, qiyas also denies recovery. Scenario 3: If the owner hires them to build a house in the middle of the road (clearly illegal), then by istihsan, after completion the owner becomes liable (due to the semblance of validity of the order after construction), while before completion the workers are liable. This nuanced approach balances the illegality of the order with the fact that the owner ultimately benefits.

Key Terms
الأمر (al-'amr): Commissioner (the person ordering the work)
الأجير (al-ajir): Hired worker (hirer) in this context
شبهة الصحة (shubhat al-sihhah): Semblance of validity that affects liability after completion

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the scenario where workers knowingly build an illegal wing, and it falls after completion, what is the istihsan ruling?
A) Workers liable and cannot recover
B) Commissioner liable
C) Both liable equally
D) No liability

Key Takeaway
Liability in hiring contracts depends on workers' knowledge and timing; istihsan shifts liability to the commissioner after completion when the order has a semblance of validity.

Liability for Digging Wells in the Road: Multiple Diggers and Causes of Death
Rulings on liability when multiple people dig a well, and when someone falls in

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi liability rulings for multiple diggers of a well in a public road, and distinguish between death by direct fall and death by hunger or grief.

Core Idea
Liability for a well in the road depends on causation: direct fall vs. indirect death like hunger; multiple diggers share liability by istihsan.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq addresses a common urban hazard: digging a well in a public road. It provides detailed rulings on who is liable when multiple people dig or modify the well, and how the cause of death affects liability. Understanding these nuances is essential for applying Hanafi diyat law to modern construction and public safety scenarios.

Guided Path
1
Identify the primary act and its ruling
Digging a well in a public road is mubah (permissible) but imposes liability for harm caused. Determine the basic rule: the digger is liable for direct consequences.
The passage does not state the permissibility explicitly, but it is implied by the discussion of liability.
2
Distinguish the multiple digger scenarios
The passage covers two sub-cases: (1) a second person digs further inside the same well, and (2) a second person widens the mouth. In each, determine liability by qiyas vs. istihsan.
By qiyas, only the first digger is liable; by istihsan, both are liable in thirds.
3
Examine the cause of death sub-rulings
If the victim dies directly from the fall, liability is clear. If death results from hunger, thirst, or grief, different opinions arise. Note Abu Hanifa's view (no liability for hunger) and Muhammad's view (liable for both hunger and grief).
The fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view.
4
Analyze the cover/uncover scenario
If the first digger covers the well and another uncovers it, the first remains liable because his digging is the primary cause.
This reinforces the principle of direct causation.
5
Consider the location factor
Liability only applies if the well is in a public road. If dug in one's own property or with an ancient right, there is no liability. If dug in another's courtyard without permission, liability arises.
This aligns with the general principle that one is not liable for harm on one's own property.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
حفر بئراً في الطريق فجاء آخر وحفر طائفة في أسفلها ثم وقع فيها إنسان ومات في القياس يضمن الأول وبه أخذ محمد وفي الاستحسان يجب الضمان عليهما أثلاثاً. ولو حفر بئراً ثم جاء رأسها فسقط فيها إنسان ومات كان الضمان عليهما أثلاثاً قالوا : تأويل المسألة أن الثاني وسع رأسها بحيث يعلم الناس أن الواقع إنما وقع في موضع بعضه من حفر الأول وبعضه من حفر الثاني، أما إذا وسع الثاني رأسها بحيث إنه إنما وقع في موضع حفر الثاني كان الضمان على الثاني وإن لم يدر فالضمان عليهما. قاضيخان قوله «حفر إلى آخره» سقط إنسان فقال الحافر إنه ألقى نفسه وكذبه الورثة في ذلك كان القول قول الحافر في قول أبي يوسف أخراً وهو قول محمد لأن الظاهر أن البصير يرى موضع قدميه، وإن كان الظاهر أن الإنسان لا يوقع نفسه إلا إذا وقعت له شدة فلا يجب الضمان بالشك. قوله حفر بئراً في الطريق ثم كساها بالتراب أو بخضر أو بما هو من جنس الأرض يضمن الأول، ولو غطى رأسها وجاء آخر ورفع الغطاء فوقع فيها إنسان ضمن الأول، وقال قاضيخان : قيد بقوله «فتلف فيه فلو لم يمت من ذلك بل مات جوعاً أو عطشاً أو غماً هل يضمن الحافر؟ لم يذكر محمد هذا وقد ذكر أبو يوسف في الإملاء خلافا فقال : على قول أبي حنيفة لا يضمن الحافر إذا مات جوعاً فالجواب كما قال أبو حنيفة، فأما إذا مات غماً فإنه يضمن الحافر. وفي الكبرى : والفتوى على قول أبي حنيفة رحمه الله . وفي الذخيرة وقال محمد : يضمن في الحالتين. هذا إذا كان الحفر في طريق المسلمين، فأما إذا كان الحفر في فناء داره فوقع فيه إنسان فمات هل يضمن إن كان الفناء لغيره يكون ضامناً، وأما إذا حفر في ملكه أو كان له حق الحفر في القديم فكذا الجواب لا يضمن، وإن
If someone digs a well in the road, then another comes and digs a portion in its bottom, then a person falls in and dies: by qiyas, the first is liable—Muhammad adopted this. By istihsan, liability is on both in thirds. And if one digs a well, then another widens its mouth, and a person falls in and dies, liability is on both in thirds. They said: The interpretation of the issue is that the second widened the mouth such that people know that the fallen person fell in a place partly from the first's digging and partly from the second's. But if the second widened the mouth such that the person fell only in the place dug by the second, liability is on the second alone; if it is unknown, liability on both. Qadi Khan said (commenting on 'he dug...'): If a person falls in and the digger says 'he threw himself in,' and the heirs deny it, the word of the digger is accepted according to the later opinion of Abu Yusuf and also Muhammad's opinion, because the apparent fact is that a seeing person sees his footsteps. Even though the apparent fact is that a person does not throw himself in unless under distress, liability is not established by doubt. If one digs a well in the road then covers it with earth or greenery or with something from the ground, the first is liable. And if one covers its mouth and another comes and removes the cover and a person falls in, the first is liable. Qadi Khan said: He conditioned with 'he perishes therein'—if he did not die from that but died of hunger, thirst, or grief, is the digger liable? Muhammad did not mention this, but Abu Yusuf mentioned in al-Imla' a difference: according to Abu Hanifa, the digger is not liable if he dies of hunger; so the answer is as Abu Hanifa said. But if he dies of grief, the digger is liable. In al-Kubra: the fatwa is on the saying of Abu Hanifa. In al-Dhakhirah: Muhammad said he is liable in both cases. This is when the digging is in the Muslims' road. But if the digging is in his courtyard and a person falls in and dies, is he liable? If the courtyard belongs to another, he is liable. But if he digs in his own property or he has the ancient right to dig, then the answer is that he is not liable.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage covers multiple sub-rulings regarding liability for digging a well in a public road. First, if two people dig the same well (one after the other), and a victim falls into it, there is a dispute: by qiyas, the first digger alone is liable (Imam Muhammad's position), but by istihsan, both are liable in thirds (likely because each contributed). If the second only widened the mouth, liability is also shared in thirds, but the precise share depends on where the victim fell—if only in the second's widened part, the second bears full liability; if unknown, both are liable equally. Second, if the digger claims the victim jumped in voluntarily, his word is taken (with a later opinion of Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) because it is assumed that a seeing person avoids such dangers; doubt prevents liability. Third, if the digger covers the well and then another uncovers it, the first digger remains liable (his act of digging remains the primary cause). Fourth, if the victim dies not directly from the fall but from hunger, thirst, or grief, there is a difference: according to Abu Hanifa, no liability for hunger death, but liability for grief death; Muhammad says liable in both; the fatwa is on Abu Hanifa's view. Fifth, the location matters: if the well is in the public road, liability applies; if in one's own courtyard (or property with ancient right), no liability; but if in another's courtyard, liability applies.

Key Terms
الخندق (al-khandaq): Trench or well dug in road
تلف (talaf): Destruction or death
استحسان (istihsan): Juridical preference, an equitable exception to qiyas
ضمان (daman): Liability, financial responsibility

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi fatwa (mufta bihi), if two persons dig the same well in a public road (first digs, second widens mouth), how is liability divided when a victim falls in?
A) Only the first digger is liable.
B) Only the second digger is liable.
C) Both are liable in equal halves.
D) Both are liable in thirds.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, liability for a well dug in a public road is shared proportionally among contributors by istihsan, and indirect causes like hunger do not trigger liability according to the relied-upon position of Abu Hanifa.

Liability for Digging Wells: Location and Causation
Ruling statement on liability for well-digging.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability rule for digging wells in public roads and non-passage areas, and distinguish scenarios where the victim's own action breaks causation.

Core Idea
Liability for well-digging depends on location and whether the victim's own action caused the death.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a series of scenarios that test when a person who digs a well is liable for someone's death. These rulings are based on the principles of direct causation and the location of the digging. Understanding these distinctions is essential in the Hanafi law of diyat (blood money).

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the general ruling
The act is digging a well. The ruling on liability depends on where the well is dug and how the death occurs.
2
Distinguish between public road and desert
If dug in a public road (qāriʿat al-ṭarīq), the digger is liable. If dug in a desert or waste land away from passage, there is no liability.
This is the first major condition.
3
Analyse the intervening act scenario
If a person falls into the well, survives, and then while trying to climb out falls again and dies, the digger is not liable. The victim's own action (climbing) broke the causal chain.
This illustrates the Hanafi emphasis on direct causation.
4
Examine the rock scenario
If the victim walks inside the well and dies from a rock: if the rock was in its natural place, no liability; if the digger moved the rock and placed it in the well, the digger is liable because he created a new hazard.
The digger's liability attaches only when he introduces a new danger, not for pre-existing conditions.
5
Extract the underlying principle
The common thread: liability requires that the digger's act be the direct cause of death, without an intervening independent act of the victim or natural pre-existing hazard.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فعلى صاحب البئر، وإذا حفر الرجل بئراً في طريق مكة في الفيافي والمفازات في غير ممر الناس فوقع فيه إنسان فإنه لا ضمان له وهذا بخلاف ما لو حفر في الطريق فإنه يصير ضامناً، فإذا حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريقة فوقع إنسان فسلم من الوقعة وطلب الخروج منها فتعلق حتى إذا كان في وسطها سقط وعطب فلا ضمان ولو مشى في أسفلها فعطب بصخرة فيها، فإن كانت الصخرة في موضعها من الأرض فلا ضمان، وإن كان صاحب البئر هدر لأنه قاتل لنفسه بجره، وإن قلعها من موضعها ووضعها في ناحية البئر فعلى صاحب البئر ؛ هكذا ذكر في المنتقى .
So it is upon the owner of the well. And if a man digs a well on the road of Makkah in the deserts and waste lands not in the path of people, and a person falls into it, there is no liability for him. This is contrary to if he dug in the road, then he becomes liable. So if he digs a well on the side of the road, and a person falls into it and is safe from the fall and seeks to get out, and then clings (to the sides) until when he is in the middle of it he falls and dies, there is no liability. And if he walks at the bottom of it and dies due to a rock in it: if the rock was in its place from the ground, no liability; and if the owner of the well removed it from its place and placed it in a side of the well, then liability is on the owner of the well. Thus it is mentioned in al-Muntaqa.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage from al-Bahr al-Raiq presents a ruling on digging wells in different locations. First, if a well is dug in the desert of Makkah away from where people walk, the digger is not liable if someone falls in because it is not a place of public passage. However, if the well is dug in the road itself, liability attaches. Then it discusses a scenario: a person falls into a well on the side of the road, survives the fall, and while trying to climb out falls again from the middle and dies—here there is no liability because the person's own action (reentering the fall) caused the death. Similarly, if a person walks inside the well and is killed by a rock that was in its natural place, no liability; but if the digger moved the rock and placed it elsewhere in the well, he is liable because he was the direct cause of the hazard. These rulings are based on the principle that the digger is only liable for direct causation or for creating an added hazard beyond the original condition.

Key Terms
قارعة الطريق (qāriʿat al-ṭarīq): The side or main part of the road where people pass
ضمان (ḍamān): Legal liability to pay compensation for harm caused

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a man digs a well on the side of the road (qāriʿat al-ṭarīq), a person falls in, safely lands, then while trying to climb out falls from the middle and dies?
A) The digger is liable for the full diyah
B) The digger is not liable because the victim's own action caused the death
C) The digger is liable only for half diyah
D) The digger is liable unless the victim was a trespasser

Key Takeaway
Liability for well-digging hinges on location (public road vs desert) and whether the victim's own action broke the chain of causation.

Slave Digging Well: Dispute Between Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn
Intra-Hanafi dispute on diyat: liability of a slave whose well kills two, with one pardon

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the positions of Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn in the case of a slave whose well kills two people when one victim's wali pardons, and identify the factual assumption that drives the disagreement.

Core Idea
One wali's pardon does not extinguish the other's claim; dispute turns on simultaneous vs successive falls.

This passage presents a subtle intra-Hanafi disagreement over liability when a slave's act causes two deaths and one guardian pardons. The dispute hinges on how we view the timing of the falls and the nature of the slave's liability. By parsing the reasoning of Abu Hanifa and the two Sahibayn, you will sharpen your ability to identify the precise point of legal contention (mawrid al-khilaf) in diyat cases.

Guided Path
1
State the facts
Identify the act (slave digs well on road), the victims (two people fall in), the pardon (first victim's wali pardons the slave), and the legal question (how much is owed to the second wali?).
The well is on a public road (qari'at al-tariq), which establishes negligence.
2
Identify Abu Hanifa's position
According to Abu Hanifa, the master must hand over the entire slave or pay full ransom (diyah). This means the second wali can claim the full value of the slave.
Abu Hanifa sees the falls as successive, so the second victim's right is independent.
3
Identify the Sahibayn's position
Abu Yusuf and Muhammad rule that only half the slave is owed. They say 'because both fell together (ma'an), and one guardian pardoned.'
They treat the falls as simultaneous, so liability is shared equally.
4
Analyse the key difference
The disagreement turns on the interpretation of 'ma'an' (together). Abu Hanifa likely considers the falls to occur one after the other, giving each victim an independent claim to the full slave. The Sahibayn treat them as a single event, so the slave's value is divided equally.
The textual expression 'ثم وقع فيها آخر' suggests sequence, but the Sahibayn apply a different perspective.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical consequence: under Abu Hanifa, the second wali gets the whole slave or full diyah; under Sahibayn, only half. This affects how much compensation the master must pay.
Later Hanafi works often prefer the Sahibayn's view in cases of near-simultaneous causation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
عبد حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق فجاء إنسان ووقع فيها فعفا عنه الولي ثم وقع فيها آخر فعلى المولى أن يدفع كله أو يفديه في قول أبي حنيفة. وقال أبو يوسف ومحمد: يدفع إليه نصف لأنهما وقعا معاً فعفا عنه أحد الوليين.
A slave dug a well on the side of the road. A person came and fell into it, and his guardian (the victim's wali) pardoned (the slave). Then another person fell into it. According to Abu Hanifa, it is upon the master to hand over the entire slave or ransom him. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad said: He hands over half to him because both fell together, and one of the two guardians pardoned.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The case: a slave digs a well in the road. The first victim's wali (guardian, i.e., the heir who can claim retaliation) pardons the slave for the first death. Then a second victim falls and dies. The question: how much of the slave is due to the second wali? Abu Hanifa says the master must hand over the whole slave (or pay full ransom) because the pardon of the first wali does not affect the second victim's right to the entire slave, as the slave is liable for both crimes. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad say only half the slave is due, because the two falls occurred 'together' (maʿan) — meaning the slave's liability was shared between the two victims from the start; the first's pardon only releases his share, so only half remains for the second. The apparent meaning of 'together' is that the two falls happened in such quick succession that the slave's well was the cause of both simultaneously, making the slave's 'body' (raqabah) liable to both equally. This illustrates a key Hanafi principle: when a slave commits multiple capital offenses, the victims' heirs share the slave's value proportionally.

Key Terms
ولي (walī): Guardian of the victim, the heir who has the right to pardon or claim qisas/diyah
يفديه (yafdīhi): He ransoms the slave (pays diyah) to redeem him from being handed over

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case of a slave who dug a well into which two people fell and died, and the first victim's wali pardons the slave, what does Abu Hanifa rule?
A) The master hands over the entire slave to the second wali
B) The master hands over half the slave
C) The master pays half diyah only
D) The slave is freed due to double liability

Key Takeaway
In diyat cases, pardon by one victim's wali does not extinguish the slave's liability to other victims; the extent depends on whether the harms are seen as simultaneous or successive.

Liability of Heirs Who Dig in the Deceased's Insolvent Estate
States the liability of heirs who dig in the deceased's house when the estate is insolvent.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability of heirs who alter the deceased's property when it is fully encumbered by debt, and identify who pays diyah if someone falls.

Core Idea
Heirs are liable for any loss they cause to a debt-encumbered estate.

This lesson covers a specific Hanafi ruling on the rights of creditors over the deceased's property. When the debt covers the entire value of the estate, the property effectively belongs to the creditors. Any action by the heirs that diminishes the value makes them responsible to the creditors, and if someone is harmed, the blood money falls on the digger's ʿāqila.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scenario's key elements
Note the death, the house, the debt covering its full value, and the heirs' digging.
The debt must fully cover the value - 'ما يستغرق قيمتها'.
2
Determine the property's legal status
When debt covers the entire value, the house is effectively owned by the creditors; heirs have no ownership right.
3
Understand liability for the deficiency
The digger is liable to the creditors for the loss in value caused by digging.
This is a direct financial compensation (ḍamān).
4
Understand liability for diyah
If someone falls into the dug hole and dies, the digger's ʿāqila pays the diyah.
ʿāqila are the agnatic relatives responsible for blood money.
5
Appreciate the role of ʿāqila
The ʿāqila shares the burden of accidental killing, not the digger alone.
This is a distinctive Hanafi rule for accidental deaths.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
رجل مات وترك داراً وعليه من الدين ما يستغرق قيمتها فحفر فيها ورثته فهو ضامن لنقصان الحفر للغرماء فإن وقع فيها إنسان فعليه ضمان ذلك على عاقلته.
A man died and left a house, and upon him was debt that covers its value. So his heirs dug in it. He (the digger) is liable for the deficiency of the digging to the creditors. Then if a person falls into it, he is liable for the compensation of that on his ʿāqila.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This ruling: if a person dies with debt that exceeds the value of his estate, the property effectively belongs to the creditors. If the heirs dig a well or hole in the house, they diminish the property's value. The heir who dug is liable to the creditors for the loss in value (nusāān al-ḥafar). Additionally, if someone falls into the hole and dies, the digging heir must pay diyah on his ʿāqila (the agnatic group responsible for paying blood money). This emphasizes that the heirs have no right to alter the deceased's property when it is encumbered by debt.

Key Terms
عاقلة (ʿāqila): The group of agnatic relatives who pay diyah on behalf of a person who commits manslaughter
غرماء (ghuramā): Creditors

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the consequence if heirs dig a well in the deceased's house when the estate is insolvent and a person subsequently falls in?
A) The digger pays diyah from his own wealth
B) The digger's ʿāqila pays the diyah
C) The estate pays the diyah
D) No diyah is due because the house was inherited

Key Takeaway
Heirs who dig in the deceased's fully indebted house are liable to creditors for the loss and, if someone falls, the diyah is on the digger's ʿāqila.

Intra-Hanafi Dispute: Freed Slave Falls into His Own Well
Intra-Hanafi dispute on liability when a freed slave falls into a well he dug.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi intra-madhhab dispute regarding a freed slave who falls into a well he dug and dies, and explain the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
A slave's own act of falling breaks causation for his death — but Abu Yusuf holds the master still liable.

This lesson examines a nuanced case from the Hanafi school: a slave digs a well, is later freed by his master, then falls into that same well and dies. The two leading Hanafi imams, Abu Yusuf and Muhammad al-Shaybani, disagree on whether the master must pay the slave's value to his heirs. Their disagreement turns on how we attribute causation when the victim is also the tortfeasor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the ruling
The act: a slave digs a well (a tort) and is then freed; later he falls into the well and dies. The question: is the master liable for his value?
This is a case of overlapping liability: the slave is both the digger (cause of the pit) and the victim.
2
State each imam's position
Abu Yusuf: the master must pay the slave's value to his heirs. Muhammad: the master pays nothing.
Muhammad's reasoning: the slave's own act of falling is the direct cause of death (he is like the killer of himself), breaking the chain from the digging.
3
Extract the implicit dalil
Abu Yusuf's dalil: the master's permission to dig (implicit) and the act of freeing do not erase the liability for the well; the slave's death is indirectly caused by the master's permission. Muhammad's dalil: the slave's own volitional act of falling severs causation (like committing suicide).
Both rely on usul principles of causation (tasbib) and direct killing (mubasharah).
4
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf
Practical difference: under Abu Yusuf, the master pays the value to the slave's heirs; under Muhammad, nothing is paid. This affects the master's financial liability.
The source does not specify which is mufta bihi, but this is a legitimate ikhtilaf within the Hanafi school.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى محمد عن أبي يوسف في عبد حفر بئراً ثم أعتقه مولاه ثم وقع العبد المعتق في البئر ومات قال على المولى قيمته لورثته قال محمد: لا أرى عليه شيئاً ولو أعتقه المولى أولاً ثم حفر ووقع فيها فلا شيء على المولى بلا خلاف. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف : مكاتب حفر بئراً في الطريق ثم قتل إنساناً فقضى عليه بقيمته ثم وقع في البئر إنسان ومات قال : يشارك الساقط في البئر الذي أخذ القيمة فيها قال : وكذلك المدبر . قال: وإذا جاء ولي الساقط في البئر فأخذ الذي أخذ قيمة المدبر من مولاه لم يكن بينه وبينه خصومة ولا أقبل بينة عليه، وإنما أقبل بينة على مولى المدبر فإذا زكت كذا في المولى يرجع على الذي أخذ القيمة بنصفها. وفي التجريد ولو كان الحافر مدبراً أو أم ولد وقضى على المولى بقيمة واحدة تعتبر القيمة يوم الحفر ولا يعتبر بزيادة القيمة ونقصانها، وأما المكاتب فتلزمه الجنايات وتعتبر قيمته يوم الحفر. ولو كان الحافر عبداً فالجنايات كلها في رقبته ويخاطب المولى بالدفع أو الفداء بجميع الأروش، فإن أعتقه المولى بعد الحفر قبل الوقوع ثم لحقته الجنايات فعلى المولى قيمته يوم عتق يشترك فيها أصحاب الجنايات التي كانت بعد العتق وقبله يضرب في ذلك كل واحد بقدر أرض جنياته ولو لم يعتق ولكن وقع واحد ومات فيدفع به ثم وقع ثان وثالث فيشتركوا مع المدفوع إليه الأول في رقبته بقدر حقوقهم. ولو أن عبداً قتل إنساناً ودفعه المولى به ثم وقع إنسان في بئر كان حفرها العبد قبل ذلك عند الدافع فالعبد يدفع نصفه إلى ولي الساقط في البئر أو يفديه بالدية، ولو عفا ولي الساقط في البئر لم يدفع إلى المولى شيء من العبد ولا خصومة من هذه المسألة بين المولى الأول وإنما يخاصم الذي في يده العبد
And in al-Muntaqa, Muhammad from Abu Yusuf regarding a slave who dug a well, then his master freed him, then the freed slave (himself) fell into the well and died. He (Abu Yusuf) said: Upon the master is his value for his heirs. Muhammad said: I do not see anything upon him. And if the master freed him first, then he dug and fell, then there is nothing upon the master without disagreement. And in Nawadir Ibn Samāʿa from Abu Yusuf: A mukatab dug a well in the road, then he killed a person, so a verdict was given against him for his value, then a person fell into the well and died. He said: The one who fell into the well shares with the one who took the value. He said: And likewise the mudabbar. He said: If the guardian of the one who fell into the well comes and takes the value of the mudabbar from the master, there is no dispute between him and him, and I do not accept testimony (beyond that) against him; rather, I accept testimony against the master of the mudabbar. And if it is confirmed, then the master (who paid) takes back half from the one who took the value. And in al-Tajrid: If the digger was a mudabbar or umm walad, and the qadi imposed upon the master a single value, the value is considered on the day of digging, and increase or decrease in value is not considered. As for the mukatab, the jinayat are binding upon him and his value is considered on the day of digging. And if the digger was a slave, all the jinayat are upon his neck, and the master is addressed with handing over or ransom for all the arsh. If the master freed him after digging but before the fall, and then the jinayat catch him, then upon the master is his value on the day of freeing, and the owners of the jinayat share in it, those that occurred after freeing and before, each striking proportionally to his injury. And if he did not free, but one fell and dies, so he hands him over, then a second and third fall, then they share with the first recipient in his body proportionally to their rights. And if a slave killed a person and the master handed him over for that, then a person fell into a well that the slave had dug before that, while the slave is with the one who received him (the first recipient), then the slave is handed over half to the guardian of the one who fell into the well, or he ransoms him with diyah. And if the guardian of the one who fell into the well pardons, nothing is handed over to the master from the slave, and there is no dispute in this issue between the first master; rather, the one in whose hand the slave is (the recipient) disputes.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage collects several rulings from different sources (al-Muntaqa, Nawadir Ibn Samāʿa, al-Tajrid) concerning the liability of various types of slaves (ordinary slave, mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad) who dig wells that cause deaths. Key points: (1) If a slave digs a well, is freed, then falls into his own well and dies, Abu Yusuf says the master pays his value to the slave's heirs; Muhammad says no payment because the slave's own act caused his death (he is like the killer of himself). If freed first then digs, no liability. (2) For a mukatab dugger, his debt is bound to his person; if he also kills someone by the well, the victims share his value proportionally. (3) For a mudabbar or umm walad, the value is fixed on the day of digging, not later changes. (4) For an ordinary slave, all diyat are on his neck; if freed after digging before falls, the master pays only the day-of-freeing value, and all victims share that value proportionally. (5) If a slave is handed over for one death but another victim appears later (from a well he dug before), the current holder of the slave must give half to the new victim or pay diyah. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi approach to slave liability: the slave's body (raqabah) is the primary asset for paying diyat, and its value is shared among multiple victims in order of occurrence or proportion.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): Not directly used in this lesson.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Muhammad al-Shaybani, if a slave digs a well, is freed, then falls into it and dies, what is the ruling?
A) The master must pay the slave's value to his heirs
B) The master pays nothing
C) The master pays half the value
D) The master pays full diyah

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, when a tortfeasor becomes the victim of his own tort, the chain of liability may be broken if his own act is the direct cause of death.

Liability and Sharing for Slave-Dug Wells: Multiple Victims and Slave Statuses
Collection of rulings on sharing slave's value among multiple victims from well-digging, with variations for slave status.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rules for sharing a slave's value among multiple victims when the slave has committed multiple jinayat, and distinguish the liability rules for mudabbar, mukatab, and umm walad.

Core Idea
A slave's value is shared among victims proportionally, with the day of digging fixing the value for mudabbars and umm walad.

This lesson covers the remaining rulings from the passage: how liability is distributed when a single slave (ordinary, mudabbar, or mukatab) digs a well that kills multiple people, and how the master's payment is affected by the slave's status and the timing of freeing. The Hanafi school has detailed rules for sharing the slave's value among victims and for determining the value date.

Guided Path
1
Identify the slave's status
Determine if the digger is an ordinary slave, mudabbar, mukatab, or umm walad. Each has different rules for liability and value assessment.
Mudabbar and umm walad: value fixed at digging day. Mukatab: jinayat binding on him, value at digging day. Ordinary slave: all jinayat on his neck, value at freeing day if freed later.
2
Determine the value date
For mudabbar and umm walad, the value is considered on the day of digging, regardless of later changes. For ordinary slave freed after digging, value is on the day of freeing.
This prevents fluctuations from affecting liability.
3
Apply sharing among multiple victims
When multiple victims fall (sequentially or simultaneously), they share the slave's value proportionally. The first victim takes the slave's body, then subsequent victims share in that body proportionally to their diyat.
If the slave is already handed over for a previous death, the current holder must share half or pay diyah.
4
Handle post-digging freeing
If the master frees the slave after digging but before any fall, the master pays the day-of-freeing value, and all victims share that amount proportionally. If the slave is not freed, the slave's body is used.
If the slave is freed after some falls but before others, the victims of falls after freeing are included in the sharing.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي المنتقى محمد عن أبي يوسف في عبد حفر بئراً ثم أعتقه مولاه ثم وقع العبد المعتق في البئر ومات قال على المولى قيمته لورثته قال محمد: لا أرى عليه شيئاً ولو أعتقه المولى أولاً ثم حفر ووقع فيها فلا شيء على المولى بلا خلاف. وفي نوادر ابن سماعة عن أبي يوسف : مكاتب حفر بئراً في الطريق ثم قتل إنساناً فقضى عليه بقيمته ثم وقع في البئر إنسان ومات قال : يشارك الساقط في البئر الذي أخذ القيمة فيها قال : وكذلك المدبر . قال: وإذا جاء ولي الساقط في البئر فأخذ الذي أخذ قيمة المدبر من مولاه لم يكن بينه وبينه خصومة ولا أقبل بينة عليه، وإنما أقبل بينة على مولى المدبر فإذا زكت كذا في المولى يرجع على الذي أخذ القيمة بنصفها. وفي التجريد ولو كان الحافر مدبراً أو أم ولد وقضى على المولى بقيمة واحدة تعتبر القيمة يوم الحفر ولا يعتبر بزيادة القيمة ونقصانها، وأما المكاتب فتلزمه الجنايات وتعتبر قيمته يوم الحفر. ولو كان الحافر عبداً فالجنايات كلها في رقبته ويخاطب المولى بالدفع أو الفداء بجميع الأروش، فإن أعتقه المولى بعد الحفر قبل الوقوع ثم لحقته الجنايات فعلى المولى قيمته يوم عتق يشترك فيها أصحاب الجنايات التي كانت بعد العتق وقبله يضرب في ذلك كل واحد بقدر أرض جنياته ولو لم يعتق ولكن وقع واحد ومات فيدفع به ثم وقع ثان وثالث فيشتركوا مع المدفوع إليه الأول في رقبته بقدر حقوقهم. ولو أن عبداً قتل إنساناً ودفعه المولى به ثم وقع إنسان في بئر كان حفرها العبد قبل ذلك عند الدافع فالعبد يدفع نصفه إلى ولي الساقط في البئر أو يفديه بالدية، ولو عفا ولي الساقط في البئر لم يدفع إلى المولى شيء من العبد ولا خصومة من هذه المسألة بين المولى الأول وإنما يخاصم الذي في يده العبد
And in al-Muntaqa, Muhammad from Abu Yusuf regarding a slave who dug a well, then his master freed him, then the freed slave (himself) fell into the well and died. He (Abu Yusuf) said: Upon the master is his value for his heirs. Muhammad said: I do not see anything upon him. And if the master freed him first, then he dug and fell, then there is nothing upon the master without disagreement. And in Nawadir Ibn Samāʿa from Abu Yusuf: A mukatab dug a well in the road, then he killed a person, so a verdict was given against him for his value, then a person fell into the well and died. He said: The one who fell into the well shares with the one who took the value. He said: And likewise the mudabbar. He said: If the guardian of the one who fell into the well comes and takes the value of the mudabbar from the master, there is no dispute between him and him, and I do not accept testimony (beyond that) against him; rather, I accept testimony against the master of the mudabbar. And if it is confirmed, then the master (who paid) takes back half from the one who took the value. And in al-Tajrid: If the digger was a mudabbar or umm walad, and the qadi imposed upon the master a single value, the value is considered on the day of digging, and increase or decrease in value is not considered. As for the mukatab, the jinayat are binding upon him and his value is considered on the day of digging. And if the digger was a slave, all the jinayat are upon his neck, and the master is addressed with handing over or ransom for all the arsh. If the master freed him after digging but before the fall, and then the jinayat catch him, then upon the master is his value on the day of freeing, and the owners of the jinayat share in it, those that occurred after freeing and before, each striking proportionally to his injury. And if he did not free, but one fell and dies, so he hands him over, then a second and third fall, then they share with the first recipient in his body proportionally to their rights. And if a slave killed a person and the master handed him over for that, then a person fell into a well that the slave had dug before that, while the slave is with the one who received him (the first recipient), then the slave is handed over half to the guardian of the one who fell into the well, or he ransoms him with diyah. And if the guardian of the one who fell into the well pardons, nothing is handed over to the master from the slave, and there is no dispute in this issue between the first master; rather, the one in whose hand the slave is (the recipient) disputes.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage collects several rulings from different sources (al-Muntaqa, Nawadir Ibn Samāʿa, al-Tajrid) concerning the liability of various types of slaves (ordinary slave, mudabbar, mukatab, umm walad) who dig wells that cause deaths. Key points: (1) If a slave digs a well, is freed, then falls into his own well and dies, Abu Yusuf says the master pays his value to the slave's heirs; Muhammad says no payment because the slave's own act caused his death (he is like the killer of himself). If freed first then digs, no liability. (2) For a mukatab dugger, his debt is bound to his person; if he also kills someone by the well, the victims share his value proportionally. (3) For a mudabbar or umm walad, the value is fixed on the day of digging, not later changes. (4) For an ordinary slave, all diyat are on his neck; if freed after digging before falls, the master pays only the day-of-freeing value, and all victims share that value proportionally. (5) If a slave is handed over for one death but another victim appears later (from a well he dug before), the current holder of the slave must give half to the new victim or pay diyah. These rulings illustrate the Hanafi approach to slave liability: the slave's body (raqabah) is the primary asset for paying diyat, and its value is shared among multiple victims in order of occurrence or proportion.

Key Terms
مدبر (mudabbar): A slave whose master has declared that he will be free upon the master's death
مكاتب (mukātab): A slave who has contracted with his master for emancipation upon payment of a sum
أم ولد (umm walad): A slave woman who has borne a child to her master

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
For a mudabbar who digs a well, what date is used to assess his value for liability purposes if multiple falls occur?
A) The day of the first fall
B) The day of digging
C) The day of the last fall
D) The day the master dies

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, when a slave's torts produce multiple victims, the slave's value is shared proportionally, with the assessment date depending on the slave's status.
