Liability for Digging in the Public Market: With and Without Ruler's Permission
States that digging in the public market with the ruler's permission absolves liability, without it imposes liability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the liability rule for altering a public market with and without the ruler's permission, and explain the legal rationale.

Core Idea
The ruler's permission removes public status; without it, liability for harm remains.

In Hanafi fiqh, public markets and roads are under the ruler's authority. This passage from al-Khāniyya clarifies a key rule: if you dig a well or build a shop with the ruler's permission, you are not liable for resulting accidents; without such permission, you bear full liability. This distinction rests on the ruler's ability to re-designate public space.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
What is the act (digging well, building shop, stationing animal)? What is the ruling (no liability with permission, liability without)?
2
Identify the key condition
What condition changes the ruling? (Permission of the ruler).
3
Understand the rationale
Why does permission change liability? (Because the spot exits from being a public road).
4
Apply the analogy
How does the same rule apply to stationing an animal in a designated spot? (Same condition: with permission, no liability).
5
Recognize the authority
What is the source of this rule? (al-Khāniyya, a Hanafi text).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الخانية ولو أن رجلاً حفر بئراً في سوق العامة أو بنى فيه دكاناً فعطب به شيء، فإن فعل ذلك بأذن الإمام لا يكون ضامناً، وبغير إذنه يكون ضامناً كما لو أوقف دابته في السوق في موضع معد للدابة فأوقف الدابة في ذلك الموضع إن عينوا ذلك الموضع بإذن السلطان فعطب لا يكون ضماناً. وإن لم يكن بإذن السلطان كان ضامناً لأن السلطان إذا أذن بذلك يخرج ذلك الموضع عن أن يكون طريقاً فتعين لإيقاف الدواب، وبغير إذن السلطان لا يخرج من أن يكون طريقاً.
And in al-Khāniyya: If a man digs a well in the general market (sūq al-ʿāmma) or builds a shop there, and something perishes by it, then if he does that with the permission of the Imam, he is not liable; without his permission, he is liable. Similarly, if he stations his beast in the market in a place designated for beasts, and he stations the beast in that place, if they designated that place with the permission of the sultan, and something perishes, there is no liability. And if without the permission of the sultan, he is liable, because if the sultan permits that, that place exits from being a road and becomes designated for stationing beasts; without the sultan's permission, it does not exit from being a road.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
This ruling from al-Khāniyya clarifies that public roads and markets are under the authority of the ruler (Imām or Sulṭān). If he permits a specific spot to be used for a well or shop, that spot is no longer considered part of the public passage; hence, the digger is not liable for accidents. Without such permission, the spot remains a public road, and any obstruction causing harm makes the digger liable. The same applies to tying animals in designated spots: the ruler's designation changes the legal status of the place.

Key Terms
سوق العامة (sūq al-ʿāmma): Public market where people gather

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a man digs a well in the public market without the ruler's permission and a person is injured?
A) He is not liable because the market is for public use
B) He is liable because the spot remains a public road
C) He is liable only if the victim was a fellow merchant
D) He is liable only if he did not post a warning

Key Takeaway
Permission of the ruler transforms the legal status of a public space, shifting liability away from the person who alters it.

Liability for Damage from Digging a Canal and Watering from a Public River
States liability for digging a canal on non-owned land and watering from a public river.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the conditions under which a person is liable for damage caused by digging a canal or watering from a public river, and distinguish between ownership of land as a factor in liability.

Core Idea
You are liable for damage caused by your actions on non-owned land or public resources, even if you are using your own property, if the harm is foreseeable.

This lesson presents two scenarios from Hanafi fiqh that clarify liability for torts arising from water management. The first scenario deals with digging a canal on land that is not your own, while the second involves watering your land from a public river and inadvertently flooding others. Understanding these rulings helps you apply the principle that responsible use of property and shared resources is key to avoiding liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its location
Determine whether the act (digging or watering) occurs on one's own property, non-owned land, or a public resource like a river.
2
Determine the causal outcome
Assess whether the act caused damage (flooding or spoiling land) through water flow.
3
Apply the rule of liability
If the act was on non-owned land or involved a public river, and damage resulted, the person is liable. If on own property, no liability even if damage occurs.
4
Consider indirect causation
In the second scenario, note that liability arises even though the damage was 'downstream' – the person caused the water to flow into the small canals by irrigating.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإذا حفر الرجل نهراً في غير ملكه فأكسر من ذلك النهر ماء يغرق أرضاً أو قرية كان ضامناً، ولو في ملکه فلا ضمان. رجل سقى أرضه من نهر العامة وكان على نهر العامة أنهار صغار مفتوحة فوهاتها ودخل الماء في الأنهار الصغار وفسد بذلك أرض قوم. قال شيخ الإسلام الأجل ظهير الدين يكون ضامناً لأنه أجرى الماء فيها .
And if a man digs a canal on non-owned land, and from that canal water bursts forth and floods land or a village, he is liable. And if (it is) on his own property, there is no liability. A man watered his land from the public river, and along the public river there were small canals with open mouths, and water entered the small canals and thereby spoilt the land of some people. The Great Shaykh al-Islām Ẓahīr al-Dīn said: He is liable because he caused the water to flow into them.
Source pages p. 116

Commentary
Two rulings: (1) Digging a canal on another's land or common land that causes flooding makes the digger liable; but if on his own land, no liability because he has the right to use his property. (2) A person waters his land from a public river, but there are open small canals branching off; the water flows into them and damages neighboring lands. According to Shaykh al-Islām Ẓahīr al-Dīn (a prominent Hanafi jurist), the person is liable because he caused the water to flow (ajrā al-māʾa) by irrigating, even indirectly. This highlights the principle that one is responsible for the foreseeable consequences of one's actions on public resources.

Key Terms
نهر العامة (nahr al-ʿāmma): Public river accessible to all

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the ruling if a man digs a canal on his own property and water overflows and floods a neighbor's land?
A) He is liable for all damages
B) He is not liable because it is his property
C) He is liable only if the flood was intentional
D) He is liable only if the neighbor warned him

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, liability for water damage hinges on whether the act was on one's own property (no liability) or on non-owned/public property (liability for damages), and on whether the person directly caused the water to flow into harm's way.

Liability for Obstructions in the Road: Daman and Ta'addi
Rulings on civil liability for road obstructions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule of ta'addi to determine whether an act in the road creates liability (daman), and distinguish between transgressive and permissible uses of public and shared spaces.

Core Idea
Liability in public roads hinges on ta'addi: creating a hazard is transgressive; removing harm or using one's own property is not.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq expands on the Matn of Kanz al-Daqa'iq, detailing when a person is responsible for harm caused by objects or actions in the road. The key concept is transgression (ta'addi): if your act creates a new danger beyond what is normally allowed, you are liable. However, certain actions, like sweeping or using a dead-end alley for residence necessities, are excused. The commentator also introduces the principle of istihsan to shift liability to the one who benefits.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its location
Determine what was done (e.g., placed a stone, poured water) and where (public road, dead-end alley, joint house).
Location affects whether the act is considered transgressive.
2
Check for ta'addi (transgression)
Did the act exceed permissible limits? Consider if it created a new hazard or was a normal use of the space.
3
Apply the default ruling
If ta'addi exists, daman (liability) is wajib; if not, there is no daman.
4
Note special distinctions
For example, sweeping is harmless unless dirt is piled; moving an object shifts liability; excessive water vs. normal water; deliberate victim assumption of risk.
5
Recognize the role of istihsan
In the shop forecourt case, liability shifts from the doer to the orderer due to benefit and permission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : ولو بهيمة فضمانها في (ماله أي لو كان الهالك في البئر أو بسقوط الجرصن بهيمة يكون ضمانها في ماله لأن العاقلة لا تتحمل ضمان المال وإبقاء الميزاب واتخاذ الطين في الطريق بمنزلة إلقاء الحجر والخشبة لأن كل واحد من ذلك مسبب بطريق من التعدي بخلاف ما إذا كان في ملكه لعدم التعدي وبخلاف ما إذا كنس الطريق فعطب بموضع كنسه إنسان حيث لم يضمن لأنه ليس بمتعد فيه لأنه لم يحدث فيه شيئاً وإنما قصد إماطة الأذى عن الطريق حتى لو جمع الكناسة في الطريق فعطب بها إنسان ضمن لوجود التعدي بشغله الطريق. ولو وضع حجراً فنحاه غيره عن موضعه فتلف به نفس أو مال كان ضمانه على من نحاه لأن فعل الأول قد انتسخ، وكذا إذا صب الماء في الطريق أو رش أو توضأ فعطب به نفس أو مال يضمن لأنه متعد فيه بخلاف ما إذا فعل ذلك في سكة غير نافذة وهو من أهلها أو قعد فيه أو وضع خشبة أو متاعة لأن الكل واحد من أهله أن يفعل ذلك لكونه من ضرورات السكن كما في الدار المشتركة بخلاف الحفر لأنه ليس من ضرورات السكن فيضمن ما عطب به كالدار المشتركة غير أنه لا يضمن في السكة ما نقص بالحفر، وفي الدار المشتركة يضمن لأن الشريكه ملكاً حقيقة في الدار حتى يبيع نصيبه ويقسم بخلاف السكة قالوا : هذا إذا رش ماء كثيراً بحيث يزلق منه عادة، وأما إذا لم يجاوز المعتاد لا يضمن . ولو تعمد المرور في موضع الصب مع علمه به يضمن الراش لأنه هو الذي خاطر بنفسه فصار كمن وثب في الطريق من جانب إلى جانب فوقع فيها بخلاف ما إذا كان بغير علمه بأن كان ليلاً أو أعمى وقيل يضمن مع العلم أيضاً إذا رش جميع الطريق لأنه مضطر إلى المرور فيه، وكذا الحكم في الخشبة الموضوعة في الطريق في جميع أجزاء الطريق أو بعضه، ولو رش فناء حانوت بإذن صاحبه فضمان ما عطب على الآمر استحساناً.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: And if (it is) a beast, its guarantee is in his wealth (i.e., if the one killed is in the well or by the fall of a jar on a beast, its guarantee is in his wealth because the 'aqila does not bear guarantee of property.) And leaving a waterspout and making mud in the road are like throwing a stone or a beam because each of these is a cause by way of transgression, contrary to when it is in his property due to lack of transgression, and contrary to when he sweeps the road and a person is injured at the place of his sweeping, where he is not liable because he is not transgressing in it; he did not create anything, rather he intended to remove harm from the road. Even if he collects the dirt in the road and a person is injured by it, he is liable because transgression exists by occupying the road. And if he places a stone and another moves it from its place, and a life or property is damaged, the guarantee is on the one who moved it because the act of the first is abrogated. Similarly, if he pours water in the road, or sprinkles, or performs wudu', and a life or property is damaged by it, he is liable because he is transgressing in it, contrary to if he does that in a dead-end alley and he is one of its residents, or sits in it, or places a beam or goods, because all of these are permissible for its residents to do due to necessity of residence, like in a jointly owned house, contrary to digging because it is not a necessity of residence, so he is liable for what is damaged by it, like the jointly owned house. However, he is not liable in the alley for what is damaged by digging, while in a jointly owned house he is liable because the partner owns genuine ownership in the house so that he sells his share and divides, unlike the alley. They said: This is if he sprinkles a lot of water such that slipping is customary from it; but if it does not exceed the usual, he is not liable. And if he deliberately passes through the place of sprinkling with knowledge of it, the sprinkler is liable because he (the passer) risked himself, thus it is like one who jumps in the road from one side to the other and falls; contrary to if it is without his knowledge, such as if nighttime or blind. And it is said: He is liable even with knowledge if he sprinkles the entire road because he is compelled to pass through it. And the same ruling applies to a beam placed in the road, whether in all parts of the road or part of it. And if he waters the courtyard of a shop with the owner's permission, the guarantee of what is damaged is upon the orderer (the shop owner) by istihsan.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage is a set of rulings from the commentary on Kanz al-Daqa'iq concerning liability (daman) for various acts performed in public roads. The author, Ibn Nujaym, expands on the Matn's ruling about a beast falling into a well or being hit by a falling jar: the liability falls on the one who dug the well or caused the jar to fall, but it is in his own wealth (mal), not on his 'aqila (tribal guarantors), because the 'aqila only bears diyah for homicide, not property damage. The author then analogizes: leaving a waterspout (mizab) protruding or piling mud in the road is equivalent to throwing a stone or beam in the road—each is an act of transgression (ta'addi) because it creates a hazard. However, if the act is done on one's own property, there is no transgression. Similarly, sweeping the road does not make one liable because it is removal of harm, not creation. But if one collects the sweepings into a pile in the road and someone is injured, he is liable because he is occupying the road. If A places a stone and B moves it elsewhere, then B is liable for any harm because A's act is 'abrogated' (intasakha). Pouring water, sprinkling, or performing wudu' in the road causing injury makes one liable due to transgression, unless done in a dead-end alley (sikka ghayr nafidha) by a resident, as residents have the right to such acts for dwelling necessities, like in a jointly owned house. However, digging is not a necessity, so one is liable for harm caused by a hole dug in an alley. But note: in an alley, one is not liable for the reduction in value caused by digging (because no property right), whereas in a jointly owned house one is liable because the partner owns a share. The commentator distinguishes between normal and excessive sprinkling: only excessive amounts causing slipping incur liability. If the victim deliberately passes through with knowledge, he is considered to have risked himself (khatara bi-nafsihi), so the sprinkler is not liable—unless the road is entirely wet, making passage necessary. The same rules apply to a beam placed across the road. Finally, if someone waters a shop's forecourt with the owner's permission, the liability falls on the orderer (the shop owner) by istihsan (juristic preference), meaning the worker is not liable even though he did the act, because the owner is the one who derived benefit and gave permission.

Key Terms
ضمان (daman): Liability/guarantee; the obligation to compensate for harm caused to another's life or property due to transgression.
تعدي (ta'addi): Transgression; acting in a way that exceeds lawful boundaries, thereby creating liability.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of departing from strict analogy (qiyas) for a more equitable ruling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, if someone sweeps the road and collects the dirt into a pile, and a person is injured by tripping on that pile, what is the ruling?
A) No liability because sweeping is removal of harm.
B) Liability because the sweeper created a new obstruction (ta'addi) by collecting dirt in the road.
C) Liability only if the sweeper was warned not to do so.
D) No liability because the road is public property.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle of ta'addi determines liability in public roads: creating an unreasonable hazard obligates compensation; acts of maintenance or normal residential use do not.

Liability for Road Obstructions: The Mutasabbib-Mubashir Distinction
Ruling on liability for road obstructions with deliberate victim action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on liability when a person builds a drain, places a beam, or constructs a bridge, and another person deliberately passes over it and is harmed, and explain the reasoning based on the concepts of mutasabbib (indirect cause) and mubashir (direct actor).

Core Idea
The deliberate act of the passer makes him the direct actor, cutting off liability from the indirect cause.

This passage from Al-Bahr al-Rā’iq addresses situations where a person builds a drain by the sultan’s order or on his own land, places a beam in a public road, or builds a bridge without the imam’s permission. The key question: who is liable if someone passes over it and is harmed? The Hanafi ruling hinges on whether the victim acted deliberately, based on the fundamental distinction between a direct actor (mubāshir) and an indirect cause (mutasabbib).

Guided Path
1
Identify the three scenarios and their permission status
List each scenario: (1) drain built by sultan’s order or on own property, (2) beam placed in the road, (3) bridge built without imam’s permission. Note that the drain and beam cases involve no transgression (ta‘addī), while the bridge case does involve transgression because the builder forfeited the imam’s right to decide location.
The permission status affects whether there is initial wrongdoing, but the liability question ultimately depends on the victim’s action.
2
State the ruling for each scenario
In all three scenarios, if a person deliberately passes over the structure and is harmed, the builder/placer is not liable (lā yadman). This is the unified ruling despite differences in permission.
3
Understand the reasoning: mutasabbib vs. mubashir
The commentator explains that the placer is a mutasabbib (indirect cause) while the passer is a mubāshir (direct actor). The passer’s deliberate action makes him the ‘possessor of the cause’ (sāḥib al-‘illa), so his own action severs the causal link to the original tortfeasor.
This principle is established elsewhere in the Hanafi fiqh of causation: the direct actor overrides the indirect cause.
4
Note the practical consequence and cross-madhhab difference
The Hanafi position means that if a victim knowingly and deliberately uses an unauthorized structure, he bears the risk. Other schools may hold the builder liable regardless, making this a point of ikhtilāf.
The passage ends by referring to prior explanation, indicating this is a well-settled Hanafi rule.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله : (ومن جعل بالوعة في طريق بأمر السلطان أو في ملكه أو وضع خشبة فيها أي في الطريق (أو قنطرة بلا إذن الإمام فتعمد الرجل المرور عليها لم يضمن أما بناء البالوعة بأمر الأمام أو في ملكه ووضع الخشبة فلأنه ليس بمتعد، وأما بناء القنطرة فلأن الباني فوت حقاً على غيره فإن التدبير في وضع القنطرة من حيث تعيين المكان للإمام فكانت جناية بهذا الاعتبار فتعمد رجل المرور عليها لم يضمن ووضع الخشبة والقنطرة وإن وجد التعدي منه فيهما لكن تعمده المرور عليهما يسقط النسبة إلى الواضع لأن الواضع متسبب والمار مباشر فصار هو صاحب علة فلا يعتبر التسبب معه وقد بيناه فيما مضى.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: (And whoever makes a drain in the road by order of the sultan or in his own property, or places a beam in it (i.e., the road), or a bridge without the permission of the imam, and a man deliberately passes over it, he is not liable. As for building a drain by order of the imam or in his own property, and placing a beam, it is because he is not a transgressor. As for building a bridge, it is because the builder forfeited a right of others; the management of placing the bridge in terms of specifying the location belongs to the imam, so it was an injury (jinaya) in this regard. So if a man deliberately passes over it, he is not liable. And placing a beam and a bridge, even if transgression exists from him in both, his (the passer) deliberate passage drops ascription to the placer because the placer is a cause (mutasabbib) and the passer is a direct actor (mubashir); so he (the passer) becomes the possessor of the cause, and the causation is not considered with him. And we have explained this previously.
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This passage continues the discussion on liability for objects placed in the road. It specifically addresses three scenarios: (1) a drain (balu'a) built by order of the sultan or on one's own property, (2) a beam placed in the road, (3) a bridge (qantara) built without the imam's permission. In all three, if a person deliberately chooses to pass over these structures and is harmed, the original placer/builder is not liable. The reasoning differs: for the drain and beam, if done with permission or on own property, there is no transgression (ta'addi) in the first place. For the bridge, even if there is transgression (because the imam had the authority to decide its location), the passer's deliberate act of passing is considered a direct intervention (mubasharah) that cuts off causal connection to the builder. The builder is only a cause (mutasabbib), while the passer becomes the direct actor. This principle is earlier established in the text: when a person directly causes his own harm, the original tortfeasor is not liable.

Key Terms
متسبب (mutasabbib): A person who creates a cause of harm indirectly, as opposed to a direct actor (mubashir).
مباشر (mubashir): A person who directly commits an act causing harm, thus the primary liable party.
جناية (jinaya): An injurious act; typically refers to a tort or crime against the person.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why is a person who builds a bridge without the imam's permission not liable when someone deliberately passes over it and falls?
A) Because the builder obtained permission from the sultan.
B) Because the passer is a direct actor (mubashir), breaking the causal link to the builder's act.
C) Because the bridge is not considered a hazard.
D) Because the builder owns the bridge.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, a deliberate intervening act of the victim severs the causal link to the original tortfeasor, who is only an indirect cause.

Liability When Hiring Workers to Dig: Knowledge, Deception, and Public Roads
Ruling on who bears liability when hired laborers dig in someone else's property or the road.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply Hanafi rules of liability (daman) to cases where hired workers cause harm while digging, distinguish between worker knowledge and ignorance, and evaluate the role of ghurur and istihsan.

Core Idea
Liability shifts to the hirer when workers are deceived; if workers know, they bear liability — with istihsan exceptions for apparent control.

This passage from al-Bahr al-Ra'iq addresses a practical question: when someone hires workers to dig on property that is not his own, who is liable for resulting harm or death? The Hanafi school provides a nuanced answer based on the worker's knowledge, the hirer's deception, and the nature of the property. By working through the text, you will learn the core rule, its exceptions, and how later fatwa authorities applied it to public roads, direct acts, and special cases involving slaves.

Guided Path
1
Identify the basic rule
The text begins with a clear statement: if workers do not know the land is not the hirer's, liability is on the hirer. If they know, it is on the workers. Note the key concept: ghurur (deception).
The ruling applies to property damage, injury, or death caused by the digging.
2
Analyze the analogy with sheep slaughter
The author compares to a hired slaughterer who kills a sheep belonging to another. The slaughterer is liable and then has recourse against the hirer if deceived. Here, the hirer is directly liable because he is the transgressor (muta'addi).
This analogy (qiyas) is overridden by istihsan in some cases, but illustrates the principle of ghurur.
3
Examine the istihsan exception
Even if workers know, if the hirer has apparent control over the place (e.g., a courtyard he uses as his own), liability reverts to him by istihsan over qiyas. Understand the reasoning: the hirer's de facto possession makes his command appear valid.
Istihsan considers the practical reality: the hirer's free disposal of the place (throwing mud, tying animals) makes it like his property.
4
Apply Shaykh al-Islam's distinction for public roads
For well-known public roads, workers are liable regardless of knowledge. For obscure roads, if the hirer informed them, they are liable; if not, he is liable.
This distinction reflects whether the workers could have known the road is public.
5
Extract rulings from later fatwa works
The text cites al-Fatawa al-Khalasa, al-Sighnaqi, and al-Muntaqa, adding nuances like direct acts (brick falling from hand) and victim's own risk (jumping over well). Recognize that the hirer is not liable if the victim's own action caused the injury.
These later authorities refine and specify the basic rule.
6
Note the special case of slaves
Abu Yusuf's report: if a hired slave digs in a wrong place, liability is on the slave's person (value), regardless of his knowledge. For a mukatab or restricted slave, the hirer is liable for the free person's death but not for the mukatab's death, and must pay the slave's value.
The slave's own liability is limited to his value (raqaba).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واستأجر أجراء يحفرون له في غير فنائه فضمانه على المستأجر ولا شيء على الآجر إن لم يعلموا أنه في غير فنائه لأن أمره قد صح إذا لم يعلموا فنقل فعلهم إلى الآمر لأنهم مغرورون من جهته فصار كما إذا أمر أجيراً بذبح هذه الشاة فذبحها ثم ظهر أن الشاة لغيره يضمن المأمور ويرجع به على الآمر لكونه مغروراً من جهته، وهنا يجب الضمان على المستأجر ابتداء لأن كل واحد منهما متسبب والأجير غير متعد والمستأجر متعد فترجح جانبه ، فإن علموا بذلك فالضمان على الآجر لأن أمره لم يصح لأنه لا يملك أن يفعل بنفسه ولا غرور من جهته لعلمهم بذلك فبقي الفعل مضافاً إليهم، ولو قال لهم هذا فنائي وليس لي حق الحفر فيه فحفروا فمات فيه إنسان فالضمان على الأجراء قياساً لأنهم علموا بفساد الأمر فلم يغرهم. وفي الاستحسان الضمان على المستأجر لأن كونه فناء لهم بمنزلة كونه مملوكاً له لانطلاق يده بالتصرف فيه من إلقاء الطين والحطب وربط الدابة والركوب وبناء الدكان فكان أمراً بالحفر في ملكه ظاهراً بالنظر إلى ما ذكرنا فكذا ينقل إليه. وقال شيخ الإسلام إذا كان الطريق معروفاً أنه للعامة ضمنوا سواء قال لهم أو لا، وإذا استأجر الرجل أجيراً ليحفر له بئراً فحفر له الأجير ووقع فيها إنسان ومات فهذا على وجهين : الأول أن يستأجر الأجير ليحفر له بئراً في الطريق فإنه على وجهين: الأول أن يكون طريقاً معروفاً لعامة المسلمين يعرفه كل أحد وفي هذا الوجه يجب الضمان على الأخير سواء علمه المستأجر بذلك أو لم يعلمه وإن كان الطريق لعامة المسلمين إلا أنه طريق غير مشهور، فإن أعلم المستأجر الأجير بأن هذا الطريق لعامة المسلمين فكذا الجواب أيضاً، فأما إذا لم يعلم فالضمان على الآمر لا على الأجير، وهذا بخلاف ما لو استأجر أجيراً لذبح شاة فذبحها ثم علم أن الشاة لغير الآمر فإن الضمان على الأجير، أعلمه المستأجر بأن الشاة لغيره أو لم يعلمه، ثم يرجع إذا لم يعلم الوجه الثاني إذا استأجره ليحفر له بئراً في الفناء وقد تقدم بيانه، وفي الفتاوى الخلاصة : إذا استأجر رجلاً ليبني له أو ليحدث له شيئاً في الطريق أو يخرج حائطاً فما عطب به من نفس أو مال فذلك على المستأجر دون الأجير استحساناً إلا إذا سقط من يده لبن فأصاب إنساناً فقتله تجب الدية على عاقلة الذي سقط من يده وعليه الكفارة، وفي السغناقي : من حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق فجاء آخر وخاطر بنفسه ووثب من أحد الجانبين إلى الجانب الآخر ووقع فيه ومات لم يضمن الحافر شيئاً. وفي المنتقى : رجل جاء بقوم إلى طريق من طرق المسلمين وقال احفروا إلى هنا بئراً أو قال ابنوا لي هنا ولم يقل غيره فإن ضمان ما عطب به من ذلك على الآمر دون الفاعل ذكر المسألة مطلقاً، وتأويلها ما إذا لم يكن الطريق مشهوراً لعامة المسلمين ولم يعلمه المستأجر بذلك كما ذكر شيخ الإسلام، وذكر عقيب هذه المسألة : رجل جاء لقوم وقال احفروا في هذا الطريق بئراً ولم يقل لي ولم يقل أستأجر على ذلك وظنوا أنه الآمر ، وكذلك لو أدخلهم داراً وقال لهم احفروا فيها فحفروا ظنوا أنها دار الآمر فهو على أن يقول إن استأجرهم على ذلك. وذكر بعد هذا بشر بن الوليد عن أبي يوسف : رجل استأجر رجلاً فحفر له في غير فنائه فالضمان في رقبة العبد، علم العبد بذلك أم لا . ولو استأجر مكاتباً أو عبداً محجوراً عليه لحفر بئر فوقعت البئر عليهما وماتا فالضمان على المستأجر في الحر لا في المكاتب ويضمن قيمة العبد لمولاه، فإذا
And if he hires workers to dig for him in other than his courtyard, then the guarantee is upon the hirer (musta'jir) and nothing upon the wage workers (ujara) if they do not know that it is other than his courtyard, because his command is valid when they do not know, so their action is transferred to the commander (the hirer) because they are deceived (maghrurun) by him. Thus it is as if he commands a hired [worker] to slaughter this sheep, and he slaughters it, then it becomes clear that the sheep belongs to another: the commanded one (the worker) guarantees, and then he has recourse against the commander because he was deceived by him. Here, however, the guarantee is upon the hirer from the beginning because each of them is a cause (mutasabbib), but the worker is not transgressing (ghayr muta'addi) while the hirer is transgressing (muta'addi), so his side preponderates. If they (the workers) know that, then the guarantee is upon the wage workers because his command was not valid (since he cannot do it himself) and there is no deception from him due to their knowledge, so the act remains attributed to them. And if he says to them: 'This is my courtyard, but I have no right to dig in it,' and they dig, and a person dies by it, then the guarantee is upon the workers by qiyas because they knew the command was invalid, so he did not deceive them. But by istihsan, the guarantee is upon the hirer because his possession of the courtyard is like ownership (milk) due to his freedom to dispose of it by throwing mud, firewood, tying animals, riding, and building a shop; so his command to dig in his apparent property (by what we mentioned) transfers liability to him. And Shaykh al-Islam said: If the road is known to be public, they (the workers) are liable whether he informed them or not. And if a man hires a worker to dig a well for him, and the worker digs, and a person falls into it and dies, then this has two aspects: The first is that he hires the worker to dig a well in the road. Then it has two further aspects: (i) the road is well-known as public – then the guarantee is upon the worker (the later one? actually 'al-akhir' perhaps means the hirer? Text problematic) – both if the hirer knew or not; (ii) if the road is public but not well-known, then if the hirer informed the worker it is public, the answer is similar; if he did not inform, then the guarantee is upon the commander (hirer), not the worker. And this differs from the case of hiring a worker to slaughter a sheep and it turns out not to be the hirer's: there, the guarantee is upon the worker, whether the hirer informed him or not, but the worker can then recourse if he was not informed. The second aspect is when he hires him to dig a well in the courtyard, which was previously explained. And in al-Fatawa al-Khalasa: If a man hires a man to build for him or to create something for him in the road or to extend a wall, then whatever is damaged of life or property, that is upon the hirer, not the worker, by istihsan – unless a brick falls from his hand and hits a person and kills him, then the diyah is upon the 'aqila of the one from whose hand it fell, and upon him the kaffara. And in al-Sighnaqi: Whoever digs a well on the main road, and another comes and risks himself and jumps from one side to the other and falls into it and dies, the digger does not guarantee anything. And in al-Muntaqa: A man brings a group to a road of the Muslims and says 'dig here a well' or 'build here for me' and says nothing else, then the guarantee of whatever is damaged from that is upon the commander (al-amir), not the doer (al-fa'il). The mas'ala is mentioned absolutely, but its interpretation is when the road is not well-known as public and the hirer did not inform them, as mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam. And after this mas'ala it mentions: A man comes to a group and says 'dig in this road a well' and does not say 'for me' nor does he hire them for that, and they think that he is the commander. Similarly, if he brings them into a house and says 'dig in it' and they dig thinking it is the commander's house, then it depends on whether he hired them for that. And after this, Bishr ibn al-Walid reports from Abu Yusuf: A man hires a man and he digs for him in other than his courtyard, then the guarantee is upon the slave's person (raqaba), whether the slave knew or not. And if he hires a mukatab or a restricted slave to dig a well, and the well falls upon both and they die, then the guarantee is upon the hirer for the free man (the mukatab?) – no, for the free man (al-hurr) not for the mukatab, and he guarantees the value of the slave to his master. Then... (text continues but we break here).
Source pages p. 117

Commentary
This lengthy passage addresses the liability when someone hires workers to dig on property that is not the hirer's own (e.g., in a public road or another's courtyard). The central principle is that if the workers are unaware of the trespass, the hirer (musta'jir) bears the liability. They are 'deceived' (maghrurun) by the hirer, so their act is attributed to him. The analogy is drawn to hiring someone to slaughter a sheep that later turns out to belong to someone else: there, the slaughterer is liable and then has recourse against the hirer if he was deceived. But here, the Hanafi position is that the hirer is directly liable from the start, because both are causes but the hirer is the transgressor while the worker is not. If the workers know it is not the hirer's property, then the workers are liable because the hirer's command is invalid (he cannot authorize trespass) and there is no deception. However, by istihsan, even if the workers know, if the hirer has apparent control over the place (like a courtyard that he uses as if it were his own), the liability still falls on the hirer. Shaykh al-Islam's view distinguishes between well-known public roads and obscure ones: for well-known public roads, workers are liable regardless of knowledge; for obscure ones, liability depends on whether the hirer informed them. The passage also collects rulings from later fatwa works: al-Fatawa al-Khalasa, al-Sighnaqi, and al-Muntaqa. These include exceptions for direct acts (like a brick falling from a worker's hand, where the worker is directly liable) and the case of deliberate risk-taking by the victim (jumping over a well). Finally, a report from Abu Yusuf through Bishr ibn al-Walid deals with slaves: if a hired slave digs in a wrong place, the liability is on the slave's person (i.e., his value), regardless of his knowledge. If a mukatab or enslaved worker dies in the well, the hirer is liable for the free man but not for the mukatab (since he is partly free), and must pay the slave's value to his master.

Key Terms
أجير (ajir): A hired worker; in this context, a person hired to perform a specific task like digging.
مستأجر (musta'jir): The person who hires an ajir; the employer.
غرور (ghurur): Deception; the state where the worker is misled by the hirer's assertion that he owns or has the right to the property.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi ruling in this passage, if a man hires workers to dig in a place that is not his property, and the workers are unaware that it does not belong to him, who is initially liable for any harm caused?
A) The workers
B) The hirer
C) Both equally
D) The property owner

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi tort law, liability for hired workers' digging hinges on the worker's knowledge and the hirer's deception; istihsan often shifts liability to the hirer, balancing fault and cause.

Diyat — Blood Money

Liability for Hired Road Work: The General Rule and Exception
Rule from al-Fatawa al-Khalasa on liability for hired road building.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule that the hirer is liable for damage from hired road work by istihsan, and identify the exception when the worker's direct act (e.g., dropping a brick) shifts liability to the worker.

Core Idea
The hirer bears liability for damage from hired work on the road, but a worker's direct act makes him liable.

This passage from al-Fatawa al-Khalasa refines the earlier ruling on liability for damage from building or digging on public roads. By default, the hirer (musta'jir) is responsible for any harm to life or property arising from the hired work, based on istihsan (juristic preference). However, an important exception arises when the damage is caused by a direct physical act of the worker, such as a brick falling from his hand. In that case, the worker bears the liability: his 'aqila must pay the diyah (blood money), and he must perform kaffara (expiation). This nuance shows how later Hanafi jurists carefully distinguished between general risks inherent in the hired task and specific, avoidable acts of the worker.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general ruling
Read the first part of the source: the hirer is liable for all damage (life or property) from building, road work, or wall extension. Note that this is established by istihsan, not by strict analogy.
The Arabic phrase 'استحساناً' indicates that the ruling derives from istihsan, a key Hanafi principle.
2
Locate the exception
Find the clause beginning with 'إلا إذا سقط من يده لبن'. This exception covers a brick falling from the worker's hand and causing death.
The exception is triggered by a direct, specific act of the worker during the work.
3
Understand the outcomes of the exception
Two obligations arise: (1) diyah (blood money) is due from the worker's 'aqila (the male relatives who share blood-money liability), and (2) the worker himself must pay kaffara (expiation, usually fasting two months or feeding the poor).
The 'aqila is a pre-modern insurance mechanism; in modern contexts, diyah may be paid by insurance or the worker directly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي الفتاوى الخلاصة : إذا استأجر رجلاً ليبني له أو ليحدث له شيئاً في الطريق أو يخرج حائطاً فما عطب به من نفس أو مال فذلك على المستأجر دون الأجير استحساناً إلا إذا سقط من يده لبن فأصاب إنساناً فقتله تجب الدية على عاقلة الذي سقط من يده وعليه الكفارة، وفي السغناقي : من حفر بئراً على قارعة الطريق فجاء آخر وخاطر بنفسه ووثب من أحد الجانبين إلى الجانب الآخر ووقع فيه ومات لم يضمن الحافر شيئاً. وفي المنتقى : رجل جاء بقوم إلى طريق من طرق المسلمين وقال احفروا إلى هنا بئراً أو قال ابنوا لي هنا ولم يقل غيره فإن ضمان ما عطب به من ذلك على الآمر دون الفاعل ذكر المسألة مطلقاً، وتأويلها ما إذا لم يكن الطريق مشهوراً لعامة المسلمين ولم يعلمه المستأجر بذلك كما ذكر شيخ الإسلام، وذكر عقيب هذه المسألة : رجل جاء لقوم وقال احفروا في هذا الطريق بئراً ولم يقل لي ولم يقل أستأجر على ذلك وظنوا أنه الآمر ، وكذلك لو أدخلهم داراً وقال لهم احفروا فيها فحفروا ظنوا أنها دار الآمر فهو على أن يقول إن استأجرهم على ذلك.
And in al-Fatawa al-Khalasa: If a man hires a man to build for him or to create something for him in the road or to extend a wall, then whatever is damaged of life or property, that is upon the hirer, not the worker, by istihsan – unless a brick falls from his hand and hits a person and kills him, then the diyah is upon the 'aqila of the one from whose hand it fell, and upon him the kaffara. And in al-Sighnaqi: Whoever digs a well on the main road, and another comes and risks himself and jumps from one side to the other and falls into it and dies, the digger does not guarantee anything. And in al-Muntaqa: A man brings a group to a road of the Muslims and says 'dig here a well' or 'build here for me' and says nothing else, then the guarantee of whatever is damaged from that is upon the commander (al-amir), not the doer (al-fa'il). The mas'ala is mentioned absolutely, but its interpretation is when the road is not well-known as public and the hirer did not inform them, as mentioned by Shaykh al-Islam. And after this mas'ala it mentions: A man comes to a group and says 'dig in this road a well' and does not say 'for me' nor does he hire them for that, and they think that he is the commander. Similarly, if he brings them into a house and says 'dig in it' and they dig thinking it is the commander's house, then it depends on whether he hired them for that.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage collects rulings from three later Hanafi fatwa works: al-Fatawa al-Khalasa, al-Sighnaqi (or al-Sighnaqi, likely a reference to a commentary), and al-Muntaqa. These are authoritative compilations from the muta'akhkhirin (later scholars). They refine the earlier rulings: (1) In al-Fatawa al-Khalasa, the general rule is that the hirer is liable for all damage from building or digging on the road, by istihsan. The exception is for a direct act like a brick falling from the worker's hand, where the worker himself is liable (diyah on his 'aqila and kaffara on him). (2) In al-Sighnaqi, if someone digs a well on the main road and a person deliberately risks himself by jumping over it and dies, the digger is not liable. This aligns with the earlier principle of deliberate risk. (3) In al-Muntaqa, if a person commands workers to dig or build on a road without specifying ownership, and they assume he is the owner, the guarantee is on the commander. However, this is interpreted (ta'wil) as applying only when the road is not well-known as public and the commander did not inform them. If the road is well-known public, the workers should know better, so they would be liable. The report also covers cases where the commander does not say 'for me' or 'I hire you'—the ruling depends on whether there was a hiring contract (ijarah). This shows the importance of contractual intention in determining liability.

Key Terms
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference; a method of departing from strict analogy for a more equitable outcome.
عاقلة ('aqila): The male paternal relatives of a person who bear joint responsibility for paying diyah in cases of accidental killing.
دية (diyah): Financial compensation paid to the heirs of a homicide victim, or for certain bodily injuries.
كفارة (kaffara): Expiation for a sin, often fasting two consecutive months or feeding sixty poor people.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to al-Fatawa al-Khalasa, who bears the diyah if a hired worker drops a brick that kills a passerby?
A) The hirer, because he is always liable.
B) The worker's 'aqila, and the worker must pay kaffara.
C) The worker alone, with no 'aqila involvement.
D) Both the hirer and worker share the diyah equally.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the hirer of road work is liable for damages by istihsan, but the worker's direct accidental act causing death shifts liability to his 'aqila and requires his own kaffara.

No Liability for Digger When Victim Deliberately Risks
Rule from al-Sighnaqi on liability when victim deliberately jumps into a well.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that a person who digs a well on a public road is not liable for the death of someone who deliberately jumps into it, based on the ruling from al-Sighnaqi.

Core Idea
If a victim knowingly takes a risk and dies, the digger is not liable.

This short but significant ruling from al-Sighnaqi addresses a scenario where the victim's own conduct is the direct cause of harm. A person digs a well on the main road. Another person comes along, deliberately risks his life by jumping across the well, falls in, and dies. The ruling: the digger is not liable at all. This aligns with the general principle that one who voluntarily exposes himself to a known danger cannot later claim compensation from the one who created the danger. The ruling reinforces the importance of the victim's consent and assumption of risk in tort liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of the digger
The digger dug a well on the main road (qari'at al-tariq). This act itself is typically dangerous and usually makes the digger liable.
Earlier lessons established that digging a well on a public road creates a strict liability for harm caused.
2
Identify the victim's action
The victim 'risks himself' (khatara bi-nafsihi) and jumps from one side to the other, falling in.
The Arabic 'خاطر بنفسه' implies a conscious, voluntary risk.
3
Determine the legal outcome
The digger does not guarantee anything (lam yadman al-hafiru shay'an). The victim's deliberate act severs the causal link.
The ruling is absolute; no compensation is due, not even partial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Liability of the Commander for Ordered Digging on a Road
Rulings from al-Muntaqa on liability when a person orders digging on a road.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state that the person who commands workers to dig on a public road is liable for damages, but only if the road is not well-known as public or if the commander did not inform them, and distinguish between cases where hiring is explicit.

Core Idea
The commander (amir) is liable for damage from digging he orders on a road, but only if the road's public status is unknown or workers were misled.

This ruling from al-Muntaqa deals with a scenario where a person orders a group to dig or build on a road, but does not specify ownership. The general rule is that the commander (al-amir) is liable for any resulting damage, not the workers (al-fa'il). However, this ruling is qualified by an interpretation (ta'wil) from Shaykh al-Islam: it only applies when the road is not well-known as public property. If the road is clearly public, the workers should know better and may bear some liability themselves. The passage also addresses cases where the commander does not use explicit hiring language, showing that the legal nature of the relationship (ijarah or not) matters. This illustrates the importance of both the commander's intention and the workers' knowledge in determining liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the general rule from al-Muntaqa
The passage begins with a general statement: if a man brings workers to a Muslim road and says 'dig here a well' or 'build here for me', then the guarantee is upon the commander (al-amir), not the doer (al-fa'il). This is stated absolutely.
The Arabic 'ذكر المسألة مطلقاً' indicates the ruling is stated without qualification.
2
Understand the ta'wil (interpretation) that qualifies the rule
Shaykh al-Islam interprets this absolute statement: it only applies when the road is not well-known (mashhur) to the general public as a public road, and the hirer (commander) did not inform the workers about its status.
The ta'wil limits the generality; if the road is obviously public, the workers should know and may be liable.
3
Analyze the second case: ambiguous command without hiring
The passage adds a case where a man says 'dig in this road a well' without saying 'for me' or hiring them. The workers assume he is the owner. Similarly, if he brings them into a house and says 'dig here' and they assume it's his house. The ruling depends on whether he actually hired them (istijar).
The key factor is the existence of an ijarah (hiring) contract. If he hired them, liability on him; if not, the workers act on their own risk.

Liability When Hiring a Mukatab or Restricted Slave to Dig
Ruling on liability and distribution when hiring a mukatab or restricted slave for digging

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to trace the liability and compensation distribution in a Hanafi ruling when a hirer, a mukatab, and a restricted slave die in a work accident.

Core Idea
The hirer is liable for the free man but not for the mukatab; the slave's value is cycled through multiple claims.

This lesson unpacks a complex Hanafi ruling from al-Bahr al-Raiq regarding liability when a hirer employs a mukatab or a restricted slave to dig a well, and the well collapses, killing all three. The ruling involves intricate calculations to ensure fair compensation among the hirer's heirs, the mukatab's heirs, and the slave's master. By mastering this case, you will learn how Hanafi fiqh handles multi-party liability with limited capacity workers.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the fatal accident
The scenario involves a hirer (a free man), a mukatab (a slave working toward freedom), and a restricted slave (mahjur 'alayh). All three die when the well collapses while digging.
The mukatab and restricted slave are hired workers with limited legal capacity.
2
Determine the initial liability
The hirer is liable for the death of the free man (himself) and must pay the value of the slave to the slave's master. He is not liable for the mukatab's death.
The mukatab's death is not compensated by the hirer.
3
Trace the first compensation flow
The master receives the slave's value from the hirer. The master then pays this value to the heirs of the free man and the mukatab. The free man's heirs receive one-third of the blood money (diyah), and the mukatab's heirs receive one-third of the slave's value.
These fractions are based on the values involved.
4
Follow the subsequent recourses
The master can demand the slave's value back from the hirer (so the hirer pays the value again to the master). The hirer then recovers one-third of that from the 'aqila of the free man. Additionally, the mukatab's heirs take one-third of the mukatab's value from the free man's estate.
The 'aqila are the male agnatic relatives responsible for paying blood money.
5
Understand the final split of the mukatab's value
From the mukatab's estate, an amount equal to his value is taken and divided between the heirs of the free man (one-third of his diyah) and the hirer (one-third of the slave's value). This completes the round of compensation.
All fractions ensure proportional distribution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو استأجر مكاتباً أو عبداً محجوراً عليه لحفر بئر فوقعت البئر عليهما وماتا فالضمان على المستأجر في الحر لا في المكاتب ويضمن قيمة العبد لمولاه، فإذا أخذ القيمة دفع المولى القيمة إلى ورثة الحر والمكاتب فيضرب ورثة الحر في قيمته بثلث الدية وورثة المكاتب بثلث قيمة المكاتب ثم يرجع المالك على المستأجر بقيمة العبد مرة فيسلم له وللمستأجر أن يرجع على عاقلة الحر بثلث قيمة العبد ويأخذ أولياء المكاتب من الحر ثلث قيمة المكاتب، ثم يؤخذ من المكاتب مقدار قيمته فيكون بين ورثة الحر والمستأجر يضرب ورثة الحر بثلث ديته والمستأجر بثلث قيمة العبد .
And if he hired a mukatab or a restricted slave to dig a well, and the well collapsed on both of them and they died, then liability is on the hirer regarding the free man (the hirer himself?) but not regarding the mukatab, and he guarantees the value of the slave to his master. Then, when he takes the value, the master pays the value to the heirs of the free man and the mukatab. The heirs of the free man take from his value one-third of the blood money, and the heirs of the mukatab take one-third of the value of the mukatab. Then the owner has recourse against the hirer for the value of the slave once, so it is retained for him. And the hirer has recourse against the 'aqila of the free man for one-third of the value of the slave. And the heirs of the mukatab take from the free man one-third of the value of the mukatab. Then from the mukatab is taken the amount of his value, which is divided between the heirs of the free man and the hirer: the heirs of the free man take one-third of his blood money, and the hirer takes one-third of the value of the slave.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage presents a specific case from the Hanafi school regarding liability when a hirer hires a mukatab (a slave who has contracted to earn his freedom) or a restricted slave (mahjur 'alayh) to dig a well. The well collapses on both the hirer (the 'free man' referred to) and the hired workers, causing death. The ruling states that the hirer is liable for the death of the free man (himself? Actually 'الحر' likely refers to the hirer, who is free) but not for the death of the mukatab. For the slave, he must pay its value to the master. Then a complex distribution follows: the master pays the value received to the heirs of the free man and the mukatab. The heirs of the free man get one-third of the blood money (diyah) based on the free man's value, and the heirs of the mukatab get one-third of the value of the mukatab. Then the owner (the master of the slave) can demand the slave's value back from the hirer. The hirer can then claim one-third of that from the 'aqila of the free man. The mukatab's heirs take one-third of the mukatab's value from the free man's estate. Then from the mukatab's estate, his value is taken and divided between the free man's heirs (one-third of his diyah) and the hirer (one-third of the slave's value). This intricate calculation ensures that all parties are compensated proportionally.

Key Terms
المكاتب (al-mukatab): A slave who has made a contract (kitaba) with his master to earn his freedom by paying installments.
العبد المحجور عليه (al-'abd al-mahjur 'alayh): A slave who is legally restricted from dealing, like a minor slave.
الدية (al-diyah): Blood money payable for wrongful death or injury.
العاقلة (al-'aqila): The group of relatives (asaba) responsible for paying blood money on behalf of a perpetrator.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the case where a hirer hires a mukatab and a restricted slave to dig, and the well collapses killing all three, who is NOT liable for the mukatab's death?
A) The hirer
B) The mukatab himself
C) The master of the slave
D) The 'aqila of the free man

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, a hirer is not liable for the death of a mukatab he hires, but if a restricted slave is hired, the hirer must pay his value to the master, leading to a multi-step compensation distribution.

Liability for Carrying Objects in the Road: The Exception for Worn Garments
Ruling with proof and exception for worn cloak

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the general rule of liability for carrying objects in the road, identify the exception for a worn cloak, and apply the conditions that trigger liability or exemption.

Core Idea
Liability for falling objects is the rule; a worn cloak is exempt due to widespread necessity.

This lesson unpacks a key rule in Hanafi tort law: if you carry something in the road and it falls on someone, you are liable. But there is an important exception for ordinary clothing like a cloak (rida') that you are wearing. The reasoning involves a condition of safety and the principle of 'umum al-balwa (widespread necessity). We will also see a nuance from Imam Muhammad about excessive or unusual garments.

Guided Path
1
Identify the General Rule
Find the rule: whoever carries something in the road and it falls on a person is liable, whether injury is from the fall or stumbling afterwards.
The ruling covers all carried objects unless exempted.
2
Understand the Reasoning (Talil)
The act of carrying goods is permissible (mubah) but conditioned on safety (taqyid bi-shart al-salama). This is analogous to shooting at a target or hunting – one is liable if harm occurs.
The condition is implicit: you must ensure safety.
3
Locate the Exception
If the object was a cloak (rida') that the person was wearing, there is no liability. The text explains the difference: the carrier intends to preserve the item so safety is required; the wearer intends to preserve his garment, but the act of wearing is absolutely permissible without the condition due to widespread need.
The exemption is specific to items customarily worn.
4
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Ikhtilaf
Muhammad adds: if one wears excessive or unusual items (e.g., felt, sackcloth, armor in peacetime), liability returns because there is no necessity (darurah). The exemption is based on 'umum al-balwa.
This limits the exception to normal clothing.
5
Apply the Principle
Now you can test scenarios: carrying a box vs. wearing a regular coat vs. wearing medieval armor in a modern street. Use the illa (why) to determine liability.
The key is whether the item is subject to widespread need.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال رحمه الله: (ومن حمل شيئاً في الطريق فسقط على إنسان ضمن) سواء تلف بالوقوع أو بالعثرة به بعد الوقوع لأن حمل المتاع في الطريق على رأسه أو على ظهره مباح له لكنه مقيد بشرط السلامة بمنزلة الرمي إلى الهدف أو الصيد . قال رحمه الله : (فلو كان رداء قد لبسه فسقط لا) أي لو كان المحمول رداء قد لبسه فسقط على إنسان فعطب به لا يضمن، والفرق بينه وبين الشيء المحمول أن الحامل يقصد حفظه فلا يخرج بالتقييد بوصف الصلاة واللابس يقصد حفظ ما يلبسه فيخرج بالتقييد بوصف السلامة فجعل في حقه مباحاً مطلقاً. وعن محمد : إذا لبس زيادة على قدر الحاجة وما لا يلبس عادة كاللبد والجوالق والدرع من الحديد في غير الحرب ضمن لأنه لا ضرورة إلى لبسه وسقوط الضمان باعتبارهما العموم البلوى.
He (the author) said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'Whoever carries something in the road and it falls on a person is liable' – whether the person dies from the fall or from stumbling over it after the fall, because carrying goods in the road on one's head or back is permissible but conditioned on safety, like shooting at a target or hunting. He said, may Allah have mercy on him: 'If it was a cloak that he was wearing and it fell, then no' – i.e., if the carried object was a cloak that he was wearing and it fell on a person and he was injured, he is not liable. The difference between it and a carried object is that the carrier intends to preserve (the object), so it is not exempt from the condition of safety, while the wearer intends to preserve what he is wearing, so it is exempt from the condition of safety, and it is considered absolutely permissible for him. And from Muhammad: If he wears more than needed and what is not usually worn, like felt, sackcloth, or an iron armor in non-war situations, he is liable because there is no necessity to wear it, and the dropping of liability is based on widespread need (umum al-balwa).
Source pages p. 119

Commentary
This passage from the sharh of Kanz al-Daqaiq explains the ruling of the matn regarding carrying objects in the road. The general rule is that if someone carries something in the road and it falls and kills or injures a person, the carrier is liable. This applies whether death occurs directly from the fall or from stumbling on the object after it fell. The reason is that carrying goods in the road is permissible (mubah) but conditioned upon safety; analogous to shooting arrows at a target or hunting, where one is responsible for mishits. However, there is an exception: if the object was a cloak (rida') that the person was wearing and it fell, there is no liability. The distinction is that the carrier of goods intends to preserve the item (so he must take care), while the wearer of a cloak intends to preserve what he is wearing, but the act of wearing a cloak is considered absolutely permissible without the condition of safety because of its ubiquity and necessity. The passage also cites a narration from Imam Muhammad: if a person wears extra clothing beyond normal need or unusual items like felt, sackcloth, or armor in peacetime, then he is liable if it falls and causes harm because there is no necessity (darura) for such wearing. The general principle is that the exemption from liability is based on 'umum al-balwa (widespread affliction/necessity), meaning that for common items, strict liability would cause hardship.

Key Terms
الرداء (al-rida'): A cloak or upper garment worn over the shoulders.
العموم البلوى (al-'umum al-balwa): Widespread occurrence or common affliction; a principle used to ease liability for frequent acts.
التقييد بشرط السلامة (al-taqyid bi-shart al-salama): Conditioning the permissibility of an act on ensuring safety; if one fails, liability arises.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why is a person not liable if his worn cloak (rida') falls on a pedestrian?
A) Because the cloak belongs to someone else.
B) Because wearing a cloak is considered absolutely permissible without the condition of safety due to widespread need.
C) Because the pedestrian was negligent.
D) Because the cloak is light in weight.

Key Takeaway
Carrying objects in the road is permissible but conditioned on safety; wearing common garments is an absolute exception due to widespread necessity, while excessive clothing revives liability.
